## Page 14

Introduction




Giovanni Pico della Mirandola (1463‚Äì94) has been a beacon of progress in
modern times, and the celebrated Oration on the Dignity of Man has been the
engine of his fame. But he never wrote a speech about the dignity of man, as
I showed in my recent book about Pico della Mirandola and his Oration in
Modern Memory. The prince‚Äôs speech announced quite different projects: per-
suading Christians to become Kabbalists in order to annihilate themselves in God;
and convincing philosophers that their path to saving wisdom was concord rather
than disputation.¬π
  This book about Heresy, Freedom, and Philosophy shows that Pico‚Äôs work,
before and after he wrote the Oration, was in no way progressive‚Äîor ‚Äòhumanist.‚Äô
My main topic is Pico‚Äôs Apology, his self-defense against heresy charges: this
public polemic reveals more about him than the famous speech that he never
gave and that deliberately kept his ideas secret. The orator‚Äôs method in the Oration
was esoteric, but the defendant in the Apology made his case openly in a voice
that was academic and belligerent, not oracular or poetic. Perhaps because of
its size and presentation, the Apology has been read less than Pico‚Äôs other writings:
as far as I know, the last book devoted entirely to it appeared more than Ô¨Åve
centuries ago.¬≤
  Because Pico‚Äôs speech was misread for so long, he came to be seen as a herald of
modernity and a prophet of Enlightenment. This book conÔ¨Årms that he was no
such thing. Pico wrote the Apology, his second book, to defend his Ô¨Årst book, the
outrageously provocative 900 Conclusions that he published a few months earlier.
This collection of theses for academic debate caused an uproar. Pope Innocent
VIII condemned thirteen of the prince‚Äôs propositions within weeks after the
Conclusions appeared. To answer the Pope‚Äôs indictment point by point, Pico
divided his Apology into thirteen parts, called ‚ÄúQuestions.‚Äù My book discusses
these Questions, labeled Q1, Q2, Q3 and so on, while focusing on six of them.

titles mention the Apology. Many students of Pico have discussed the Apology in telling his story: see
especially, besides Fornaciari‚Äôs ‚ÄúIntroduction,‚Äù Dorez and Thuasne (1897); Dulles (1941); Monnerjahn
(1960); Di Napoli (1965); Lubac (1974); Craven (1981); Reinhardt (1989); Euler (1998); Valcke (2005);

2  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

  Chapter 1: To put Pico‚Äôs Questions in context, my Ô¨Årst chapter introduces
the Apology as a whole‚Äîa record of conÔ¨Çict between Pico and his papal judges
about basic points of Christian doctrine, like God‚Äôs incarnation as a human, the
descent of Jesus to Hell, Christ‚Äôs eucharistic body, and constraints on belief about
such teachings. The six Questions studied here show better than the other seven
how the prince confronted his prosecutors on these fundamentals of his faith
and theirs.
   After Jesus was cruciÔ¨Åed but before he rose from the dead, he descended to Hell
in order to release good people imprisoned there temporarily: this ‚ÄòHarrowing of
Hell‚Äô was a primary but perplexing article of the Christian creed. When Jesus went
down to Hell, his body was still buried. But Pico and his adversaries in court, like
all Christians, thought of the Hell that Jesus visited as a place‚Äîor a place
containing many places, if Dante was right. How could Christ, without a body,
be in a place, any place at all?
  The person who took this trip to the underworld was God‚Äîa pure spirit,
eternal and changeless. But this God had taken on a human body, as Jesus, in
order to save other embodied humans. Yet Pico knew that human bodies were
material and imperfect, that they changed, suffered, and died. Then how could a
changeless God be both perfect and embodied? To understand the dogma of
incarnation‚Äîthe embodiment of God‚Äîin a philosophical way, the inquiring
prince needed a theory of divine embodiment. But the version that he proposed
offended the authorities, and they ruled that his ideas were heretical.
  Chapter 2: To express his theories and defend them, Pico used techniques and
terminology of scholastic theology, as taught by Thomas Aquinas, John Duns
Scotus, and William of Ockham but also by Jean Cabrol, Pierre de la Palud, Jean
Quidort, and other lesser Ô¨Ågures. To document his Apology, Pico relied on three
dozen medieval authorities in all‚Äîboth obscure and eminent. Their philosophical
vocabulary was specialized, even arcane, but Pico had to master it, and he did. My
second chapter shows the complexity and depth of what he learned.
  An indispensable part of the scholastic language that Pico acquired‚Äîwith life
or death consequences for him‚Äîwas the medieval terminology of ‚Äòsuppo- words.‚Äô
He inherited these technical terms from a tradition ten centuries old, and vener-
able age was part of their value for him. The suppo- words tracked in my second
chapter were Pico‚Äôs tools throughout the Apology: this intricate terminology is a
portal to his way of doing philosophy. Doorways to the prince‚Äôs defense against
heresy charges have been shut for Ô¨Åve centuries: Pico della Mirandola on Trial
opens them.
  Chapter 3: My third chapter analyzes Pico‚Äôs account of Christian teaching on
the  incarnation. When he  published  a  thesis about  this  doctrine  in  the
Conclusions, the Pope swiftly attacked the proposition along with twelve others.
To make the offending statement in the Conclusions and to restate  it in the
Apology, Pico used an odd word not found in today‚Äôs dictionaries of classical

## Page 16

Ôù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤ÔùØÔù§ÔùµÔù£Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ  3

Latin, the verb suppositare. This unusual verb and  its cousins‚Äîother suppo-
words‚Äîoccur two dozen times just in this one Question on the incarnation.
  As a matter of Christian orthodoxy, who was right about the sacred dogma of
incarnation: the prince or his papal opponents? Answers to this threatening
question turned on suppo- words. The intemperate author of the Apology revered
a Church that executed people like himself, before and after his heresy trial,
because the Church‚Äôs agents condemned wrong answers to such questions as
grievous sins, not just misunderstandings‚Äîas insults to God that deserved capital
punishment. Mistakes about terminology could be fatal.
  Chapter 4: When Pico, a pious Christian, went to church and prayed at Mass,
he believed that Christ came in his body to that very place, where he and others
could see God there in front of them on an altar. All Christians thought that Christ
came every day‚Äîbody and blood, soul and divinity‚Äîto altars in churches every-
where in Christendom. Yet no one ever actually saw the body or the blood. In all
those places, the only sacramental sights were bread and wine blessed by a priest:
how could that be? Pico took such questions up in three of his condemned theses,
discussed in my fourth chapter.
  Chapter 5: My Ô¨Åfth chapter moves from the metaphysics of divine embodiment
and the semantics of suppo- words to the ethics of belief‚Äîto the moral environ-
ment of Pico‚Äôs views about articles of faith, like the eucharist and incarnation, and
of denunciations of his views by opponents. The papal agents who condemned the
prince thought that some beliefs, as articles of faith, were necessary for salvation.
Pico agreed‚Äîin principle. But as a practical matter, he had to defend himself in a
heresy trial. By the standards of his time and place‚ÄîItaly in the late Ô¨Åfteenth
century‚Äîwho made the better case about faith and belief: Pico or his assailants?
My Ô¨Åfth chapter takes this question up.
  Conclusions: Since the middle of the last century, textbooks written for college
students have promoted only one Pico, purportedly a hero of progressive human-
ism. But his stance in the Conclusions and Apology, which were products of late
medieval culture, was in no way progressive. The familiar, forward-looking Pico
did not write the Apology. The grim scene of his book, his report on a battle for life
and honor, was the proximate medieval past where human history was despised as
the annals of sin. To understand Pico‚Äôs universe of dismal expectations, our best
guide is his Apology. But the author did not choose his book‚Äôs topics, and its
theories were not his own. He learned them from medieval teachers.
  During his lifetime, Pico allowed only three of his books to be printed‚Äîthe
Apology, Conclusions, and Heptaplus‚Äîand none was a humanist project. Now a
false icon of humanism, he wrote little in the humanist style and circulated none of
it widely. His thoroughly medieval works may seem strange to modern readers
who come to them for the Ô¨Årst time. They‚Äôre utterly unlike writings by Poggio
Bracciolini, Agnolo Poliziano, Lorenzo Valla, and others for whom the word

## Page 17

4  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

‚Äòhumanist‚Äô might be a better Ô¨Åt. Pico‚Äôs Conclusions and Apology are products of the
late Middle Ages.
  Studying Pico as the author of these books and linking them with their sources
relocates the author in the history of European thought: his thinking in these
works was medieval and scholastic, and so was his self-presentation‚Äînot modern
or even pre-modern.
   Procedures: Pico  spent  years  studying philosophy and  called  himself  a
philosopher‚Äîoften and insistently. He was right: he was a philosopher in the
same way that Aquinas, Scotus, and Ockham were philosophers. But in the
Anglophone world, where analytic philosophy is the standard, philosophers now
pay little attention to him: most assume that his way of doing philosophy is alien
to them and useless. As a result, Pico has rarely been studied in a way that
Anglophone philosophers today would recognize as philosophical.
  My book aims to bring Pico closer to current philosophical conversations by
clarifying his thought with tools of analytic philosophy‚Äîseldom applied on the
scale of this book to a Renaissance text. Realizing that the ways of philosophers
may annoy or bafÔ¨Çe some historians, and vice versa, I‚Äôve done this in order to
make my investigations recognizable as philosophy to philosophers without
making them inscrutable to historians. For the theory that supports this project,
see my article in the British Journal for the History of Philosophy.¬≥ In broader
terms, Pico della Mirandola on Trial is a book for students and teachers of history,
languages, law, literature, philosophy, religion, rhetoric, and theology, also medi-
eval and renaissance studies. Pico fans in their legions might like it too, I hope.
  Nonetheless (in the interest of full disclosure), because Pico‚Äôs Apology is a work
of philosophy, because Western philosophy since Descartes has taken paths
unknown to Pico, and because my own outlook is post-Cartesian, I Ô¨Ånd the
medieval Apology hard to read. The circumstances of the book‚Äôs production,
especially the author‚Äôs haste under pressure, aggravated other difÔ¨Åculties, which
start with language no longer familiar to readers like me.
   If a philosopher today mentions Dasein when discussing Heidegger or refers to
Bedeutung when talking about Frege, she can assume that other philosophers will
understand. Likewise, when Pico used suppo- words like suppositum and suppo-
sitare again and again in the Apology, he knew that other philosophers would
understand. But suppositum and its cousins are now obscure: even experts on pre-
Cartesian thought are not always sure that they, not to speak of their readers,
understand this terminology.
  For this reason, I‚Äôve paid close attention to the Latin of the Apology‚Äôs Questions
and the Conclusions‚Äîwhich is in no way ‚Äòhumanist‚Äô Latin. When English words
in texts that I‚Äôve translated correspond ambiguously to the Latin possibilities, I‚Äôve




   ¬≥ Copenhaver (2020b).

Ôù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤ÔùØÔù§ÔùµÔù£Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ  5

supplied the Latin in parentheses. I‚Äôve also provided full English translations for
four of Pico‚Äôs Questions (Q4, 8, 9, and 10) at the ends of Chapters 3, 4 and 5. Pico‚Äôs
arguments, in the manner of scholastic philosophy, are often dense and almost
never supported by formalizations like ‚ÄòÔù°is Ôù¢‚Äô or ‚Äòif p, then q.‚Äô To clarify Pico‚Äôs
reasoning and to avoid ambiguity and repetition, I‚Äôve used such devices to
supplement words and phrases represented by them. For example: double quota-
tion marks always indicate direct quotations from sources cited, while single
quotation marks mainly distinguish using a word, like ‚Äòfrog‚Äô in ‚Äòa frog has four
legs,‚Äô from mentioning a word, as in ‚Äòfrog has four letters.‚Äô I‚Äôve also added lists and
tables to summarize formalizations used over long stretches of text.
   I became interested in Pico‚Äôs heresy trial while working on an edition and
translation of the Apology for the I Tatti Renaissance Library (ITRL). An ITRL
edition and translation of the Oration and of the Life of Pico by his nephew will
appear in 2022. Over the years, several ITRL projects contributed to my Magic and
the Dignity of Man (2019), whose topic is Pico‚Äôs Oration. This book, which studies
the Apology, is entirely separate in concept and content: agreement with my Pico
book of 2019 is thorough, but there is no overlap. This new book is entirely
different.‚Å¥
   I Ô¨Årst encountered Pico‚Äôs kind of philosophy at Loyola College (as it was then)
in Baltimore in 1960‚Äîbefore the Second Vatican Council turned Roman Catholic
education upside down. When students of my age enrolled in psychology courses
at other colleges, they read Freud and B. F. Skinner while I was memorizing
Aquinas on phantasms at Loyola. My Jesuit teachers delivered a curriculum still
shaped by a Plan of Studies written in the sixteenth century to promote Thomist
scholasticism. When  I  left Loyola in 1964,  I kept reading philosophy while
exploring the history of Pico‚Äôs world. But four decades passed before the next
time I saw philosophy historicized as consequentially as it had been at Loyola‚Äî
where the main motives of my teachers were religious, not academic.
   Until 2003, when  I moved from UCLA‚Äôs bureaucracy to  its Philosophy
Department, my philosophical education continued, as hostility to history grad-
ually decreased among Anglophone philosophers. The days of ‚Äújust say no to
history‚Äù as a philosopher‚Äôs slogan were waning. The tide had been high at the
Ô¨Çood, however, and I seldom saw the waters bridged‚Äîfrom either side‚Äîfor forty
years after I left Loyola. My history friends and my philosophy friends usually
talked past one another, when they talked at all. Starting almost twenty years ago,

Calvin Normore changed all that for me‚Äîchanged it utterly. Our seminars at

  To stimulate such conversations is an aim of this book.
  Only a book‚Äôs readers can judge whether it succeeds‚Äîwhether it works. For my
readers and judges, I have a request, then a suggestion. First, please be kinder than
Pico‚Äôs judges were to him. Next, before starting on the Ô¨Årst chapter, read one of
Pico‚Äôs Questions translated at the end of the third, fourth, and Ô¨Åfth chapters:
they‚Äôre quite short. Then go back and read the whole book before trying the same
Question again. If you understand the Question better on a second try, the book
will have done its job‚ÄîI think.
  Generous, learned, and patient friends have read all or parts of this book, have
discussed it with me and have improved it greatly: my thanks to Michael Allen,

## Page 20

1
       Humanism Goes to Hell



                  1.1 Pico‚Äôs Scholastic Ambitions

During his short life‚Äîoutside Tuscany and the lands of his cousins, the Este
dukes‚ÄîGiovanni Pico della Mirandola was known best by his writings, not his
captivating person. He was most accessible in the three books that he chose to
circulate in print: Ô¨Årst the 900 Conclusions in 1486, then the Apology in 1487, and
Ô¨Ånally the Heptaplus in 1489. Nothing else that he wrote was publicized in this
way while he lived. And the author of these books did not write like a humanist‚Äî
like Poggio Bracciolini, Agnolo Poliziano, or Lorenzo Valla‚Äîor like himself when
he wrote the Oration.¬π
  The language of the 900 Conclusions, as Pico described them, was ‚Äúnot the
splendid Roman tongue but the way the most famous disputants talk in Paris,
since this is the custom for almost all philosophers of our day.‚Äù Italian contem-
poraries of the prince like Antonio Cittadini, Pietro Pomponazzi, and Nicoletta
Vernia were such ‚Äúphilosophers of our day‚Äù‚Äîcalled ‚Äòscholastics‚Äô now. Their Latin
had evolved earlier, however, in medieval schools and universities, where Peter
Lombard in the twelfth Christian century, not Cicero in pagan Rome, set the
template for communication. But in Pico‚Äôs day some theologians spoke the
scholastic dialect in the Holy City as papal dependents, employees, and courtiers.
When Innocent VIII launched an investigation of the Conclusions, just weeks after
the book was printed, clerics schooled in this way scrutinized it in Rome as the
Pope‚Äôs agents.¬≤
  The investigators on the Pope‚Äôs commission, which he created in February of
1487, were papal agents in two senses. OfÔ¨Åcially, they were Innocent‚Äôs appointees
acting on his behalf in a juridical proceeding: in Pico‚Äôs words, they were ‚Äúdeputed
by his Holiness to hear and decide on my conclusions.‚Äù Practically and politically,
they were the Pontiff ‚Äôs tools‚Äînot neutral judges but prosecutors pursuing a guilty

  The Pope indicted Pico‚Äôs theses in February, and his servants brought their
verdicts in before the third week of March‚Äîlightning-fast by Vatican standards.
Innocent appointed sixteen commissioners, twelve of whom attended the Ô¨Årst

Garcia, a Thomist whose reputation rests on the book that he published in 1489 to
rebut Pico‚Äôs Apology. Bishop Garcia was an expert on theology, and the trial
documents list three other commissioners as theologians, one as a lawyer, and
another four as ofÔ¨Åcials of the papal court. The panel included Dominicans like
Garcia, but also Augustinians, Franciscans, and Servites‚Äîmaking a show of
balance among the great religious orders.
   Innocent‚Äôs mind was made up, however, and his charge to his people was angry:
he savaged Pico‚Äôs theses as confused, deviant, intolerable, irregular, obscure,
scandalous, and reeking of heresy. His stormy indictment armed the commission-

terminology‚Äîex vi verborum, ‚Äòfrom the meaning of the words‚Äô‚Äîthat framed
what they eventually decided: they repeated this phrase in four of their verdicts.
The same trial record shows them not tarrying for lengthy deliberations: ofÔ¨Åcially
every vote was registered as unanimous and unqualiÔ¨Åed. Documented responses
by the judges to Pico‚Äôs testimony followed a pattern: after hearing the defendant,
they ignored what he said and found him guilty as charged.¬≥
  Pico attended some of the trial to explain his theses and answer questions.

Throughout the Apology, though sometimes with less heat, Pico attacked his papal
attackers by insulting them and the Pope‚Äîsurely not a smart strategy. His antics
can be chalked up to an immature aristocrat‚Äôs arrogance. But why were the
commissioners at least as combative as he was? The record shows that hostility
to the prince was their mission from the start.‚Åµ
  Nothing in the Ô¨Årst few pages of the Conclusions should have worried them:
there they found ‚ÄúLatin philosophers and theologians‚Äù‚Äîas Pico wrote‚Äîincluding
Albert the Great, Thomas Aquinas, and John Duns Scotus. Sages in other parts of
the book‚ÄîHermes Trismegistus, Maimonides, Zoroaster‚Äîmay have been less
familiar, but statements collected under exotic names also followed the form
advertised by Pico: ‚Äúthe way the most famous disputants talk in Paris.‚Äù Since all
900 theses were meant, in principle, for disputation in this scholastic way, they
were telegraphic, careless about grammar, and not at all eloquent. Accordingly,
Roman investigators of the Conclusions who had read this Ô¨Årst book by Pico
would not have expected to see Ô¨Åne Latin in the opening pages of his second
printed work: they knew that the Apology was purpose-made (and hastily) to
defend inelegant propositions that they had already branded as criminal.‚Å∂
  Nonetheless, the prince apparently thought he could hypnotize the Pope‚Äôs high-
ranking functionaries with recycled oratory: the introduction he sent them was
part of the Oration written (but never delivered) to introduce the Conclusions.
These few pages were less recherch√© and more straightforward than the rest of the
speech‚Äîbut still high and contrived rhetorical art. As the author summed up this
imprudent display of his talent‚Äîstunning though  it was‚Äîhis judgment went
from bad to worse. ‚ÄúI must change my way of speaking,‚Äù he hissed: ‚ÄúI‚Äôm talking
to barbarians, and as the proverb neatly puts it, stammerers understand only those
who stammer.‚Äù Accordingly, what the papal barbarians saw on most pages of the
Apology looked exactly like their own scholastic theology‚Äîbecause it was scho-
lastic theology.‚Å∑

  The beginning of Question 6 (Q6) of the Apology, for example, introduced

    If one takes the usual approach (communis via) to the possibility of suppositing
   (suppositatio) with regard to any creature whatever, I say that, without the
   substance (substantia) of bread changing (conversio) into Christ‚Äôs body or with-
   out eliminating (annihilatio) the breadness (paneitas),  it can happen that
   Christ‚Äôs body is on the altar in keeping with sacramental reality (veritas sacra-
   menti) because the statement (dictum) would be made to speak about something
   possible (de possibili), not about being so (de sic esse). With this conclusion I do
  not mean to say that bread is not changed into Christ‚Äôs body; that Christ‚Äôs body is
   factually (de facto) in the sacrament factually established by Christ; nor that the
   sacrament‚Äôs actual (actualis) reality is Christ‚Äôs body being present (praesentiali-
   tas) to bread factually taken on (assumptum) or supposited (suppositum) by the
  Word (a Verbo). In fact, I explicitly reject these inherence (de inesse) claims at the
  end of the conclusion. Because the statement (dictum) there is about possibility
   (de possibili), not about being so (de sic esse), I want the ‚Äòpossible‚Äô (ly possibile) to
  be referred not only to the previous statement (dictum) about the suppositing of
   the bread (suppositatio panis) by Christ but also to the ‚Äòsacramental reality
   (veritas sacramenti) of the Eucharist‚Äô‚Äîto the sacrament itself and even to the
   presence of Christ‚Äôs body together with the bread that has been taken on
   (assumptus).

leading expert on Pico‚Äôs style has assigned the boldface words to a ‚Äúlexicon in
which the Latin of the Conclusions and Apology is distinct from that of Pico‚Äôs other
works, revealing its ‚Äòmedieval‚Äô and ‚ÄòParisian‚Äô nature.‚Äù‚Å∏

  The rhetorical situations of the Conclusions and Apology were different, to be

most discussed of Pico‚Äôs 900 propositions, was utterly unintelligible to his judges
in Rome and completely detached from the  scholastic philosophizing  that

broadest sense, all the theses were disputational in intent, and the pattern of all

summas. The form of Q5 reÔ¨Çects its own exotic content, however, while Q7‚Äôs
format is more like a polemic by Eusebius than a summa by Aquinas, and the
content of Q7 is philological. If the whole Apology were like Q7, scholars would
now see Pico‚Äôs book as foreshadowing the achievements of Erasmus in patristic
philology‚Äîa humanist undertaking motivated, in part, by historical conscious-
ness and realized by historical methods. But Q7 and Q5 are not like the rest of the
Apology, where the other eleven Questions are scholastic through and through.¬π‚Å∂


                        1.2 Bread and Wine

Eucharistic theology, the topic of three condemned theses, had been a Ô¨Çashpoint
since the eleventh century when a Church Council forced Berengar of Tours, a
troublesome priest, to recant his views on the sacrament. His retraction, while
afÔ¨Årming that Christ‚Äôs body and blood were really present on the altar with the
consecrated bread and wine, also declared that the divine body, still looking and
tasting like bread, was ‚Äúheld and broken in the hands of priests and crushed by the
teeth of the faithful.‚Äù These grotesque words soon found their way into canon law,
where they remain today to stimulate theological ingenuity and immortalize
Berengar, named seven times by Pico in Q6.¬π‚Å∑ Using Berengar‚Äôs scandalous
‚Äòconfession‚Äô to explicate the ‚Äúsustaining of breadness‚Äù in the sacrament, the prince
also noted that ‚Äúas to breadness, the body is crushed by teeth, while remaining
whole  as  to humanness.‚Äù  Ritual cannibalism was  the  alternative  to  these



   ¬π‚Å∂ For an annotated translation of Q7 on Origen see Crouzel (1977); also Terracciano (2012) and

implausible abstractions, which is why the author of the Apology‚Äîjoining the

  When he was putting the Conclusions together, however, nothing forced the
prince to taunt the authorities about the eucharist. He goaded them more than
once, not just in the Q6 thesis but also in Q9 and Q10: this last statement claimed
that ‚Äúthe words this is my body said in the consecration are taken materially, not as
signifying.‚Äù The crux was the phrase, hoc est enim corpus meum, spoken by priests
in order to change bread into Christ‚Äôs body at the holiest moment of the mass. The
demonstrative hoc was a puzzle: when a priest says ‚Äòthis,‚Äô what is he talking about?
To what does hoc refer? Not the bread, which is not Christ‚Äôs body. And not Christ‚Äôs
body, which is not present until after the priest has uttered the sentence that starts
with hoc. Pico was alert to these questions and others that theologians had asked
about the eucharist.¬π‚Åπ
  Although hocus-pocus‚Äîa resonant expression‚Äîapparently did not originate
from Protestant derision of the Catholic eucharist, some have assumed so. Pico
put fuel in this furnace with his response to the hoc conundrum. When he
interpreted the words of consecration ‚Äúmaterially, not as signifying,‚Äù he appealed
to an invention of medieval logicians that had peaked by 1350 in works by Jean
Buridan, William of Ockham and others. Their theory of logic and semantics,
called ‚Äòsupposition,‚Äô was elaborate yet effective and has earned the respect of
modern experts. The details are unimportant for present purposes, and up-to-
date accounts are available; it‚Äôs enough to know that the Q10 thesis speciÔ¨Åes one
type of supposition, called ‚Äòmaterial,‚Äô which applies when a word in a statement
(more precisely, a term in a proposition) refers not to a thing but to itself: to a
word, that is to say‚Äîlike ‚Äòfrog‚Äô in ‚Äòfrog has four letters.‚Äô¬≤‚Å∞
   If you look ‚Äòfrog‚Äô up in a dictionary, you‚Äôll Ô¨Ånd that this word‚Äîin isolation,
apart from other words‚Äîusually signiÔ¨Åes a member of the genus Rana. A major
achievement of supposition theory was to distinguish how words signify in this
way from how they stand for various items when they are used, along with other
words, in propositions; a word supposits or has supposition only in propositional
context‚Äîlike hoc in hoc est enim corpus meum. If the words of consecration refer to
words, not to bread or a body, their supposition (unhelpfully) is called material.¬≤¬π
To support this analysis of the eucharistic hoc, Pico‚Äôs Q10 cited the Ordinary
Gloss on Gratian‚Äôs Decretum, an ofÔ¨Åcial statement of Church law: ‚Äúthe Lord used

this word in a signifying way (signiÔ¨Åcative),‚Äù according to a glossator, ‚Äúand we use

  Although Pico had logic and law on his side, the Pope‚Äôs agents ruled that the
thesis defended by Q10 was ‚Äúscandalous and contrary to the usual view (communis
opinio).‚Äù They also called the Q9 thesis ‚Äúerroneous‚Äù: Pico proposed there that
‚Äúsomeone who says that an accident cannot exist unless it exists-in (inexistat) will
be able to hold to the sacrament of the Eucharist even while holding that the
substance of bread does not remain.‚Äù Pico complained that he was given no reason
for this Ô¨Ånding of error, but one objection starts with elementary metaphysics.¬≤¬≥
   Eucharistic dogma required that the substance of bread could not remain after
consecration because by then the bread had changed into Christ‚Äôs body, a com-
ponent of a different (body/soul) substance. Thus far the Q9 thesis was orthodox,
agreeing ‚Äúthat the substance of bread does not remain.‚Äù But Pico insisted that the
dogma could be honored while also holding that ‚Äúan accident cannot exist unless
it exists-in.‚Äù The color, size, and weight of bread to be consecrated are accidents of
the bread: darken the color by toasting the bread or reduce the size and weight by
breaking a piece off, and the bread substance will  still be there, though with
different accidents or features or qualities.¬≤‚Å¥
  This substance/accident distinction was basic metaphysics: an accident (the
bread‚Äôs color) differs from a substance (the bread) because a substance, in order to
be what it is‚Äîbread, in this case‚Äîneeds no particular accident, whereas all the
bread‚Äôs actual accidents, in order to be there at all, need the bread. Exactly why and
how accidents need substances requires further inquiry, and the answer assumed
by the Q9 thesis‚Äîthat accidents need substances in order to exist-in them‚Äîwas
compatible with conventional metaphysics. But anyone who bothered to propose
such a thesis‚ÄîPico, for example‚Äîwould realize that the questioning could go on
and on: might accidents exist in some way other than existing-in? After  all,
substances  exist without  existing-in, so  the  possibility  of  existing without
existing-in was given.
  Pico accepted that after bread on the altar had become Christ‚Äôs body, the bready
substance was no longer present. He and everyone else knew, nonetheless, that
bready accidents could still be seen, smelled, and tasted. But how could such
accidents survive without the bready substance that was no longer on hand? The
Q9 thesis made this question harder by suggesting that existing-in might be the

unknown to Aristotle, in short chapters that came to be known as ‚Äòlittle logicals.‚Äô

restrictions, appellations and what not.‚Äù This apparatus that More and other
humanists assailed was Pico‚Äôs main line of defense for the eucharistic propositions

                       1.3 Learned Heretics

Before, during and after Pico‚Äôs lifetime, the eucharist was a dangerous dogma:
some who got it wrong were punished severely. John Wyclif, who taught logic,
philosophy and theology at Oxford, offered a public challenge to the ofÔ¨Åcial line in
1381‚Äîthe year of the Peasants‚Äô Revolt in England. After Richard II put the
rebellion down, the King married Anne of Bohemia in 1382, and Wyclif‚Äôs ideas
drifted east to the Queen‚Äôs homeland. In 1415, a Church Council at Constance
called these teachings heretical. The Council also snared Jan Hus, a Bohemian
theologian who taught at the Charles University in Prague. Hus was no agitator,
but the prelates at Constance sent him to the stake (Figures 5.1 and C.2).¬≤‚Åπ
   Religious war then raged in central Europe for twenty more years, and a passage
from John‚Äôs Gospel became a battle-cry of Utraquism, a ‚ÄòHussite‚Äô heresy in Rome‚Äôs
eyes: ‚Äúwhoever eats my Ô¨Çesh and drinks my blood has eternal life,‚Äù Jesus said: ‚Äúhim
will I raise up at the last day.‚Äù Accordingly, some Christians insisted that they
needed both the Ô¨Çesh and the blood‚Äîthe sacrament in utraque specie, under two
appearances, bread and wine. Luther, Zwingli, and others were still litigating the
eucharistic Ô¨Åne print a century later; when Pico chose to dispute about the
sacrament in 1486, he picked an issue sure to draw Ô¨Åre.¬≥‚Å∞
  Utraquism came too late for one connoisseur of heresies, however‚ÄîGuido
Terrena of Perpignan, who died in 1342, years before Hus was born. Guido was
a Carmelite theologian, the head of his order and a bishop; his best-known work
was a Summa on Heresies and Refutations of Them. This handbook classiÔ¨Åed
scores of heresies by name, from Arians, Beguines, and Cathars to Sethians,
Theodosians, and Waldensians. Even the obscure Tesseradecatites had a label:
they went wrong by Judaizing‚Äîinsisting on a Jewish metric of forty days to
compute the date of Easter, a high holy day for Christians. In addition, Guido
accounted for ‚Äúheresies without names‚Äù and awarded fourth place to nameless
transgressors who denied that ‚ÄúChrist descended to Hell for the purpose of
liberating humans.‚Äù To prove the contrary, Guido cited seven verses of scripture
which conÔ¨Årmed that ‚ÄúChrist‚Äôs soul descended into Hell to lead prisoners out of
the darkness and shadows of death and redeem them by the merit of his own
suffering.‚Äù¬≥¬π
  Pico consulted Guido about heresies. In fact, he took several paragraphs of Q1
verbatim from Guido‚Äôs Summa, though he named the author only once in the
Apology. The heresy in the Q1 thesis attacked by the prince‚Äôs inquisitors was about
the Harrowing of Hell, basically as authenticated by Guido: when Jesus descended

to the see of Chichester. Formal proceedings began in 1457. There was plenty to
talk about since nine of the Bishop‚Äôs books were in evidence, and there was no
serious rebuttal of the concocted charges. Within months his books were burned,
he recanted his purported errors, and his hold on ofÔ¨Åce slipped away. His writings
‚Äúas regards the descent of Christ into hell‚Äù had given his enemies a target. The
orthodox position, stating that Christ ‚Äúwas cruciÔ¨Åed, died and was buried, and
descended into Hell,‚Äù was more beloved in popular devotion than debated as
dogmatic theology, and its basis was the Apostles‚Äô Creed‚Äîa prayer that even
illiterate Christians knew by heart. Pecock, denying that this cherished belief was
apostolic, rejected the descent clause in the Creed as a later invention with no basis
in scripture.¬≥¬≥

                         1.4 Places in Hell

By the later Ô¨Åfteenth century, when Pico was writing his books, some authorities
(including Pecock and Lorenzo Valla) no longer believed that the apostles them-
selves had composed the twelve ‚Äòarticles‚Äô of the old Latin creed whose roots
reached back more than a millennium. In the form that became authoritative in
the West by the seventh century, the fourth clause of this ‚ÄòApostles‚Äô Creed‚Äô stated
that Christ ‚Äúdescended into Hell.‚Äù Thomas Aquinas, who attributed the Creed‚Äôs
descent clause to the apostle Philip, was a key Ô¨Ågure in Pico‚Äôs prosecution: the Q1
thesis linked Thomas with the position that Pico seemed to reject, that Christ went
down to Hell ‚Äútruly and as far as real presence is concerned.‚Äù Was the prince so
daring that he simply denied this hallowed profession of faith?¬≥‚Å∏
  No: under interrogation he insisted that ‚Äúno one should understand me to say
that Christ‚Äôs soul did not descend into Hell.‚Äù His judges, nonetheless, ruled that
the Q1 thesis


   strictly speaking (secundum rigorem sermonis) and given the meaning of the
  words (ex vi verborum), is false, erroneous, heretical and contrary to the truth of
   sacred scripture, notwithstanding any explanation produced about the conclu-
   sion by the aforesaid Lord Count Giovanni.

The interrogators recorded their obdurate verdict on March 5, 1487‚Äîbefore the
Apology was written, of course. Pico wrote the book only because of their verdict‚Äî
to refute their decision on the Q1 proposition along with condemnations, some
harsher than others, of twelve other theses. At the start of Q1, just after restating
his position, the defendant insisted that he was right‚Äîby denying that his thesis
was wrong in the way speciÔ¨Åed by the judges, ‚Äúin virtue of its language (de virtute
sermonis) and according to the meaning of its words (secundum vim verborum).‚Äù
If the Pope‚Äôs commissioners wanted to hold the prince to the letter of his
statements, he would rebut them in the same way‚Äîby weighing every syllable
of what he, they, and others said about the issue at hand, framed by the topog-
raphy of Hell.¬≥‚Åπ
  Dante imagined many different places in his underworld, and other Christians
commonly distinguished a ‚ÄúHell of the damned‚Äù from ‚Äúthe place in Hell where the
just were kept‚Äù‚Äîas Aquinas described it. Thomas also taught that Jesus went to
Hell both essentially and effectively, with different effects for different locations.

‚Äúwhile remaining in one part of Hell‚Äù‚Äîthe escapable part‚Äî‚Äúhe distributed his
effect to all the parts.‚Äù‚Å¥‚Å∞
  Thomas‚Äôs account (whose complexity challenged Pico to reply in kind) was
motivated by awkward yet scripturally warranted beliefs about the descent. When
Jesus went down to release the blessed Fathers from Limbo, his unresurrected
body was still in the tomb, so only his soul could have descended, not his body.
But how could a disembodied soul go to a place‚Äîone district of Hell rather than
another? Related questions applied to all the damned souls, whose bones were
moldering in their graves. What place could hold these doomed spirits, and how
could eternal Ô¨Åre roast them  if their bodies were somewhere else? Thomas
multiplied such difÔ¨Åculties about Hell when he wrote that Christ not only sanc-
tiÔ¨Åed the Fathers spiritually, within themselves, but ‚Äúalso visited them in the place
(visitaret et loco)‚Äù of their conÔ¨Ånement‚Äîin Limbo‚Äîand his elaborate explanation
went beyond the Creed‚Äôs simple words: descendit ad inferna.  If this was a
Christian‚Äôs only instruction, that Christ ‚Äúdescended to Hell,‚Äù what else was a
believer obliged to believe about the descent? This question was at the heart of
Pico‚Äôs defense in Q1.‚Å¥¬π
  The prince had an answer: ‚Äúit clearly sufÔ¨Åces for a Christian person to believe
that Christ descended to Hell . . . in the soul only, and that . . . the manner of this
descent . . . was possible, seemly, prompt and suitable.‚Äù His thesis denied none of
this, he insisted, only that Christ‚Äôs presence in Hell‚Äîpresent just by his soul, as
Aquinas agreed‚Äîwas, in other respects, not ‚Äúas proposed by Thomas and on the
usual approach.‚Äù‚Å¥¬≤
  The Q1 thesis follows in full; its wording in the Apology exactly matches the
proposition printed in the Conclusions (the divisions are mine however):


   Christ (Ôù°) did not truly, and (Ôù¢) as far as real presence is concerned, (Ôù£) descend
   to Hell, (Ôù§) as proposed by Thomas and on the usual approach, but (Ôù•) only in
   regard to the effect.‚Å¥¬≥

                       1.5 Doxastic Bondage


Pico divided Q1 into four parts (described later), and he dedicated the last (call it
the ‚Äòepistemic‚Äô part) to constraints on belief, not the descent clause, where the key

Harrowing of Hell‚Äîwhich as a dogmatic problem was secondary‚Äîto a larger
question bearing on all his theses: were there limits, and what were they, on the
Church‚Äôs authority to compel someone‚Äôs beliefs with a charge of heresy? If there

  The Pope‚Äôs agents had reason to worry that a general solution might rescue the

  In Q1, before proceeding to doxastic ethics‚Äîa broader topic than Christ‚Äôs

knowledge (scientia). If so, other options were having faith (Ô¨Ådes), a gift from God,
and believing (credere), a human capability. The content of belief had to be
believable yet could not be demonstrated; unlike a theorem proved by geometry,
a belief was not demonstrable, or else it would be knowledge. And before enter-
taining a belief, which (unlike faith) would always be provisional, a conscientious
believer like the prince would have observed the world, consulted authorities, read
their books, disputed with them and with himself. At some point, however, even
the most fastidious believer could only believe what she believed‚Äîleaving her in
doxastic bondage, whose loose shackles accommodated uncertainty.‚Å¥‚Å∑

                      1.7 Scholastic Disputes


To repeat: eleven of the thirteen theses defended in the Apology were essentially
indistinguishable, in form and content, from the output of medieval theologians
who, for several centuries, had been parsing the Sentences of Peter Lombard. Pico
never cited this ponderous textbook directly in Q1. But he mentioned its title more
than a dozen times in passages like this: ‚ÄúScotus says that sacred scripture does not
hold that Christ descended to Hell, as Fran√ßois de Meyronnes reports on the
fourth book of Sentences.‚Äù Fran√ßois, who died in 1328, was an aggressive Scotist.
Others whose commentaries on the Sentences informed the Q1 dispute‚Äîthrough
citations by Pico‚Äîwere Aquinas, Durand, Herv√© de N√©dellec, and Scotus himself.
The Sentences was the main vehicle for theology in the universities where they

   blocks them from learning the truth because, given the different views on such
   issues held by educated and well-informed people, the truth will be found better
  by disputing to support opposing positions in different ways . . . . Hence to block
   this way of seeking and clarifying the truth is clearly to impede progress.

  Pico shifted the conversation from Hell to heavenly messengers after just a few

prophylactic and practical‚Äîare easy to imagine.

heinous kind. Christ‚Äôs soul in Hell, as scholastic theologians described it, was a
‚Äúseparated substance‚Äù‚Äîseparated from his body. But angels, pure spirits without
bodies, were also treated as separated substances. Hence, angels were safer sub-
stitutes for Christ in Pico‚Äôs metaphysical inquiry.
  A practical reason for switching to angels was that the theological literature
was abundant, unlike discussions of the descent clause. One indicator is a modern
edition of Cabrol‚Äôs Defenses‚Äîindexed in great detail‚Äîwhich has eight columns of
entries under the heading angelus but only half a column under infernus, where
just four entries are relevant to the descent clause. For descensus and cognate
words there is no entry at all. The Bible told more stories about angels than
about Hell, and theologians responded accordingly. Pico studied the localization
of angels in order to understand how Christ‚Äôs soul could have been located
in Hell.‚Å∑‚Å∞


                1.8 Ethics, Logic, and Metaphysics

words, Hobbes mocked the ‚Äúerror of separated essence‚Äù that had been the

  There are two ways for scripture to say that something is or is not in a place or
   enclosable (circumscriptibile), either because it produces distance in a place by
   permitting measurement (dimensio) in length, height and breadth or because it is
   directed and delimited (deÔ¨Ånitur et determinatur) to a place, since when it is
  somewhere, it is not found everywhere, which beÔ¨Åts not only a body but also
   every created spirit.‚Å∑¬≤


If so, an angel could be somewhere, in or at a place, and yet some insisted that
angels could have places ‚Äúonly by activity.‚Äù Others countered that  if an angel

   tive). And on this point my approach agrees with . . . Scotus and all the Scotists . . .
   Just as it beÔ¨Åts a bodily substance to have been somewhere by a shared measure
   only through quantity, so it was beÔ¨Åtting for Christ‚Äôs soul to have been in Hell‚Äî

   presence of Christ‚Äôs soul was what he did there.‚Å∑‚Å¥


Drawing on advanced medieval theories, Pico aligned himself with venerable
beliefs that Hobbes and his contemporaries eventually abandoned as they broke
new trails for philosophy and theology. The prince‚Äôs project in the Conclusions
and Apology did not look to the future. Nor did he favor plain speech in theology.

  This coupled proposition  is the equivalent of a disjunctive (disjunctivus)

Pico described Q1B as a ‚Äúproposition with a coupled extreme (copulatus extre-

number,‚Äô an ‚Äòextreme‚Äô is one term (‚Äòthree‚Äô), and the other (‚Äònumber‚Äô) is either
another extreme or else the ‚Äòmiddle‚Äô term.‚Å∑‚Å∑
  Ockham, while analyzing propositions where conjunctions or adverbs qualiÔ¨Åed
the terms, had explained that


  when the conjunction ‚Äòand‚Äô occurs in such a proposition . . . this can be either
   a coupled proposition or a proposition with a coupled extreme (copulatus
   extremus): . . . ‚Äòthree and two are Ô¨Åve,‚Äô for example. . . . If the proposition has a
  coupled subject, it‚Äôs true. If the proposition is coupled, it‚Äôs false.

In the latter case, where the whole proposition, ‚Äòthree and two are Ô¨Åve,‚Äô is coupled,
the false meaning is that three is Ô¨Åve and two is Ô¨Åve. But if only the subject term,
‚Äòthree and two,‚Äô is coupled, the meaning is that three and two together are Ô¨Åve,
which is true. Pico‚Äôs tactic was to transmute Q1 into such a form in order to
protect it from his judges. His tools came from a branch of logic that analyzed
‚Äòand,‚Äô ‚Äòbut,‚Äô ‚Äòif,‚Äô ‚Äòor,‚Äô and other terms known as syncategoremata. The language that
he and Ockham used for such purposes was not the simple speech of Gospel faith,
though Pico tried to clarify his point with a plain example:


   s¬π Socrates is human; . . .

   s¬≤ Socrates is human, and God is not God; . . .

   s¬≥ therefore, Socrates is not human.


Plainly, s¬π is true, but s¬≤ is plainly false: so, even though the s¬π part of s¬≤ is true, the
whole coupled s¬≤ is false. And yet to conclude s¬≥ by negating s¬≤ would be ‚Äúquite
childish,‚Äù Pico insisted. He realized that moving the ‚Äònot‚Äô in s¬≤ to the left and
replacing ‚Äòand‚Äô with ‚Äòor‚Äô could produce an equivalent proposition,

   s‚Å¥ Socrates is not human, or God is God.


But then, if Socrates were restored to the human race by negating both parts of the
coupled s‚Å¥, this move would also entail that ‚ÄòGod is not God‚Äô‚Äîan unhappy result.
Next, in a ‚Äúsimilar case,‚Äù Pico pointed out that


  to argue from this coupled negative,
    Q1A: Christ did not truly and as far as the real presence is concerned, as
     stated by Thomas, descend . . . ,

mistake by proposing a proposition in public, which  is acceptable in several

Pope and his agents‚Äî‚Äúwho have judged a proposition to be unqualiÔ¨Åedly heretical
even though it has a Catholic meaning.‚Äù‚Å∏‚Å∞
  Although the full roster of Pico‚Äôs detractors in Rome was probably unknown

prince‚Äôs ethical grievance was not inspired by humanists like Leonardo Bruni,

Europe‚Äôs high culture. Jokes about ‚Äòthe schools‚Äô were crowd-pleasers by the time
Descartes, Galileo, Hobbes, Locke and their allies cleared the decks for a new kind

  By their standards, Pico‚Äôs way of doing philosophy in order to clarify theology
was retrograde. By the time Locke wrote his Essay, scholasticism mattered to him
only in the way that the ancien r√©gime mattered to Robespierre. The medieval
architects of yesterday‚Äôs philosophy were no longer important for philosophical
revolutionaries, and Ockham interested them even less than Scotus. Pico had
made use  of Ockham‚Äôs Dialogue, however. He may  also have known the
Centiloquium widely attributed to Ockham and printed in the late Quattrocento.
A topic in this little book was the metaphysics of the incarnation, meant to explain
how the deity could take on a non-divine human nature in the person of Christ.
Pico‚Äôs Q4 thesis engaged this doctrine:


   I do not assent to the usual statement by theologians who say that God can make
  any nature whatever the supposit, granting this instead only for a nature able to
   reason.

Saying that God ‚Äúmakes a nature the supposit‚Äù was the arcane technical talk of
medieval theologians‚Äîanother way of saying that God ‚Äòtakes on‚Äô or ‚Äòassumes‚Äô a
nature. In the miracle of God‚Äôs incarnation (enÔ¨Çeshment), the supposited item
was the Ô¨Çeshy human nature of Jesus, divinized in the process. The suppositing in
this case was metaphysical, as in the Q6 thesis, not the semantic kind involved in
Q9 and Q10.‚Å∏¬≥
  Late in the seventeenth century, outside Jesuit colleges and Dominican or
Franciscan convents, these distinctions would have been lost on many acute
philosophers because the metaphysics of supposition was dying in disgrace and
the semantics of supposition had fallen out of the curriculum. But readers
addressed by the Q4 thesis‚ÄîPico‚Äôs judges, certainly‚Äîwould have recognized
both terminologies, as in this recital from the Centiloquium:


   every merely afÔ¨Årmative proposition is possible which is composed of terms that
  can be shared; in which no negation is posited; whose terms supposit (suppo-
   nunt) for something; and which is not completely falsiÔ¨Åed for divine supposits
   (suppositis) without new terms being brought in.

Pico and his contemporaries‚Äîthose who cared about philosophy‚Äîwould have
known that the second suppo- word might be metaphysical but that the Ô¨Årst was
manifestly semantic. Reading on, braving the logical gymnastics, they would also

the very same kind. Pico‚Äôs persona in these books‚Äîtwo-thirds of his public
record‚Äîwas scholastic, not humanist. The word ‚Äòhumanist,‚Äô of course, was rare
in his day and did not name an advocate of what most people (apart from experts

them hanging just to annoy you? You could not know that he lifted them all from

2
           What‚Äôs In a Word?

             Metaphysics and Semantics



                        2.1 Gigantic Jokes

Words, words, words: Pico‚Äôs Apology used more than Ô¨Åfty thousand, and many
were non-standard Latin, invented in the Middle Ages to express post-classical
innovations in logic, metaphysics, and theology. Medieval novelties in the Apology
include nouns like assumptibilitas, impassibilitas, and substantialitas; verbs like
dogmatizare, necessitare, and substentiÔ¨Åcare; also adjectives like compossibilis,
elevabilis, and susceptibilis to go with the nouns; as well as adverbs like ampliative,
circumscriptive, and recitative to modify the verbs. The tiny ly, a deÔ¨Ånite article
unknown to speakers of Rome‚Äôs ancient tongue, appears thirteen times. And one
of the prince‚Äôs new adjectives was suppositabilis.¬π
  A millennium or more before Pico wrote, if Cicero, Quintilian, or Servius had
come across suppositabilis, the word might have surprised them. These gatekeep-
ers of Latin might have considered suppositabilis not a ‚Äòreal word,‚Äô as people say
now in similar circumstances. Once uttered or written, however, any word is at
least a little real. Twenty years ago, who knew what ‚Äòbiohacking‚Äô means now? But
even then a competent speaker of English would have recognized ‚Äòbio‚Äô + ‚Äòhacking.‚Äô
The same goes for native speakers of pre-medieval Latin in comparable situations.
Supponere, ‚Äòto put under,‚Äô and suppositum, ‚Äòwhat is put under,‚Äô were standard
in Cicero‚Äôs lexicon. He could have guessed right away‚Äîif he heard or saw
suppositabilis‚Äîthat ‚Äòable to be put under‚Äô might be the thought.
  In Cicero‚Äôs world, items ‚Äòput under‚Äô as supposita were usually physical things,
like bricks or boards. But supposita in Pico‚Äôs philosophical usage were bodiless,
either items of logic and language or metaphysical entities. Putting one tangible
thing under another is easy to grasp: if someone asks you to put a large rock under
a smaller rock, you‚Äôll know right away where the bigger suppositum goes. But what
about supposita that can‚Äôt be located in space, moved around, or touched? The
question points to a gap where comedy can slip in between ordinary, everyday
puttings and the rarer philosophical kind. A philosophical suppositum might be
funny because it surprises, signaling a disconnect: you expect one thing (a joke

   Surprise is one way for words to be funny. Another is deviance, by transgressing
norms that someone wants to enforce.¬≤ Not too long ago, TV audiences in North
America laughed at racist mockery of speech: Amos‚Äôn Andy, Charlie Chan, the
Cisco Kid, and other shameful entertainments. Also, long before television,
Romans made fun of how Phoenicians talked, just as Greeks had ridiculed
Persians. In Pico‚Äôs era, the literati now called ‚Äòhumanists‚Äô‚Äîas if all their habits
were humane‚Äîrevived this and other ancient vices along with ancient virtues.
Fran√ßois Rabelais, who may have been born in the decade when Pico wrote his
Conclusions, became a master of this classicizing culture and a great artist in
prose.¬≥
   Rabelais mentioned Pico nowhere in his writings, even though he dropped
names on every page he wrote‚Äîeither explicitly or by allusion, sometimes to jeer,
sometimes to cheer. Traces of Erasmus are everywhere in his stories, also echoes of
Thomas More and like-minded intellectuals, including Agrippa von Nettesheim,
Andrea  Alciati, Guillaume Bud√©, Marsilio Ficino, Jacques Lef√®vre d‚Äô√âtaples,
Cl√©ment Marot, Johann Reuchlin, Andr√© Tiraqueau, and Lorenzo Valla. In this
setting‚Äîespecially since Rabelais was curious about Kabbalah‚Äîignoring Pico
seems odd.‚Å¥
  Time and place may have been reasons enough: when Rabelais was doing his
best work in France, Pico, an Italian, had been dead for forty years. But Ficino was
Italian too, like Valla, who died in 1457‚Äîsix years before Pico was born. If Valla
and other erudite celebrities were ‚Äòhumanists‚Äô (still a rare word at the time), maybe
Rabelais saw Pico as something different. If all he had to go on was Pico‚Äôs Apology
and Conclusions, he would have found little to connect the prince with Bud√©,
Erasmus, or More. Quite the contrary: in these scandalous works of the prince‚Äôs
youth, Pico‚Äôs language was mostly the kind that Rabelais belittled with uproarious
comedy. His jokes mocked deviations‚Äîlike suppositabilis‚Äîfrom the genteel Latin
of Erasmus, More and other heirs of Petrarch and Coluccio Salutati, who had been
grumbling about philosophical speech since the fourteenth century.
  The funniest comedy by Rabelais was the Very HorriÔ¨Åc Life of the Great
Gargantua. Before printing was invented, this giant was born in an earlier age of
miracles, from his mother‚Äôs ear, a virgin birth that showed what a wonder he was.
His colossal ancestors‚Äîincluding Gargamelle, his mother, and her husband,
Grangousier‚Äîwere famed for huge gullets, good for gluttonous eating, and even
better for guzzling wine by the barrel. Rabelais, who took vows as a Franciscan
friar around 1510, released the story of Gargantua‚Äôs adventures‚Äîhis second work

   After they had therefored (ergot√©) pro and con, they concluded syllogistically (in

  In the middle of the next century, by the time Moli√®re was staging other

                 2.2 Old Words in Modern Times


Like Francis Bacon and Shakespeare in England or Bruno and Galileo in Italy,
Montaigne, Descartes, and Moli√®re established new norms in France for a modern
literary vernacular. Although Rabelais had written nothing normal or modern, the
effect of his books was progressive. The hilarity instigated by Gargantua and
Pantagruel was a solvent for stale dogma, showing how to laugh the past away into
irrelevance. After such comic encouragement, a thoroughly serious modern sci-
ence began with Newton, just as modern philosophy in the same key began with
Descartes: two of their books‚Äîthe Meditationes of 1641 and the Principia of
1687‚Äîare early monuments of modernity, and both were written in Latin.
   Science has not repealed Newton‚Äôs laws, and the language of physics  still
includes vernacular replicas of his Latin terminology: acceleratio, gravitas, reactio,
velocitas, and so  on. The same goes  for  Descartes and  his  Meditationes.
Philosophers still ask if mind (mens) is just matter (materia), if thoughts are real
(realis), and if a claim that God exists (existere) could be veriÔ¨Åed (verus).
  Descartes wrote the Meditations in an ancient language rarely used in his time
for everyday purposes, and he inherited his philosophical vocabulary from a past
that was remote‚Äîmore or less. The newer part of the meditator‚Äôs lexicon came
from medieval debates about metaphysics, nature and theology where essentia,
qualitas, quantitas, substantia, and related terms were central and constantly
contested. While Descartes was alive, the terminal phase of this combat erupted:
critics of medieval philosophy charged that the ‚Äúcanting of the schoolmen‚Äù‚Äîthe
technical terminology of scholastic philosophy‚Äîwas a perversion of language.
Unlike Rabelais, they were deadly serious.¬π‚Åµ
  Thomas Hobbes, an accomplished Latinist who wrote his philosophical master-
piece in English, attacked the scholastics for using speech that had ‚Äúno signiÔ¨Åca-
tion at all.‚Äù The Catholic eucharist horriÔ¨Åed this unorthodox Protestant, and he
demanded to know how saying ‚Äúcertain words‚Äù could make ‚Äúwhiteness, round-
ness, magnitude, quality, corruptibility . . . go out of the wafer into the body of our
blessed Saviour,‚Äù charging that ‚Äúthose -nesses, -tudes and -ties‚Äù were like ‚Äúspirits
possessing‚Äù Christ‚Äôs body. Accusing Catholics of treating their own sacrament like




   ¬π‚Åµ Hobbes, Lev. 1.5.6.

Descartes accepted essentia, qualitas, quantitas, substantia, and others from his

Another key item in the medieval lexicon‚Äîthe noun suppositum along with its

the body of his book but only to say that the meditator ‚Äúassumes‚Äù or ‚Äúsupposes‚Äù
something.¬π‚Å∏

miracle, ‚ÄúGod is a human,‚Äù as equivalent to ‚Äúthe Word is a suppositum, which is
a human.‚Äù Hence, according to Liceto, the word ‚ÄúGod‚Äù in the original proposition
‚Äúsupposits for a supposit‚Äù‚Äîsupponit pro supposito.¬π‚Åπ

metaphysics and semantics, between language and ontology.

Heidegger could Ô¨Åll in the blanks with . . . in seiner jeweiligen Weise. Quine‚Äôs
‚Äúvalue of a variable‚Äù is a logical phrase, however, and m¬π was an anti-metaphysical
slogan.¬≤¬π

The boundaries have been hard to police, and one solution might just say no‚Äîlike
Heidegger or like Quine‚Äîto metaphysics. Moreover, if the fabric of reality were
actually  linguistic,  semantics  alone  could tend  to   it, making  metaphysics
expendable.

  John Wyclif knew all about metaphysics but never heard of semantics‚Äîunder

            2.3 Theodicy, Blasphemy, and Bad Spelling


Could God ever be mistaken? If the Creator of the universe is the God of perfect-
being theology, the question is impertinent: a perfect Deity is not just Ô¨Çawless
but omnipotent, omniscient, and absolutely the best‚Äînot merely good. But if
the creation has evil in it‚Äîdeath, disease, pain, and so on‚Äîwhat about the
Creator? Theodicy, alert to defects in the universe, asks such questions. Perhaps
a thoroughly good world  is God‚Äôs plan, but mistakes are made.  If theodicy
asks whether God‚Äîacting in eternity, not in time‚Äîis mistaken (utrum Deus est
falsus), this question (qT) might be well-formed. Medieval philosophers thought
that‚Äîin principle‚Äîreason could conÔ¨Årm conclusions  like cT as answers to
such questions.





   ¬≤¬≤ Aug. Doct. 1.2; Conti (2013), p. 304; Lahey (2009), pp. 79‚Äì82; (2012), p. 532.

## Page 68

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  55


   p¬π Something that makes a defective thing is mistaken;
   p¬≤ God is the maker of a defective thing:
  cT therefore, God is mistaken.

The syllogism‚Äîstarting with a deÔ¨Ånitional statement (p¬π), adding an observation
(p¬≤), and concluding with cT‚Äîis formally correct (valid). The conclusion (cT) is
also a proposition in good form, whether it‚Äôs true or false. But maybe something
has gone wrong with p¬π and/or p¬≤. If so, maybe cT is false, despite its good form.
  Nothing about the form of cT as it stands, as long as we ignore its relation to
facts, causes concern: so it may be that ‚Äòis God mistaken,‚Äô the question (qT) behind
cT, is also well-formed. With the creation of the universe under scrutiny, we can
also ask a different question (qS):  ‚Äòis God misspelled?‚Äô But this question goes
nowhere because God isn‚Äôt the sort of thing that can be spelled, correctly or
incorrectly. Even though the qS query asserts nothing, it looks like nonsense‚Äîa
nonsense question.
  Common conventions of punctuation now alert readers to such problems. We
can write qS in a way that makes the form of this question, no matter the content,
visibly distinct from the form of qT:

  qS Is ‚ÄòGod‚Äô misspelled?
  qT Is God mistaken?

Medieval students of the relevant issues‚Äîtheology, theodicy, semantics, philology
and logic‚Äîseldom used such devices when they asked their questions, like these,
in Latin:

  qS Utrum Deus est perperam scriptus.
  qT Utrum Deus est falsus.¬≤¬≥

Addressing qT by proving a negative answer would conÔ¨Årm that the Creator is
infallible and perfect. But scribes who wrote the creation story down were not
perfect, and where Hebrew scripture Ô¨Årst mentions the Almighty, God‚Äôs name
looks plural even though the closest verb is singular: Bereshit bara Elohim. ‚ÄúIn the
beginning God/Gods created‚Äù: which is correct? If one scribe had corrupted the
text, another could emend it.
  Educated people asked the pertinent questions about biblical philology, just as
their colleagues asked about biblical theology. They treated questions like qS and
qT as queries in good order, and cS, Deus est perperam scriptus, was a possible
response to  qS. Medieval thinkers lacked modern devices that  clarify such

inquiries, but they applied other resources, including various theories of ‚Äòsuppos-

  Medieval critics of language and thought distinguished supposition from sig-
niÔ¨Åcation (signiÔ¨Åcatio) as different species of the same genus, called ‚Äòproperties of
terms.‚Äô Terms are words or phrases that are parts of propositions‚Äîstatements like
p¬π, p¬≤, and cT. With or without these propositions, however, the words in them
have the property of signiÔ¨Åcation. The word ‚ÄòGod‚Äô in cT signiÔ¨Åes or has signiÔ¨Åca-

universe‚Äô is one of these signiÔ¨Åcations. The property of signifying belongs to ‚ÄòGod,‚Äô

other hand, belongs only to a word that is a term in propositional context.¬≤‚Å¥
  Even such elementary understandings about supposition were disputed by the
time the theory reached its peak in the Ô¨Årst half of the fourteenth century, in
writings by William of Ockham and others. The theory‚Äôs roots were older,
however, in place by the end of the twelfth century. An inÔ¨Çuential (though
inadequate) introduction to the theory was available by 1250 in Peter of Spain‚Äôs
Summaries of Logic.
   Peter wrote his Summaries for teenagers who began the university curriculum
with a basic course in logic. Six of the twelve books of the Summaries introduce the
logic invented by Aristotle, explicated by Porphyry, and Latinized by Boethius
(Figure 2.1) before he was killed in prison. Much of this material was available in
Latin seven centuries before the Summaries, and its focus was the syllogism: an
argument-form in three propositions (two premisses and a conclusion), each
needing two terms (subject and predicate) along with a quantiÔ¨Åer (explicit or
implicit) and a copula‚Äîlike ‚Äòno God is mistaken,‚Äô where ‚Äòno‚Äô (nullus) is a quan-
tiÔ¨Åer, ‚ÄòGod‚Äô (Deus) is the subject term, ‚Äòis‚Äô (est) is the copula, and ‚Äòmistaken‚Äô(falsus)
is the predicate term, adding up to nullus Deus est falsus (cN).¬≤‚Åµ
  To most Christians, such sentences would have seemed bizarre, perverse and
blasphemous. But when logicians constructed them to present their theories, they
used a specialized, regimented language, the Latin of logic books, which was
artiÔ¨Åcial and tightly constrained. In their ideal state, example-sentences like cN‚Äî
designed for elementary manuals‚Äîalways connected a singular modiÔ¨Åer (like

maybe Peter‚Äôs (corporate) memory of this experience motivated his lengthy

supposition theory was still a novelty when he summed it up for adolescents.¬≤‚Åπ

background in mind, Peter explained that ‚Äúsupposition and signiÔ¨Åcation are

statements true or false.
  For this purpose, Peter classiÔ¨Åed types of suppositing as common or discrete,
natural or accidental, simple or personal, and delimited or confused. Even at the
time, these labels were not transparent: at Ô¨Årst glance, Peter‚Äôs personal, for
example, would seem not to need any persons. He described this type of suppos-

when someone says a human runs, the term human supposits for those under it.‚Äù
But ‚Äòhorse‚Äô or ‚Äòwagon,‚Äô which are not persons, would have worked just as well as
‚Äúhuman‚Äù to illustrate personal supposition.
  Nonetheless, a modern account of supposition starts with the personal type as
‚Äúthe standard case, where a term stands for ordinary objects.‚Äù But Peter‚Äôs per-

medieval: he treated the homo in cR, where this word designates at least one
individual human, as also suitable for naming the species of which such individ-
uals are subordinate (inferiores) members‚Äîeven though, as substances, individ-
uals are primary and species are secondary.¬≥¬π
  The eight types of supposition listed by Peter would not have helped his
students with sentences like  cS, ‚ÄòGod  is misspelled.‚Äô Later logicians classiÔ¨Åed
such statements under  material  supposition, which‚Äîlike material evidence
today in a court of law‚Äîwas not about matter in the usual sense. In material
supposition, the subject of cS stands for  itself, the word ‚ÄòGod,‚Äô not for God.
Accordingly, William of Ockham described supposition as ‚Äúmaterial when a
term does not supposit as signifying but supposits for a spoken or written word,
which is clear in man is a noun, where man supposits for itself yet does not signify
itself.‚Äù If the semantic form of cS follows Ockham‚Äôs example, homo est nomen, then
the word Deus in cS‚ÄîDeus est perperam scriptus‚Äîsupposits for itself, not the
Deity, and cS says nothing irreverent.¬≥¬≤

the subject of cL ‚Äúsupposit (supponit) personally and for singulars, . . . or does it
supposit personally and for a shared form?‚Äù Ockham was testing a theory that he
had just stated: a theory of semantics. He began the same short paragraph by
asserting that another statement (cP) is true: ‚Äúa complete intellectual substance,

in cP  is a substance that  is also a person and thus, prima facie, physical or
metaphysical, rather than semantic or logical.¬≥¬≥
  Suppositing can be described as a dyadic relation, Ôù∏RÔùπ, whose relata, Ôù∏and Ôùπ,
are words, thoughts, or non-linguistic, extramental things in various conÔ¨Ågur-
ations tracked by different types (personal, material and so on) of supposition.
This is the type of relation expressed by supponit in Ockham‚Äôs qL, whose relata are
a word or term, ‚Äúhuman,‚Äù and either a primary substance (a ‚Äúsingular‚Äù like
Socrates) or a secondary substance (a ‚Äúshared form‚Äù like the human species).
Either way, the supponit in qL reaches beyond language and mind to the non-
linguistic, non-mental items in the world called res or ‚Äòthings.‚Äô One such item
mentioned in cP is a suppositum which is or is like a person (Socrates) and a
substance (also Socrates) and also a nature (the one that belongs only to Socrates,
not to you, Plato, or me).
  The noun suppositum in cP is therefore substantial, not merely relational like
the verb supponit in qL. And the task of this suppositum is metaphysical (indicat-
ing a substance or person or nature), whereas the job done by supponit is semantic
(relating words or thoughts to things or to one another). When Ockham left the
relational supponit so close to the substantive suppositum without signaling a
distinction between them, was he stranded between semantics and metaphysics?
Maybe not. Maybe elementary grammar was enough to Ô¨Çag this other distinction
because the noun suppositum and the verb supponit differ as parts of speech.
  Consider another statement (already discussed) from the Centiloquium often
attributed to Ockham: ‚ÄúGod has taken on some nature in the unity of a supposit
(in unitate suppositi).‚Äù The context was the metaphysics of the incarnation, used
by theologians to address a hard question: how could a bodiless Deity have the
embodied nature of Jesus? One answer: nothing is impossible for an omnipotent
God who ‚Äúcan take on a stone, a stick of wood and so on.‚Äù Absolute power cleared
the way for logicians to contemplate shocking statements like ‚ÄòGod is a stone‚Äô and
analyze them as ‚Äúpropositions whose terms supposit for something.‚Äù When
various semantic Ôù∏RÔùπrelations were up for discussion, even  if Ôù∏and Ôùπwere
God and a stone, to ‚Äúsupposit for something‚Äù might be theologically blameless,
and ‚ÄòGod is a stone‚Äô might be no more irreverent than ‚ÄòGod is misspelled.‚Äô¬≥‚Å¥
  But the ‚Äúunity of a supposit‚Äù mentioned by the Centiloquium was the core of
divinity itself in theological discussions: it was a term of art that stressed Christ‚Äôs

in essence.


                           2.5 Relations


In the Apology, Pico used suppositum, supponere and their cousins nearly 150
times to defend himself, mainly on points of metaphysics. But like other philo-
sophers of his day, he also used these suppo- words for quite different purposes,
logical and semantic. Moreover, the differences between these applications‚Äîthe
metaphysical and the semantic‚Äîwere crucial for his case in court. Unless the
prince‚Äôs judges realized that his suppo- terminology was metaphysical at one
moment but semantic at another, his arguments would pass them by. And yet
the defendant never highlighted or clariÔ¨Åed this crucial distinction.
  By staying silent about  it, Pico did as his medieval predecessors had done
for nearly a millennium, ever since Boethius devised a Latin vocabulary for
Aristotelian logic and metaphysics as philosophical foundations of Christian
dogma.  Heirs  of  Boethius‚ÄîPico‚Äôs  philosophical  ancestors‚Äîdeveloped  the
Apology‚Äôs suppositional metaphysics and suppositional semantics in the Middle
Ages. These medieval theologians, philosophers, and grammarians often crossed
the metaphysical/semantic divide, but they rarely took note of it‚Äîdespite its
momentous consequences.




   ¬≥‚Åµ Aquinas, ST 3.2.2, as in note 87 of this chapter, explains that in creatures ‚Äúthe nature and the
suppositum really differ‚Äù but that ‚Äúin God the suppositum is not really anything other than the nature.‚Äù
   ¬≥‚Å∂ Likewise, the relation in which a unique Ôù´relates in a unitary way to Ôù´is formally a Ôù´RÔù´dyad.

  To repeat: both metaphysical relations and semantic relations were expressed
by suppositum, supponere, and their relatives. The general form of a dyadic
relation between any Ôù∏and any Ôùπis Ôù∏RÔùπ. Other types of relation are interpret-
ations or special cases of R. In a list of relations, more or less abstract‚Äîlike P for
‚Äòputting,‚Äô as in ‚Äòputting with,‚Äô ‚Äòputting under,‚Äô ‚Äòputting over,‚Äô and so on‚Äîrelata can
also be interpreted by replacing Ôù∏and Ôùπwith Ôù∑for items of language, Ôù≠for mental
items and Ôù≤for real, extramental, non-linguistic things.
   Relata are more restricted in some relations than in others. If ‚Äòputting‚Äô applies
only to real, physical things, for example, only Ôù≤relata will be involved in P
relations of putting. But if anything can replace anything in a more generalized
replacement (Q) relation, Ôù≠, Ôù≤and Ôù∑relata will all be possible for Q: since one word
can replace another just as one person or thing or substance can replace another,
this Q relation might be metaphysical/physical or semantic or also mental.
  The list of relations in Table 2.1 is long but only suggestive‚Äînot complete
or deÔ¨Ånitive. Relations or types of relation (as with Q for replacing) may over-
lap and may be compatible with one another‚Äîor not compatible. The Ô¨Årst
column in Table 2.1 gives names of relations tagged with an arbitrary symbol
in the second (broken) column, followed by symbols for relata in the third
column. IdentiÔ¨Åcations of relata are provisional, by no means well established.
Directionality, symmetry, transitivity and so on may be relevant but, for the sake of
simplicity, are not indicated. The R relation, the most general, is in boldface.
   Latin expressions with relational forms from A B Œì to Œ¶ Œ® Œ©‚Äîusing Greek
letters for semantics, Roman for metaphysics and mixed cases‚Äîare common in
medieval texts at the intersection of two difÔ¨Åcult theological doctrines: the
Trinity, stating that the one God  is also three persons (personae); and the
incarnation, stating that one of the persons, Christ, has two natures (naturae),
one divine, the other human. God is Christ; Christ is God; Christ has a divine
nature and a human nature: so the one God has a divine nature and a human
nature. This human nature is not an abstraction, like ‚Äòhumanity.‚Äô The concrete,
singular human nature in Christ is Christ‚Äôs alone, just as the human nature in
Socrates belonged only to the most annoying son of Sophroniscus. Both these
human natures are particular concrete instances of the humanity that  is an
abstract essence or universal.
  The ‚Äòtaking-on‚Äô relation, Ôù≤AÔù≤, as used by some medieval philosophers about
God and a human nature, applies to them if and only if an identity relation, Ôù≤IÔù≤,
also applies, which is possible if the divine essence is distinct contingently and
formally‚Äîbut not necessarily and really‚Äîfrom the nature taken on. This meta-
physical Ô¨Ånding appeals to contingency in order to understand a real, change-
less, eternal, and absolutely perfect Creator.¬≥‚Å∑

In some settings, suppositum is also more or less synonymous with substantia. But
a human nature is surely not God‚Äôs substance, so in t‚Å¥‚Äîas in other contexts‚Äîthe
suppositum is hard to pin down. Two types of substantia, primary and secondary,
must also be tracked. Examples of primary substances created by God are the
oldest oak on the Acropolis, the red frog named Rosalie, the green frog named
Roscoe, and the most annoying son of Sophroniscus. The God who created them
all is deÔ¨Ånitely primary but maybe not a substance.

  When students in medieval universities learned logic, they also learned this

  In proposition r¬π, Rosalia refers to the primary substance named by the subject

   r¬π Rosalia est rana, ‚ÄòRosalie is frog‚Äô;

   r¬≤ Rosalia est rufa, ‚ÄòRosalie is red‚Äô;

   r¬≥ Rosalia est Rosalia, ‚ÄòRosalie is Rosalie.‚Äô


In each proposition, a predicate term (rana, rufa, Rosalia) is said of (predicated of)
the subject term (Rosalia). This logico-linguistic or semantic relation, Œ¶‚Äîwhich is
saying-of or being said-of‚Äîwas sometimes held to reÔ¨Çect a metaphysical relation,
B, being-in or inhering. Accordingly, when rufa is said of Rosalia by  r¬≤, the
proposition implies that the color red is in Rosalie or belongs to her‚Äînothing
remarkable. Not so clear is r¬π, however, and the ill-formed English sentence‚Äî
lacking a deÔ¨Ånite or indeÔ¨Ånite article before ‚Äòfrog‚Äô‚Äîalerts the reader. Correcting
the usage by writing ‚Äòa frog‚Äô instead of ‚Äòfrog‚Äô in r¬π might suggest that the predicate
term picks out some one particular frog, the one named Rosalie, as in r¬≥‚Äînot
obviously a false statement, but not informative either, and a violation of the
(realist) rule that primary substances can‚Äôt be predicates.
  The information that r¬π delivers can be expressed in different ways: that Rosalie
is a member of the frog species; that the frog essence or frog nature belongs to
Rosalie; and so on. In these cases, as with rufa in r¬≤, rana can be correctly and
informatively predicated of Rosalia. Like rufa, this rana can be said of Rosalie,
although the species rana is not in Rosalie as the color rufa is in her, as an accident
inhering contingently in a substance or a quality belonging contingently to a
subject. Without ceasing to be Rosalie, Rosalia could cease to be rufa, but as herself
she could not stop belonging to the rana species.
  One response to all this was a fuller statement of the realist rule (already
mentioned) that governs primary and secondary substances: secondary substances
like the species rana can be said of other things, but primary substances like
Rosalie cannot be in or be said of other things. This rule governs r¬π, where the rana
in the predicate can‚Äôt designate a primary substance. Moreover, rufa in r¬≤ does not

                  2.6 Putting and Coming Under


The Latin used in universities by Peter of Spain, Ockham, and their colleagues had
acquired philosophical resources from the Greek language centuries earlier, when
Augustine, Boethius, Cicero, and other native speakers of Latin studied, translated,
and commented on works by Aristotle, Plato, Porphyry, and other Greek authors.
Primitives of the Greek philosophical lexicon were the simple verbs ‚Äòlie‚Äô (Œ∫Œµ·øñŒºŒ±Œπ), ‚Äòput‚Äô

(·ΩëœÄŒø-) and ‚Äòdown‚Äô (Œ∫Œ±œÑŒ±-). Latin analogs of Œ∫Œµ·øñŒºŒ±Œπ, œÑŒØŒ∏Œ∑ŒºŒπ, ·º¥œÉœÑŒ∑ŒºŒπ, Œ≤Œ¨ŒªŒªœâ, ·ΩëœÄŒø- and
Œ∫Œ±œÑŒ±-, are jaceo (‚Äòlie‚Äô), pono (‚Äòput‚Äô), sto (‚Äòstand‚Äô) and jacio (‚Äòthrow‚Äô), compounded
with sub- as a preÔ¨Åx.

stantia (‚Äòsubstance‚Äô). However, the related verb substare, ‚Äòstand under,‚Äô corres-
ponds not to ·ΩëœÜ·Ω∑œÉœÑŒ∑ŒºŒπ, ‚Äòstand under,‚Äô but to a passive form of the same verb,

## Page 84

  The  verb  supponere  and  its  nominalizations,  like  suppositum,  mainly
