hide something by switching it deceptively for something else. In such uses, the
P/Q relation acquired moral and legal weight‚Äîputting a baby under a blanket in
an S transaction, for example, in order to ‚Äòsubstitute‚Äô a different infant and ‚Äòpass
it off ‚Äô as the original, as in comedies by Plautus and Terence.‚Å¥‚Å∏ Absent the legal
or moral burden, this comedic switching had a serious future in suppositional
semantics. Otherwise, suppo- words in ancient Latin showed little philosophical
promise.
  A few statements by Cicero are marginal exceptions. Making a point about
species in a genus, for example, he used supponere in a taxonomical T relation.
Discussing another writer‚Äôs text, he used the same verb in a ŒÝ ‚Äòlisting‚Äô relation to
register movement from one item to another in a series.‚Å¥‚Åπ This banal use of suppo-
words is frequent in the medieval commentary literature where richer applications
also evolved. To comment on an author‚Äôs text, a commentator would keep saying
(of the author) ‚Äòthen he puts this down‚Äô or ‚Äòthen he adds,‚Äô again and again:
supponit, supponit, supponit . . .‚Åµ‚Å∞
  For this and other uses of suppo- words, see Table 2.2, where the difference
between the Ô¨Årst two rows indicates changes in technical vocabulary between the
classical Latin of Cicero‚Äôs era and the Latin of Boethius. The table also shows that
both Boethius and Priscian were inÔ¨Çuential: their innovations persisted in works
by Peter Helias, Abelard, Gilbert of Poitiers, and later medieval authorities.
Although the table displays the ancestry of Œ® (standing-for) and Œ© (suppositing)
relations developed after the twelfth century by terminist logicians, these later
types themselves are not included.
  By the time Augustine was writing in the late fourth century, Christians in the
Latin West realized that Greek theology had a useful terminology that they could
Latinize. Boethius, a Christian executed by a mightier Christian in 524 Ôù£Ôù•, planned
to put all of Plato and Aristotle into Latin, but he Ô¨Ånished only a few logical
works and some commentaries. One was Porphyry‚Äôs Introduction, a short and
teachable essay on metaphysics and  its terminology that was used in Greek
schools to introduce Aristotle‚Äôs logical writings. Presented in this way, Aristotle‚Äôs
Categories and other logical treatises acquired a metaphysical framework that
stayed with them in Latin translations and commentaries, and the few that
Boethius Ô¨Ånished (in very late antiquity or the earliest medieval times: take your
pick) were enormously inÔ¨Çuential.‚Åµ¬π

founding. But this F relation is said suitably of creatures, whose features like

such support, is also not a subiectum for his creatures, although he is the Ô¨Årst
principle that grounds their being.

separated from what it is a token of because the one is an utterance and signifying,

  Elsewhere, a semantic Œî/Œò relation (propositional/referring) could also suggest

philosophy that had been written in Greek‚Äîthe language of the Christian Gospels
and Epistles as well as the tongue of Plato and Aristotle.


                           2.7 Grammar

Priscian of Caesarea, a contemporary of Boethius, wrote a grammar‚Äîhis bulky
Elements in eighteen books‚Äîthat analyzed Latin in great detail for medieval
speakers and writers. Since Priscian was born in North Africa, his maternal
language may have been neither Latin nor Greek, though Greek prevailed in
Constantinople, where he worked. Earlier Latin grammars, according to the
experts, had been just grammatical and devoid of semantics‚Äîlet alone metaphys-
ics. But Priscian took his lead from Greek grammarians, especially Apollonius
Dyscolus (Ô¨Ç. 150 Ôù£Ôù•), whose language-theory was Stoic and whose philosophical
outlook encouraged attention to semantics in grammar.‚Åµ‚Å∂
  The reported dearth of semantics in the earliest Latin grammars raises a
question: what would a grammar be like without semantics? Maybe just lists of
word-classes and inÔ¨Çections under headings like ‚Äònoun‚Äô and ‚Äòverb‚Äô? But semantic
issues would be inevitable in a richer account of grammar. Once syntax was a
topic, anaphora would come up, for example: anaphoric language, words referring
to antecedent words, expressed semantic Œò/Q/S relations by replacing or switch-
ing words in order to refer. Every student of elementary Latin had to master the
relative, indeÔ¨Ånite and interrogative pronouns, qui and quis (‚Äòwho‚Äô), which were
always anaphoric, explicitly or implicitly.
  Whatever Priscian‚Äôs original purposes and sources were, his medieval readers
had to haul metaphysical baggage right from the start, when he ruled that ‚Äúit
belongs to a noun to signify substance and quality.‚Äù Elsewhere he deÔ¨Åned a noun
as a ‚Äúpart of speech signifying a common or proper quality of some one thing

  Another use of a suppo- word by Priscian was more elusive, coming just after a

priority in this setting, and the priority was metaphysical.‚Åµ‚Å∏

  human is also understood), or even a part is substituted (subicitur) and is taken

other relations besides Œû‚Äîat least Œò and Q for referring and replacing‚Äîwere also

  In the twelfth century, Peter Helias commented on Priscian‚Äôs Elements on the

asking questions, stating objections, and answering them. Hence, in any passage of

   a pronoun takes the place of a noun, which names a substance plus a proper

   a pronoun designates a substance without designating its proper quality;

founding F relation seems right for some of the suppo- words in these passages,
which does not rule out other cases of ‚Äòreferent‚Äô in the Œò or Œ£ relations of referring

referring, like name-giving, was semantic, with no metaphysics needed. Elsewhere,
however, the suppositio that ‚Äúputs a thing under substantial or accidental forms‚Äù

founding and also for semantic Œò (referring), Q (replacing), or Œ£ (anaphoric)
relations, Peter opened Priscian‚Äôs legacy up to colleagues in twelfth-century

                      2.8 Logic and Theology


The dazzling Peter Abelard, a generation older than Peter Helias, worked in the
same cultural and institutional environment but was not satisÔ¨Åed with grammar
as a response to the great theological problems of the day. Logic was a more exact
instrument, he decided. One of his efforts in this Ô¨Åeld was a Logic in the form of
commentary on works by Aristotle, Porphyry, and Boethius. Abelard was also a
theologian‚Äîdaringly creative about the Trinity. The Christian Theology was a

product of his middle period: unlike earlier and later ventures, this Theology was

  Abelard wasn‚Äôt always provocative. While commenting on Boethius, who

The sustaining was  explicit, foundational and metaphysical, but so was the

From one point of view, this account of putting-under went astray by mixing logic

The suppositio or coming-under which is foundational‚Äîa metaphysical thing-to-
thing connection‚Äîis also semantic and reaches from word to word.‚Å∑‚Åµ
  According to Abelard, philosophers‚Äîlike himself‚Äîwho applied these new
notions to theology were taking big risks. His Christian Theology had this warning
for students of Aristotle‚Äôs ten categories:


  They make every item subject (subiiciant) either to substance or to another of the
   rest of the most general items, and they would certainly do the same with God as
   well if they counted him along with substances . . . since nothing at all is located
   outside them. Indeed, all things except substances are incapable of existing on
   their own‚Äîunless they are sustained by underliers (subiectis), in other words,
   just as there is no way for whiteness to exist except in an underlying (subiecto)
  body . . . . But substances can subsist by themselves and persist in their own nature
  when all the rest have been destroyed . . . . Hence it‚Äôs clear that God, who in the
  whole array of things is the sole and singular Ô¨Årst principle (principium), is by no
  means to be counted among things that are not substances. According to philosophers,
   however, God also does not come under (supponitur) substance, even though he is
  what exists on its own and most of all.‚Å∑‚Å∂


In F relations of founding, substances underlaid features like color that could not
exist without substances, which went on existing when some of their features
vanished. God was obviously not one of the items which, since they were not
substances, needed substances as foundations. Moreover, as the unique ground of
everything created, the Creator was also not subordinate to substance in a genus/
species T taxonomy: none of the categories, neither substance nor accidents of
substance, encompassed the Deity. Although logic was Abelard‚Äôs method, his
project was metaphysical and theological‚Äîto assure that the Creator‚Äôs relation

                         2.9 Terms of Art

Gilbert‚Äôs use of suppo- words in F, G, ŒÝ, P, T, and Œ¶ relations (Table 2.2) makes
difÔ¨Åcult reading‚Äîin part because the terminology was still evolving‚Äîeven though
his handling of these words was careful, insightful and parsimonious, as far as
dogma would allow. A deÔ¨Åning feature of Gilbert‚Äôs situation‚Äîas of 1150, a few
years before he died‚Äîwas that the Latin suppo- words did not yet say what
‚Äòsupposit‚Äô and ‚Äòsupposition‚Äô now say in English to students of medieval philoso-
phy. Two centuries later, however‚Äîaround the time when Ockham died‚Äîthis
was no longer so: the technical terminology of supposition that Ockham inherited,
reÔ¨Åned and passed on to Wyclif, Pico and others was well established before 1350.
Not so in 1150: hence, if relations of Ôù≠, Ôù≤, and Ôù∑relata, as indicated then by Latin
suppo- words, are rendered now by ‚Äòsupposit,‚Äô ‚Äòsupposition‚Äô and so on in English,
the Anglicized Latin terms are not good translations: they‚Äôre transliterations of last
resort.
  When Ockham wrote that ‚Äúsupposition  is material when a term doesn‚Äôt
supposit as signifying but supposits for a spoken or written word,‚Äù he used


   ‚Å∏¬≥ Valente (2011), pp. 66‚Äì8, 74 argues for Gilbert‚Äôs ‚Äúpragmatic reformulation‚Äù of older linguistic
principles.

## Page 98

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  85


suppo- words to state a theory that did not yet exist when Gilbert used the very
same Latin words for related purposes.‚Å∏‚Å¥ This is why suppositum, suppositio and
their relatives‚Äîas words in texts written by Gilbert, Abelard, Peter Helias, and
their contemporaries‚Äîare not well translated now by transliterating. In our time,
when experts on language and logic use ‚Äòsupposition‚Äô to talk about achievements
of their own medieval ancestors, the inventors they usually have in mind are
Ockham, Buridan and  their not-too-distant predecessors‚Äîless remote than
Gilbert, Abelard, and Peter Helias. These  earlier innovators had also used
suppo- words, but rendering them with the terminology of supposition-theory
would be anachronistic and misleading.
  Without ‚Äòsupposition,‚Äô the available language is clumsy, to be sure: there is no
single alternative in English nor even just a few. Just in the semantic domain‚Äî
without regard to the double duty, metaphysical and semantic, done by suppo-
words‚Äîtheir nearest equivalents in Œì, Œî, Œò, ŒÝ, P, Q, S and other relations, in texts
from the twelfth century or before, include ‚Äòlisting,‚Äô ‚Äòpredicating,‚Äô ‚Äòproposing,‚Äô
‚Äòputting,‚Äô  ‚Äòreferring,‚Äô  ‚Äòreplacing,‚Äô  ‚Äòsignifying,‚Äô  ‚Äòswitching,‚Äô  and  others:  see
Tables 2.1 and 2.2. The English muddle is an artifact of the philosophical Latin
of Gilbert‚Äôs era‚Äîall the more credit to Ockham and his contemporaries for sorting
it out.
  The clarifying had been under way earlier, however, even before Peter of Spain
wrote about supposition and gave the new theory its Ô¨Årst effective vehicle‚Äîa
textbook with a large student readership that also caught the attention of special-
ists like Buridan. This very inÔ¨Çuential Peter was almost certainly not the Peter of
Spain who became Pope John XXI in 1276, but the faulty identiÔ¨Åcation (dating
from the late thirteenth century) is informative, suggesting that some of Peter‚Äôs
contemporaries (especially Dominicans) thought of him as a contemporary of
Thomas Aquinas, who died in 1274 after three decades of studying and teaching
theology.‚Å∏‚Åµ
   Peter of Spain‚Äôs crude theory may help us gauge Thomas‚Äôs awareness of
semantic suppositing. Although Thomas knew that suppo- words were terms of
art for logicians, his theology focused on metaphysical applications.‚Å∏‚Å∂ Both uses
came up in his writings, however. In a formidably abstract passage of the
Scriptum, he asked whether there were two hypostases, two supposita or two res
naturae in Christ: this last item‚Äîliterally a ‚Äúthing of a nature‚Äù‚Äîwas constituted
by a nature and could give a deÔ¨Ånite character (quidditas) to an item that had the
nature. To clarify this, Thomas applied yet another distinction between names of

## Page 99

86  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

Ô¨Årst imposition (names naming things) and names of second imposition (names
naming names of things), and he classiÔ¨Åed the phrase res naturae as


   a name of Ô¨Årst imposition, signifying a particular by referring to a shared nature,
   while the word suppositum is a name of second imposition, signifying just the
   belonging (habitudo) of a particular to a shared nature, inasmuch as the particu-
   lar stands within (subsistit) the nature, but signifying the particular inasmuch as
   the particular is exceeded (exceditur) by the nature . . . . The upshot, however, is
  not to say that there are two hypostases or supposita or things of a nature in
   Christ, for the human nature in Christ is not a thing of a nature nor a suppositum
   but the nature itself, in that no composite nature is a thing or a suppositum of
    itself, and likewise it is not a hypostasis but an essence (usia).


Having covered those bases, Thomas addressed the question about two supposita
in Christ and replied that ‚Äúthere is only one supposit in the manner just stated,
unless this was said to be a linguistic supposit (suppositum locutionis), because
in that way a supposit is about anything that can be said.‚Äù This was unusual:
a medieval user of the bivalent suppo- terminology calling attention to the
metaphysical/semantic distinction. Thomas‚Äôs point was to insist on the meta-
physical usage.‚Å∏‚Å∑
  When he took the same problem up in the great Summa, he showed once more
that he was aware of the semantic suppositum‚Äîbut without highlighting his
awareness. Again, he wanted to prove that (a) there was only one hypostasis in
Christ, not two, a divine and a human; but that (b) a union of human and divine
natures in Christ existed in this single hypostasis, which (c) was the basis (suppo-
situm) for taking the human nature on. To pick this mystery apart, Thomas
compared the absolutely singular divine suppositum in Christ with one of the
manifold human supposita that belonged to many individual persons‚Äîlike Christ:


  We say that this human reasons; is capable of laughing; and is a reasoning animal.
  And there is a reason why this human is said to be a suppositum: namely, that this
  one is the suppositum for whatever items belong to the human, accepting the
   predication of them. . . . It‚Äôs plainly a heresy long since condemned by the Church
   to claim that in Christ there are two hypostases or two supposita, or that the
  union did not take place in the hypostasis or suppositum.‚Å∏‚Å∏


A human person supposits for the human form in a founding F relation. The form
is a species: the genus Ôù°ÔùÆÔù©Ôù≠Ôù°Ôù¨plus the differences Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù°Ôù≥ÔùØÔùÆÔù©ÔùÆÔùß, Ôù≠ÔùØÔù≤Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¨, Ôù£Ôù°Ôù∞Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù¨Ôù•
ÔùØÔù¶Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùµÔùßÔù®Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß, and so on. This distinct species or form  is predicated of an

## Page 100

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  87


individual person, who is a substance with a suppositum. Used in this way of
humans, Thomas‚Äôs suppo- words expressed a metaphysical F relation of founding
that underwrote a semantic Œ¶ relation of predicating, and he used this conjunc-
tion of metaphysics with semantics to talk about God in analogous terms.
   If the human/divine analogy had been perfect, the absolutely unitary hypostasis
in Christ‚Äîwhich was a suppositum, according to Thomas‚Äîcould have been in an
F relation with many relata, as in humans. This real multiplicity of human
attributes is what Thomas‚Äôs language may have suggested by treating what is
predicated of humans as plural (eorum praedicatio). Between creatures and their
Creator, however, no analogy was perfect.
  In a created composite‚Äîlike an ensouled human body‚Äîthe suppositum was an
undivided whole that really differed from the creature‚Äôs nature. This nature‚Äîa
humanness or humanitas for human creatures‚Äîwas not directly predicated of the
human suppositum: we say homo est humanus but not homo est humanitas. And
this difference between a created suppositum (homo) and a created essence (also
humanitas) was a real distinction. In God, however, the uncreated divine essence
(divinitas) was not really distinct from Christ‚Äôs divine hypostasis or suppositum,
even though these could be distinguished in thought and by words, so that
divinitas indicated Christ‚Äôs godhood while the suppositum indicated Christ‚Äôs
subsistence.
  The divine/human disanalogy (by way of some additional metaphysics about
personae) persuaded Thomas that creaturely F relations of founding did not apply
to the Creator‚Äôs supposita. Part of his argument follows, locating a natura in a
species and starting with creatures, where


   the nature and the suppositum really differ, not as something entirely separate,
   but such that the very nature of the species is included in the suppositum . . . .
  Hence a suppositum is signiÔ¨Åed as a whole that has a nature as a formal part and
   perfective of  it. And on account of this, in composites of matter and form,
   a nature is not predicated of a suppositum, for we do not say that this human
    is his humanity. But  if there is something in which there is nothing else at
    all beyond the account (ratio) of the species or  its nature, as in God, the
   suppositum, in reality, is not anything other than the nature, merely in the way
   of understanding.‚Å∏‚Åπ

When Thomas said that ‚Äúa nature is not predicated of a suppositum,‚Äù a reader
aware of supposition theory might have thought that he was invoking the seman-
tics that Peter of Spain, his contemporary, had presented in his Summaries of
Logic. This would have been a misreading, however: the focus of suppositum in

this passage was metaphysical, in the founding F relation for creatures but in some
different relation for God‚Äîa grounding G relation, perhaps. Nonetheless, since
Thomas wrote that such a suppositum was ‚ÄúsigniÔ¨Åed‚Äù and also implied that, in

semantic implications. And yet Thomas‚Äôs appreciation of the semantic problems
and possibilities for suppositum was a long way from Ockham‚Äôs mature suppos-

English, ‚Äòbasis‚Äô or some similar choice for suppositum may be better than the
Latinate ‚Äòsupposit,‚Äô which will mystify many readers.
  Theologians after Thomas used metaphysical suppo- words along with ‚Äòbeing,‚Äô
‚Äòessence,‚Äô ‚Äòexistence,‚Äô ‚Äòhypostasis,‚Äô ‚Äònature,‚Äô ‚Äòperson,‚Äô ‚Äòsubject,‚Äô ‚Äòsubsistence,‚Äô and
‚Äòsubstance‚Äô to express key distinctions about the Trinity, incarnation and euchar-
ist. Scotus, a connoisseur of distinctions, was fond of suppo- words. In his
vocabulary, supposita were both created and uncreated. Created supposita where
the ordinary case, to which divine supposita were exalted exceptions.
  Supposita in general, both created and uncreated, were  real, self-standing
(subsistent) individuals, each with a particular nature. They were also ultimate
(as distinct from proximate) subjects of properties: they underlaid essences and
accidents but not natures. They were agents and, in fact, the only sources of action,
and if they were thinking agents, they were persons. Supposita were not parts, did
not inhere in anything and were not concepts, even though they were represented
by concepts. They could be relational or not, produced (caused) or not and
productive (causal) or not. As a contingent matter‚Äîhence not necessarily‚Äîthey
were not shared (communicated), but they gave existence to natures that could be
shared with other supposita.
  Divine supposita were uncreated, plural, individual persons, not produced, not
sharing or repeating the natures of other supposita and not constituted by relations.
All the divine supposita had one and the same subsistent essence from which they
were distinct and to which they did not give existence. They were agents‚Äî
individual persons‚Äîwhose essential actions were understanding and willing.‚Åπ‚Å∞
  Scotus applied his suppo- terminology to investigate God‚Äôs essential unity and
simplicity; this passage, where God the Father was the ‚ÄúFirst Suppositum,‚Äù was
typical:


   It does not seem intelligible that the supposita are many without making the
   essence many unless some distinction applies between the account (ratio) of an
   essence and the account of a suppositum, and . . . one must look to this distinc-
   tion. Hence  I say‚Äîwithout  insisting and  without  prejudice  to  a  better

Scotus showed what a supposit was not by contrasting it with an essence, and he
explained what a supposit was by way of one of its features‚Äîformal unshare-

  One must see just how this predicate homo is taken, and people say it is taken for
   the suppositum so that the predication is through identity, while suggesting that
   this  is implied (implicatio): the Word  is the suppositum, which  is a human
  (Verbum est suppositum, quod est homo).  I reject this since the discussion
  would then go to inÔ¨Ånity because the implication is on the side of the predicate
  and predicates a human of something brought in through the subject. And then
   I ask whether the predicate is taken formally, or else for the suppositum, when the
   statement is Verbum est suppositum, quod est homo. If the predicate is taken
   formally, the proposition holds because the predicating is formal and not just
  through identity. But if the predicate stands only for the implicated suppositum,
   the discussion will go to inÔ¨Ånity. Moreover, predicating a nature of the supposi-
  tum is formal, not just through identity.

Scotus unpacked g¬≤, ‚ÄúGod is a human,‚Äù as g¬≥, ‚Äúthe Word is a suppositum, which is a
human,‚Äù Verbum est suppositum, quod est homo. Since suppositum in g¬≥ stood for
a term in g¬≤, semantic complexity was front and center, as Scotus saw when he
analyzed the predicational form of g¬≤ and g¬≥. But the six suppo- words that he used
had no semantic burden: they were purely metaphysical.‚Åπ¬≤

  from  it (ab  illa)  formally. Note, however,  that  this  case  of  formality  is

  Evidence left by Aquinas in the great Summa marked the trail that Scotus chose
not to follow. Thomas examined g¬≤, Deus est homo, to refute opponents who
claimed that


  God does not supposit (ly Deus non supponit) the true and natural God. . . . Some
   grant this proposition, . . . yet they do not save the truth of the predication. For
   they say that a human is predicated of God by reason of a kind of conjoining
   either of rank or authority or even of affection or indwelling. . . . Those who put
  two supposits (supposita) or hypostases in Christ fall into the same error. In fact,
    if two items are distinct as either a supposit (secundum suppositum) or a
   hypostasis, it‚Äôs impossible to understand how one might be predicated of the
   other except by some sort of Ô¨Ågurative expression . . . . This proposition, God is a
  human, is true and correct, in my view, not just because the terms are correct‚Äî
   because Christ is truly God and truly human‚Äîbut because of the truth of the
   predication. For a noun signifying a shared nature concretely can supposit
   (supponere) for whatever is included in the shared nature, just as this noun
  human can supposit (supponere) for any particular (singularis) human.
    And so this word God, from the manner of its own signiÔ¨Åcation (modo suae
   signiÔ¨Åcationis), can supposit for (supponere pro) the person of the Son of God . . . .
  For a supposit (suppositum) of any nature, however, a noun which signiÔ¨Åes that
   nature in the concrete can be truly and correctly predicated‚Äîas a human is
   predicated truly and correctly of Socrates and Plato. Therefore, because the Son
   of God‚Äôs person, for which this noun God supposits (supponit), is the supposit
   (suppositum) of the human nature, a human can be predicated of the noun God
   in that a human supposits for (supponit pro) the Son of God‚Äôs person.‚Åπ‚Å¥

## Page 104

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  91


Like Scotus, Aquinas approached g¬≤ by studying it as a predication. Unlike Scotus,
however, he called on the semantic vocabulary of supposition to make his case.
Four of Thomas‚Äôs ten suppo- words are metaphysical, but six are semantic: he uses
both to say that ‚Äúthe Son of God‚Äôs person, for which this noun God supposits, is
the supposit of the human nature‚Äù; the verb is semantic but the noun is meta-
physical. And like many other philosophers, Thomas usually deployed these
polysemous words without noting the crucial differences in play. This vagueness
was a common response to suppo- words from the sixth century through the
Ô¨Åfteenth century.
  A robust suppo- terminology for the semantic Œ© relation of suppositing in
Table 2.1 was well in place by Ockham‚Äôs time, when he and others used these
words in a metaphysical way as well. They created a technical vocabulary for
semantics out of materials that had been taking shape since the days of Boethius
and Priscian, and during those eight centuries the same suppo- words had crucial
metaphysical applications.
  Where metaphysical (B, F, G, I, T) and semantic (Œò, Œû, Œ£, Œ¶, Œ®) uses converged
or coincided, how did speakers and listeners, writers and readers treat these
words? Were they thinking‚Äîas Table 2.3 suggests‚Äîof a superordinate relation
less general than Ôù∏RÔùπbut general enough to cover not only subordinate meta-
physical relations but also subordinate semantic relations? What might have done
the job was an Ôù∏PÔùπrelation of putting broad enough to include a transitive putting
under, an intransitive coming under, a passive being put under and also a static,
positional being under.
  Items related by the Ôù∏PÔùπputting relation are all around us. Things put under,
coming under and being under are bricks, babies, blankets, and many more‚Äî
everyday things usually unnoticed by philosophers. Not so for Ôù∏Œ¶Ôùπand the
relation of predication, which is distinctively philosophical and distinctly logico-
semantic. If Table 2.3 could be replaced by Table 2.4, substituting Ôù∏Œ¶Ôùπfor Ôù∏PÔùπ, the
tale of the suppo- words would be more satisfying for some philosophers. If a
higher-order predicating relation, Ôù∏Œ¶Ôùπ, were prior in this way to metaphysical
relations and all other semantic relations, then all metaphysical relations would
derive from or express the semantic predicating relation. Table 2.5 illustrates the
reverse possibility, where a higher-order taxonomical relation, Ôù∏TÔùπ, is prior to
semantic relations and  all other metaphysical relations, so that  all semantic
relations would derive from a metaphysical taxonomy. If these are nominalist
and realist positions, which is which?
  In any case, Aquinas, Ockham and others who applied the suppo- vocabulary to
metaphysical problems also used semantic suppo- words in the same contexts and
sometimes in the same passages. In a few relevant places, Scotus seems to have
been less explicit about the semantic side of the same metaphysical issues exam-
ined by Thomas and Ockham. And no one seems to have been troubled by the
great divide‚Äîmetaphysical/semantic‚Äîin the suppo- lexicon.

## Page 106

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  93

predicational Ôù∏Œ¶Ôùπor such a taxonomical Ôù∏TÔùπor some other suitable relation of
philosophical interest. For now, however, the evidence is best represented by
Table 2.3 and the Ôù∏PÔùπrelation of putting, coming and being under, which many
medieval philosophers found useful but was not distinctly philosophical.
  Evidence  for a happier conclusion might be  easier  to Ô¨Ånd  if  historical
circumstances‚Äîcounterfactually‚Äîhad  forced  Descartes, Hobbes, Locke, and
their allies to give suppositum and supponere the scrutiny given to substantia,
essentia, and qualitas. Plainly, the innovators cared little about suppo- words.
But why?
  For the semantic vocabulary, an answer is plain enough. The reformers paid
little attention to traditional Aristotelian logic‚Äîthe scholastic locus of suppos-
itional semantics‚Äîbecause it had been displaced, before they were born, by the
new logics of Peter Ramus and his predecessor, Rudolf Agricola. While the
innovators were innovating, semantic supposition theory was not prominent in
the re-engineered Aristotelian logic of Franco Burgersdijk, a contemporary of
Descartes. And in Hume‚Äôs lifetime, after the scientiÔ¨Åc revolutionaries were dead
and gone, Burgersdijk‚Äôs logic was still the standard in universities.‚Åπ‚Åµ
  A philosophically satisfying account of the fate of metaphysical suppo- words
is harder to come by, but non-philosophical evidence is incontestably relevant
and as clear as day. After the Catholic Descartes sabotaged his Church‚Äôs doctrines
of substantial forms and detachable accidents, his most celebrated successors
in  philosophical innovation were  either  Protestants or  else not  Christian:
Hobbes, Hume, Kant, Leibniz, Locke, and Spinoza. But both Descartes and
Hobbes were contemporaries of Luke Wadding and his team of Scotists who
reÔ¨Åned  the  nuances  of  metaphysical  supposita  for  Catholic  intellectuals.
Nonetheless, the doctrinal matrix of this metaphysics‚Äîthe trinitarian, eucharistic
and Christological dogma of the Roman Church‚Äîwas alien to Spinoza and
repugnant, more or less, to the others: even the clubbable Leibniz. Unsurprisingly,
the leading post-Cartesian philosophers mainly ignored suppositional metaphys-
ics along with the logical kind.
  The religious facts are plain enough, but religion should not constrain philo-
sophers in their philosophizing. Nonetheless, even in strictly philosophical terms,
suppositional metaphysics‚Äîunlike teachings about substance, essence, quality
and quantity‚Äîwas a bridge too far for scholastic theologians: even they had
declined to put yet more strain on their overwrought ontology by explicitly
deÔ¨Åning the associated semantics as cognate, yet distinct, and then giving a clear
account of how this might be so. In fact, they went the other way: as they kept
crossing the metaphysics/semantics divide without calling attention to  it, dog-
matic imperatives pushed them to multiply entities (supposita in addition to




   ‚Åπ‚Åµ Burgersdijk (1660), pp. 78‚Äì80; Copenhaver (2017), pp. 25‚Äì40.

  This overreaching about supposita differed from their handling of substance,
essence, quality and quantity in related but different circumstances. The latter
terminology, dear to medieval theologians, was also prized by medieval experts on
natural philosophy who said little about theology‚Äîeven when their views were
theologically consequential‚Äîand who could bypass disputes about  religious
dogma when they philosophized ex professo about nature. As for the theologians
themselves, they overdrew their explanatory accounts while inÔ¨Çating a currency‚Äî
suppositional metaphysics‚Äîlater abandoned as counterfeit by philosophers who
continued to trade in substance and essence.


                  2.10 Pico Against Humanism


In his Apology, not quite 56,000 words, Pico used many dozens of suppo- words,
even though, just a few months earlier, he had needed this terminology only Ô¨Åve
times in the 18,000 words of the Conclusions. Two of these Ô¨Åve uses were in
condemned theses: Q4 on the incarnation,


   I do not assent to the usual statement by theologians who say that God can make
  any nature whatever the supposit, granting this instead only for a nature able to
   reason (non assentior communi sententiae theologorum dicentium posse Deum
   quamlibet naturam suppositare, sed de rationabili tantum hoc concedo);


and Q6 on the eucharist,


    if one takes the usual approach to the possibility of suppositing with regard to
  any creature whatever, I say that, without the substance of bread changing into
   Christ‚Äôs body or without annihilating the breadness, it can happen that Christ‚Äôs
  body is on the altar in keeping with the reality of the sacrament, which would be a
   statement about possibility, not about being so. (si teneatur communis via de
   possibilitate suppositationis respectu cuiusque creaturae, dico quod, sine conver-
   sione substantiae panis in corpus Christi vel paneitatis annihilatione, Ô¨Åeri potest ut
   in altari  sit corpus Christi secundum veritatem sacramenti, quod  sit dictum
  loquendo de possibili non de sic esse).

Pico‚Äôs comment about ‚Äúsuppositing with regard to any creature whatever‚Äù‚Äî
including bread as well as human bodies‚Äîlinked the eucharistic theorizing in
Q6 with speculation in Q4 about another article of faith: the incarnation. The next
chapters of this book will examine the connection. But the tallies of suppo- words
in  the  prince‚Äôs two books  explain  themselves:  this medieval  terminology,

## Page 108

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°Ôù∑ÔùØÔù≤Ôù§?  95

incidental in the Conclusions, became indispensable for the Apology‚Äîafter the
Pope condemned the Q4, Q6, and other theses. Pico relied heavily on suppo-
words only when he needed them to defend himself.‚Åπ‚Å∂
   Pico‚Äôs use of suppo- words in these two propositions was plainly metaphysical,
like two other such cases in the Conclusions. Neither of these other propositions
was condemned, and one‚Äîbeyond the scope of this book‚Äîproclaimed that the
prince had transformed  Kabbalists  into  believing  Christians. ‚ÄúNo Hebrew
Kabbalist can deny,‚Äù he insisted,


   that the name Jesus, if we interpret it according to the method and principles of
   Kabbalah, signiÔ¨Åes precisely all of this and nothing else, as follows: God, Son of
  God and the Wisdom of the Father united through the third person of the Deity
  (who is the hottest Ô¨Åre of Love) to human nature in the unity of a supposit.

Three Hebrew letters in a sacred name, Yeshu (,)◊ï◊©◊ô  convinced Pico that Jews had
to accept the Trinity. In the Ô¨Ånal phrase of his thesis, naturae humanae in unitate
suppositi unitum, the noun suppositum expressed standard Christological meta-
physics and trinitarian theology. Elsewhere a shorter thesis applied the verb
suppositare  to a metaphysical conundrum debated by Aquinas and  other
scholastics:


  The three divine persons together could have supposited a single nature.‚Åπ‚Å∑

   logicum non pertinent).

  Did Pico dissent about logic in order to suggest that suppositional semantics

  The abundant scholastic terminology in the prince‚Äôs Conclusions, which were

that Rabelais would exploit as comedy.
  Before any of Pico‚Äôs writings was printed, some of his letters in the elegant
humanist style circulated and captivated their readers: two in particular were more

Greek philosophy, his only peers were Ficino and Poliziano, and he was ten years

diplomacy, and a Pico connection was timely for both purposes: the wealthy
prodigy was a cousin of the powerful Este family. From the shadows of his own
unassuming prose‚Äîso Barbaro insisted‚Äîhis letter labored to make Pico‚Äôs Latin
style gleam, while he encouraged and praised the young man for studying Greek.
Speaking of Greek, Barbaro observed gratuitously that in recent centuries no one
ignorant of that language had written good Latin, least of all barbarians north of
the Alps: too bad for students and teachers at Oxford and Paris.¬π‚Å∞‚Å∞

scholastic replied that he and his colleagues saw fame as a reward for successful
specialization: the glory they cared about was their own philosophical kind, where
literary and rhetorical standards were beside the point‚Äîat best. This was the heart
of Pico‚Äôs message: experience had convinced him that philosophers were entitled
to talk as they wished to each other‚Äîand to the world‚Äîin order to achieve their
own special purposes.
  Unlike rhetoric, whose goal was to persuade, philosophy wanted the truth. For
this purpose, displays of oratory were worse than useless‚Äîat least distracting and
at worst destructive. Granted: orators and philosophers both needed words, which
got their meanings by convention or naturally. But  if meanings were natural,
philosophers were in charge because they‚Äînot orators‚Äîwere the experts on
nature. And if meanings were conventional, philosophers could settle their own
conventions‚Äîjust like everyone else.
  As the letter went on, the comparisons got worse for orators: their rhetoric was
morally wrong because  it aimed to deceive, whereas honest philosophers just
wanted to make things clear. Accordingly, the best philosophical style was like
God‚Äôs speech in sacred scripture: serious and simple, not Ô¨Çashy and ornate,
‚Äúgrandeur in rough speech rather than graceful delicacy.‚Äù Barbarians worshipped

                          3.1 Incarnation

The Ô¨Årst chapter of John‚Äôs Gospel told early Christians a sacred story‚Äîfrom
creation to salvation. ‚ÄúIn the beginning was the Word,‚Äù and then ‚Äúthe Word
was made Ô¨Çesh.‚Äù Believers also formulated creeds‚Äîshort prayers to declare their
faith in a divine Trinity. Nine Latin words were enough in one such prayer to
pledge trust in a Father and Creator, and only seven were needed for a Holy Spirit.
But a Son who was born, died, and rose from the dead required Ô¨Åfty-one words.¬π
   John‚Äôs Gospel was a book of the Bible‚ÄîGod‚Äôs words, hence indisputably true.
But early Christians who revered the Gospels and recited creeds were soon
debating  about  their  one God who  was  also  three  persons.  Distinct
‚ÄòChristological‚Äô issues arose for the second person, God‚Äôs Son. Gospel stories
and creedal statements spoke about the Son in ungodlike, human terms: he was
born to a woman, suffered, died, and was buried before a miraculous resurrection.
Coming back from the dead was strange, but a destructible Deity was even
stranger. How  could  a  Creator  of  the  universe‚Äîeternal,  changeless, and
bodiless‚Äîfeel pain and perish like a mortal and yet be divine?
  Learned people and Church ofÔ¨Åcials asked such questions in sermons, letters,
and books that survived through the Middle Ages. Meanwhile, as Europe‚Äôs Latin
West and Greek East drifted into conÔ¨Çict, this ‚Äòpatristic‚Äô theology reÔ¨Çected deep-
ening cultural divisions. Greek thinkers attacked other Greeks, while Jerome,
Augustine, Gregory the Great, and a few others dominated Latin conversations
through the early seventh century. These Western Fathers fueled controversies
among later scholars who could not read Greek. The last Greek theologian studied
in the Middle Ages by Latin authors was John of Damascus, a Syrian monk whose
monastery was near Jerusalem. John died around 750, and four centuries later an
Italian translated his works into Latin‚Äîin time for Peter Lombard to use them.¬≤
  John wrote poems, sermons and defenses of orthodox theology. In early
contests about Christian faith, orthodoxy was a label devised by winners, who
called the losers ‚Äòheretics‚Äô: Arians, Manichees, Monophysites, Nestorians, and

of persons and in Christ‚Äôs two natures.¬π‚Å∞

   to suitability for the aforesaid uniting. This suitability depends on two features of
   a human nature‚Äîrank and need. As for rank: this is because a human nature,

When taking-on was the question, Thomas varied his answers, adjusting them to
the occasion. In the passage from the Summa, God‚Äôs absolute power, already a
much discussed topic, was not at issue. Later‚Äîafter Scotus, Ockham, Nicole

God could take on a stone, and his answer was yes:


   Just as God‚Äôs likeness (similitudo) exists in a human nature in the pattern of his
  image (ratione imaginis), so God‚Äôs likeness exists in an unreasoning creature in a
   vestigial pattern . . . . Therefore, just as a human nature can be united with God, so
  can an unreasoning creature.¬π‚Åπ


He tested this position carefully against the views of Albert, Bernard of Gannat,
Durand, Godfrey of Fontaines, Henry of Ghent, John of Naples, and Scotus. They
and he used many suppo- words, including the unusual suppositare, a key term in
Pico‚Äôs Q4 thesis: this and other suppo- words occur twenty-seven times in Q4.
Both for the title of Q4 and for the Q4 thesis, the prince, who read Capreolus,
chose suppositare‚Äînot supponere, the usual verb. He came back to suppositare
eighteen times in Q4, where other unusual usages are suppositabilis (twice) and
suppositatio (once).¬≤‚Å∞

  Where did this exotic vocabulary come from? Since the Latin is medieval, one
place to look is the beginning of the Conclusions, where Pico listed 115 proposi-

in a thesis given to Aquinas:

   Potuerunt tres personae divinae simul suppositare unam naturam.¬≤¬π

Everyone agreed that there are three supposits in the Trinity, not one just one‚Äîa
single supposit for each divine person. One supposit belongs to the second person,
the Son, who has two natures, divine and human. But the Ô¨Årst person, the Father,
has only a divine nature, like the third person, the Holy Spirit. God is all three
persons, however, who were able together to make the one and only divine nature
a single supposit in two cases‚Äîfor the Father and the Holy Spirit. If this was the
point of Pico‚Äôs proposition about the three persons, it was nothing new. Likewise
another thesis assigned to Aquinas:


  Designated matter is the principle of individuation.¬≤¬≤


This axiom of Thomist metaphysics (more later on its meaning) was well known
and widely accepted, though agreement about it was far from unanimous. One
energetic critic was Henry of Ghent, who‚Äîalong with Aquinas‚Äîwas one of the
six theologians headlined at the start of the Conclusions. But Pico never mentioned
Henry in the twenty-nine propositions, including the Q4 thesis, that especially
provoked his enemies in Rome. The only medieval Ô¨Ågure mentioned in these
conclusions was Aquinas‚Äîboth times to correct him.¬≤¬≥
  What were Pico‚Äôs credentials as a critic of theology? Except for his writings, the
only evidence of his education comes from the biography by his nephew, supple-

he studied in Padua, Paris and elsewhere. The Conclusions and Apology were
shaped by Pico‚Äôs university experience, but the relevant propositions in the
Conclusions show no special interest in suppo- words. All in all, the prince left
few clues in this earlier work for readers puzzled by suppositare in the Q4 thesis.

The renderings of suppositare are ‚Äúassume,‚Äù ‚Äúassume as a form,‚Äù ‚Äúbe the substan-

   Since Henry of Ghent‚Äôs presence is the largest in Q4‚Äîeven larger than Pico‚Äôs‚Äî
maybe Henry could help. Like other theologians of his day, he proposed intricate
philosophical solutions to knotty doctrinal problems. One puzzle, the eucharist,
was part of every Christian‚Äôs experience. Henry was a priest and often saw people
eating Christ‚Äôs body after he did the same: he was a minister of the blessed
sacrament that Jesus established on the day before he died. On the day after, the
Lord‚Äôs body was in the tomb. The sacrament, already instituted, could have been
celebrated during this mournful time. But if a celebrant were to have said ‚Äúthis is
my body,‚Äù and then bread changed into the sacred body, could the body also have
stayed in the tomb? And during the three days after the cruciÔ¨Åxion, the soul of
Jesus visited Hell while his body was buried. During this period, the triduum,
could the soul in Hell also have been on an altar where the Lord was present in
‚Äúbody and blood, soul and divinity‚Äù?¬≤‚Åµ
  Three doctrines especially caused interminable debate among Henry and his
opponents‚Äînot just Christ‚Äôs real presence in the eucharist but also the Trinity of
three persons in one God and Christ‚Äôs two natures in one person. Henry excelled
in these controversies and proposed original answers to three intertwined ques-
tions about the principle of individuation; about the human soul as the form of the
body; and about the distinction between essence and existence.¬≤‚Å∂
  Henry took part in the Parisian condemnations of 1277 that attacked Aquinas
and other Aristotelians on such points. Unlike Thomas before him and Scotus
after him, he was not a member of a religious order. But theology was his subject.

                    3.2 Assenting and Asserting

As Pico opened his Q4 defense, he divided the Q4 thesis into a ‚Äúconceding‚Äù and a
‚Äúrefusing‚Äù part. A Ô¨Åner division (indicated by my tags) might have been better,
though none of the Ô¨Åve pieces would have been easy:


   I (Ôù°) do not assent to (Ôù¢) the usual statement by theologians who say (Ôù£) that God
  can make any nature whatever the supposit, (Ôù§) granting this instead only for (Ôù•)
   a nature able to reason.¬≥¬≤

The refusing part is Ôù°+ Ôù¢+ Ôù£, where Pico identiÔ¨Åes his opponents as theologians
and declines to accept what they propose; next comes the conceding part, Ôù§+ Ôù•,
which accepts what Pico does not oppose. Part Ôù£, if it is true, states a fact about the
Almighty, which Pico does not deny. He only refuses assent to Ôù¢and Ôù£together,
where Ôù£is an assertion made by theologians about God, not a fact about God.
Here, at the beginning of Q4, Pico‚Äôs assenting/asserting distinction makes a big
difference, but he does not say why until near the end of Q4. Even there, however,
while restating a lengthy argument whose burden has been metaphysical, he
passes over deeper epistemic questions involving assent, deferring them to Q8.¬≥¬≥
  Meanwhile, he insists that refusing assent to Ôù¢+ Ôù£does not‚Äî‚Äúin virtue of the
language,‚Äù de virtute sermonis, because of the literal meaning of the words used‚Äî
detract from God‚Äôs omnipotence; this is because his refusal, although it withholds
assent, has been carefully worded to assert neither x nor not-x:

  x ‚ÄòGod can do as Ôù£says‚Äô;

  not-x ‚ÄòGod can‚Äôt do as Ôù£says.‚Äô

## Page 124

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù°ÔùÆÔù¢Ôù•Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•ÔùÆÔùØÔùÆ?  111


If God exists, either x is true or not-x is true but not both, and informed opinion in
Pico‚Äôs day was on the side of x and God‚Äôs absolute power. But the prince
recognized no obligation to assent explicitly to every manifestation‚Äîcountlessly
many‚Äîof divine omnipotence, even though this doctrine was a rock-solid article
of faith. Pico thought that the faithful were bound only to believe general state-
ments like ‚ÄòGod is all powerful‚Äô and also not to disbelieve them.¬≥‚Å¥
  In this loose framework, he was careful not to write ‚ÄòI assert‚Äô not-x, which would
have been his own statement, not a fact about God. Instead, he wrote ‚ÄúI do not
assent to‚Äù x, a different statement and likewise not a fact about God. He also
claimed that not-x could not be inferred from his not assenting to x. And he was
right: refusing to accept a proposition doesn‚Äôt entail its contradiction.¬≥‚Åµ
  But then the prince apparently contradicted himself. He had just said that when
he wrote the Q4 thesis he was careful only to refuse assent to its Ôù¢and Ôù£parts.
Then he announced that (i) he personally (in me) did not refuse assent to Ôù¢+ Ôù£
even though (ii) this was allowed because he was ‚Äúnot obliged to believe the
opposite explicitly (oppositum . . . explicite credere), even if it were true, since this
could still be unknown to me as explicit and in particular.‚Äù The opposite of what?
Of x or not-x or something else? Either Pico‚Äôs writing was sloppy or the text is
Ô¨Çawed, and both possibilities Ô¨Åt the circumstances: a panicky document rushed to
completion within three weeks. In any event, Pico claimed that information
bearing on his obligation to believe might not be available to him in a sufÔ¨Åciently
explicit form.¬≥‚Å∂
  Then he added that ‚ÄòGod can‚Äôt‚Äô statements‚Äîlike not-x‚Äîwere hard to handle
and might disparage the Almighty‚Äôs power. This was why asserting not-x would

   Pico‚Äôs reasoning inverts a rhetorical device that philosophers classiÔ¨Åed as a
fallacy‚Äîlike arguing ad hominem, by attacking someone personally; or ad bacu-
lum, by threatening harm; or ad verecundiam, by citing an irrelevant authority.
Henry‚Äôs authority, by contrast, was plainly relevant to the Q4 thesis, as Q4 would
show at length. But Ô¨Årst Pico set a rhetorical trap by redesigning a sophistical
maneuver: tu quoque.
  In its standard form, this ‚Äòyou too‚Äô ploy arms an accused person by disarming
an accuser: how dare you accuse me of what you have done too? You also; tu
quoque! Pico re-engineered tu quoque and put his judges on notice: how could
they possibly accuse him of what they, the most respected theologians, had done
too? They also: illi quoque! Pico‚Äôs rhetoric did not expose ‚Äòa pot calling the kettle
black,‚Äô as the proverb says. Instead, in the role of the accused, the prince put on a
hero‚Äôs shining armor‚Äîthe trappings of Henry of Ghent‚Äîwhose feats of theo-
logical combat validated his own deeds because they were the same.¬≥‚Åπ
  Pico meant his rhetorical gesture about Henry to cover the whole Apology and
immunize  all the condemned theses: case by case, the prince implied, views
contested by his propositions caused eminent authorities to change their minds
and reverse themselves. But he lost touch with this persuasive evidence in Q4‚Äôs
conclusion, after summing up Henry‚Äôs position at Q4.24.
   After 1900 words‚Äîmore than half of this document‚Äôs 3500 words‚Äîspent on
Henry‚Äôs exonerating revision, Pico‚Äôs analysis of the theologian‚Äôs metaphysics
highlighted not the  illi quoque gambit but the less theatrical contrast between
asserting and assenting. This distinction was crucial for the Q4 thesis which, in
virtue of its language, refuses ‚Äúassent.‚Äù But no other condemned thesis makes a
Ô¨Årst-person, non assentior statement. Most theses defended by the Apology are just
bare doctrine, like the Q13 proposition, a third-person declaration with no Ô¨Årst-
person voice:


  The soul understands nothing actually and distinctly except itself.


There is nothing in this thesis about doubting, supposing or believing the content
(k) of the thesis‚Äîwhat it says. The thesis has propositional content, which is k, but
no explicitly doxastic content‚Äînothing to afÔ¨Årm or deny that-k. And when Pico
had this proposition printed in the Conclusions‚Äîas a statement offered for
debate‚Äîhe took no epistemic responsibility for doubting or supposing or believ-
ing that-k. The Q13 and Q4 theses differ conspicuously because only the latter
advertises its doxastic content, which Pico framed as not assenting (non assentior):

  Most theses defended in the Apology are like the Q13 proposition. They make

defense, the prince enlisted Henry of Ghent as an ally, knowing that Henry had
opposed Thomas Aquinas in the condemnations of 1277.


                 3.3 Henry of Ghent‚Äôs Metaphysics


Aquinas and his followers were devout Christian supernaturalists who went some
distance with Aristotle‚Äôs naturalism. They classiÔ¨Åed all of creation, at the highest
level of generality, as either immaterial or material, so that all of it was either
material and composite, like humans and frogs, or else immaterial and non-
composite, like angels. Henry‚Äôs outlook was less naturalist and more biblical. He
divided all being, both material and immaterial, composite and non-composite,
into the uncreated (the Creator) and the created (everything else). He replaced the
basic separation of matter from non-matter in Aristotelian metaphysics with a
different partition between creatures and the Creator.‚Å¥¬π
  From either point of view, Henry‚Äôs or Thomas‚Äôs, what makes one creature
different from another‚ÄîPlato rather than Socrates‚Äîin a deep, lasting way?
Aristotelians like Aquinas treated humans as matter/form and body/soul com-
posites that differ from one another in many ways: longer/shorter, wider/nar-
rower, heavier/lighter, and so on. But such accidental features are transient‚Äînot
deep, lasting differences. So what about form? Since Socrates and Plato are both
human, the form or essence or nature of humanness can‚Äôt distinguish them.
Matter is an alternative‚Äînot matter in general, since all humans are made of it,
but some parcel of matter. What makes Plato distinct from Socrates as an
individual‚Äîunitary and self-standing‚Äîis Plato‚Äôs batch of matter as apart from
his teacher‚Äôs. Matter quantiÔ¨Åed in this way is the principle of individuation,
according to Thomas.‚Å¥¬≤
  Henry disagreed because he thought that angels, as pure spirits, have no matter
to individuate them, and yet they act as individuals in the Bible. But angels‚Äîand

                    3.4 Henry‚Äôs Quodlibet 13.5


Henry divided his thirteenth Quodlibet into seventeen questions, some comparing
the Creator with creatures, others dealing mainly with creatures. Three of the Ô¨Årst
group discuss God‚Äôs power. One question asks  if the Creator can put a body
outside the universe, beyond the highest heavens. Another puzzles about Christ‚Äôs
soul during the three days: when it was separated from the body in the tomb, could
the soul have been joined, on an altar or elsewhere, with the same body? Pico
found a defense of his Q4 thesis in Henry‚Äôs third question about God‚Äôs power:
whether the Almighty ‚Äúcould have taken on (assumere) a non-intellectual sub-
stance in the unity of a person.‚Äù This person, the Son, is the second of three in the
Trinity, and the issue at hand also involves the Son‚Äôs two natures, divine and
human.‚Åµ¬≤
  The singular, concrete human nature belonging only to Jesus has been taken on
(assumptus) by God in the miracle of the incarnation (Figure 3.1): medieval
Christians respected this mystery, which did not prevent Henry and other philo-
sophers from trying to clarify it.
  One approach was to ask whether God could have taken on a nature of some
other kind: the nature of a stone, a plant or an unreasoning animal. Divine power
was not in doubt, however. According to Henry, only created natures were in
question, not the Creator. He recognized two answers, noting that he no longer
favored the more accepted one: that the Almighty can take on any creature‚Äôs
nature.‚Åµ¬≥
  What dislodged Henry from this standard position was a worry about act and
potency, two principles of Aristotelian metaphysics. A hot brick can grow cold: a
brick that is now actually hot is also potentially cold‚Äîhot ‚Äúin act‚Äù but simultan-
eously cold ‚Äúin potency.‚Äù In countless complementary ways, all creatures are both
in act and in potency.‚Åµ‚Å¥ If some actual creature‚Äôs nature‚Äîa tree‚Äôs, for example‚Äî
were taken on by the Creator, it would be swept up to the highest heavens. And
since such a nature would be real, its existence would be in act. Moreover, because
blessed souls also actually exist with God in heaven, it seems that an existence can
be raised up. But what about a tree‚Äôs potencies? A tree that exists in act is also in

  A soul is born in its body only to ensoul (animare) it. Hence, if the soul were put

Henry‚Äôs response turned on distinctions about ways of being:

   I don‚Äôt see why Christ‚Äôs soul during the three days could not have been in its body

To settle this dispute, Henry concluded that ‚Äúno nature can be received by God in
the way described, by co-envelopment, unless it is an intellectual nature.‚Äù Such a

subordinating suppresses accidental, non-essential Ô¨Çaws in the body that might
scar the soul even when separated. However, because the soul masters the body
and mediates for it, the body/soul composite, ‚Äúwith its own intellectual strength,‚Äù
can be taken on as a whole by God. This is why the human soul‚Äôs faculty of reason
supplies the entire body/soul composite with the required Ôù°Ôù≥¬≤ potency‚Äîthe
intellectual capability that a human nature needs in order to stand on its own in
a divine supposit by an Ôù°Ôù≥¬π act.‚Å∂‚Å∂

  Uniting in this way with divinity itself is possible only for reasoning creatures
and only by co-envelopment. To spell these restrictions out, Henry used the
ancient imagery of wax and a seal in a confusing way, even though the customary
purpose of the images was to clarify abstractions by making them concrete. When
Augustine, in his authoritative book on the Trinity, wrote that ‚Äúan image (imago)
goes from a ring into wax without leaving the ring,‚Äù he was trying to Ô¨Åt physical
pieces into a metaphysical puzzle about copies of a model and the single form
shared by them all.‚Å∂‚Å∑
  Henry‚Äôs analog for the model/copy/form schema‚Äîoriginal (prototypus), image
(imago), and pattern (ratio)‚Äîwas just as abstract. But anyone could visualize a
seal (sigillum) imprinting wax with the pattern (ratio) of an image (imago) carved
into the seal. The imprinted material might be metal instead of wax, and designs
(Ô¨Ågurae) on metal coins struck from a die were everyday sights. Henry erected a
metaphysics on this foundation of ordinary objects:

    model        copy           form
     original       image            pattern
     prototypus    imago             ratio
     seal            sealed wax       pattern
     die          stamped coin     design


The triune Creator is the original (prototypus) of the triadic divine image (imago)
created in Adam with a pattern (ratio) multiplied in the psychological faculties of
Adam‚Äôs heirs. Henry took his proof-texts for this doctrine from the Ô¨Årst chapter of
Genesis (‚Äúin our image and likeness‚Äù), whose story was reÔ¨Çected in the Ô¨Årst
chapter of John‚Äôs Gospel (‚Äúthe Word was made Ô¨Çesh‚Äù), along with comments by
John of Damascus on the image in Adam. By inserting the Damascene‚Äôs remarks
between two different applications of the seal/wax and die/coin metaphors, Henry
put his pictures out of focus.‚Å∂‚Å∏
  Before citing John, Henry had established that the intellectual Ôù°Ôù≥¬≤ potency
needed for an Ôù°Ôù≥¬π act of divine subsistence is complete for the pattern (ratio) of
God‚Äôs image (imago) created in Adam. Nothing in which this pattern was incom-
plete, he maintained, could be accepted by God and taken on. Although the
pattern is complete in all humans, after Adam the image was not impressed
from its divine original (prototypus), just as the design (Ô¨Ågura) on a coin can
transfer the pattern of a coined image elsewhere without the original die that
stamped the coin.‚Å∂‚Åπ

                         3.5 Pico‚Äôs Henry

Pico presented this long excerpt from Henry‚Äôs Qdl. 13.5 as evidence that the
verdict against his own Q4 thesis was mistaken‚Äîthat the Pope‚Äôs commission
was wrong to rule that his thesis, because of its language, was suggestive of heresy.
Pico objected that he had only declined to assent, as a matter of academic debate,
to a statement commonly made by theologians, ‚Äúthat God can make any nature
whatever the supposit,‚Äù claiming instead that this suppositing is possible ‚Äúonly for

  Maybe the experts who saw nothing wrong with Henry‚Äôs position were mis-

wrote commentaries on the standard textbook of theology, Peter Lombard‚Äôs
Sentences.

stick or a donkey into a divine supposit‚Äîan avatar of his own Ôù¢S being. In that
case, would custom or common sense or decent restraint inhibit the fantasies of

against him.‚Åπ¬≤

outright. He focused on a reply to the court where he said ‚ÄúI follow Henry in
this,‚Äù pointing out that ‚Äúthis‚Äù was the Q4 thesis exactly as stated, a refusal of
assent. By following Henry in order to justify withholding assent‚Äînot in order
to make an assertion‚Äîhe did not assert ‚Äúthe view that says God cannot make any
nature whatever the supposit.‚Äù Since all competent theologians knew that not
assenting to r was different from asserting not-r, his judges were either incompetent
or malevolent‚Äîor so the prince implied.‚Åπ‚Åµ
  Their second error was to forget Henry of Ghent‚Äôs stature as a theologian. This
distinguished clergyman, at the height of his theological powers, changed his mind
and defended a position that he had opposed when he was less experienced‚Äîthat
‚Äúnot any nature can be made the supposit by God.‚Äù The view that Henry
abandoned was the very same position from which Pico withheld assent in the
Q4 thesis‚Äîcondemned as suggesting heresy. Did the Pope‚Äôs commission believe
that Henry, ‚Äúthe one called the Stately Doctor because of the excellence of his
teaching,‚Äù was Pico‚Äôs partner in crime?‚Åπ‚Å∂
   Third, Pico aimed another barrage at the court‚Äôs bungled proceedings and bad
faith. Either his judges were right to condemn the Q4 thesis or they were wrong. If
they were wrong, the prince was not guilty as charged, and their prosecution was
inept. But if they were right, meaning that God‚Äôs omnipotence had been insulted,
he was guilty of heresy of the worst kind, and the Q4 thesis plainly did more than
just suggest it: again, not guilty as charged by blundering judges. They lost both
ways: they were either wrong and incompetent or right and negligent.‚Åπ‚Å∑
  Fourth and Ô¨Ånally, the court disgraced itself by Ô¨Ånding against the Q4 thesis de
virtute sermonis‚Äî‚Äúin virtue of  its language.‚Äù Even  if Henry‚Äôs position were
absolutely and incontrovertibly heretical (strange for someone of his reputation),
Pico‚Äôs thesis‚Äîjust because of how he stated it, precisely in virtue of its language‚Äî
would not likewise be heretical. This, Pico maintained, was because the Q4 thesis
‚Äúdoes not entail (non infert)‚Äù what Henry said.‚Åπ‚Å∏
  In this concluding part of Q4, the prince had no need to go over the asserting/
assenting distinction again after stating and restating it so often. But the words de
virtute sermonis, used three times in this short Ô¨Ånal paragraph, have the same
effect. As a forensic weapon used by Pico‚Äôs enemies, this phrase‚Äîthe equivalent of
vi sermonis in the court‚Äôs verdict‚Äîaimed straight at the terminology of the Q4
thesis:


  Non assentior communi sententiae theologorum dicentium posse Deum quamli-
   bet naturam suppositare, sed de rationabili tantum hoc concedo.



   ‚Åπ‚Åµ Pico, Q4.36 (Apo. p. 47; Afr., pp. 150‚Äì2); Kennedy (1983), pp. 155‚Äì6, 160.
   ‚Åπ‚Å∂ Pico, Q4.37 (Apo. p. 47; Afr., p. 152).         ‚Åπ‚Å∑ Pico, Q4.38 (Apo. p. 47; Afr., p. 152).
   ‚Åπ‚Å∏ Pico, Q4.39 (Apo. p. 47; Afr., p. 152).

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù°ÔùÆÔù¢Ôù•Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•ÔùÆÔùØÔùÆ?  135

In theological debate, any of these seventeen words‚Äîeven the harmless sed (‚Äúbut‚Äù)
or the innocuous de (‚Äúabout‚Äù)‚Äîmight be scrutinized de virtute sermonis. Likelier
targets, however, were the nine italicized words whose narrower lexical range
might provoke a theologian. Five of these‚ÄîDeus (‚ÄúGod‚Äù), natura (‚Äúnature‚Äù), posse
(‚Äúcan‚Äù), rationabilis (‚Äúreasoning‚Äù), suppositare (‚Äúmake a supposit‚Äù)‚Äîare key
terms in the metaphysics of Q4. But here, at Q4.39, Pico had already rested his
metaphysical case after Q4.31 in order to make (and score) rhetorical, logical and
juridical points. From his own tactical point of view, the decisive words in the
Q4 thesis were the two Ô¨Årst-person verbs‚Äîassentior (‚ÄúI assent‚Äù) and concedo
(‚ÄúI grant‚Äù)‚Äîand the Ô¨Årst was the controlling term. Q4 ended as it began, with
Pico looking for cover under the word ‚Äúassent.‚Äù
  When Pico wrote the original version of the Q4 thesis, his project was not yet
the Apology; his task at the time was the 900 Conclusions. But even in this very
different situation, fueled by conÔ¨Ådence and not yet charged with heresy, he
reduced his exposure with the words he picked to begin his thesis: non assentior,
‚ÄúI do not assent.‚Äù He could have written non credo, ‚ÄúI do not believe,‚Äù but‚Äîfor
once‚Äîcaution got the best of him. Had he asserted disbelief, he would have given
his enemies more to shoot at, and his defense in Q4 would have had less basis in
the Q4 thesis.‚Åπ‚Åπ
  Pico had no thought of needing an Apology when he Ô¨Årst wrote the troublesome
Q4 proposition for the Conclusions. The chronology of his works shows that he
did not devise the crucial assenting/asserting distinction‚Äîstated at the beginning
of Q4 and repeated later‚Äîjust to give himself cover under Ô¨Åre. The Q4 thesis and
its defense in Q4 are authentic statements by a skilled interpreter‚Äîeducated in the
scholastic tradition‚Äîof the mystery of the incarnation, a sacred  article  of
Christian faith. The following is what Pico wrote in his Apology.¬π‚Å∞‚Å∞


                    3.6 Pico‚Äôs Fourth Question
        Whether An Unreasoning Nature Can Be Made
                  The Supposit by God

1 The fourth conclusion condemned by them is this:


   I do not assent to the usual statement by theologians who say that God can make
  any nature whatever the supposit, granting this instead only for a nature able to
   reason.





    ‚Åπ‚Åπ Pico, Q4.1 (Apo. p. 41; Afr., p. 134); Dorez and Thuasne (1897), pp. 124, 129.

136  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨


By this conclusion I do not mean to deny that God can make any nature whatever
the supposit. I mean only to refuse particular and speciÔ¨Åc assent to this assertion:
God can make any nature whatever the supposit. And there is a great difference
between these two meanings because even if this were a matter of possibilities
according to God‚Äôs absolute power, still, in virtue of the language, merely refusing
positive, explicit assent to this statement in particular does not, in virtue of the
language, detract from God‚Äôs omnipotence nor, in virtue of the language, suggest
any heresy. The reason  is that not every faithful Christian  is held to assent
explicitly and in particular to all possibilities regarding God‚Äôs absolute omnipo-
tence or to profess them explicitly and in particular: it sufÔ¨Åces to believe such
things in general and implicitly and to disbelieve none of them obstinately.
  2 For this reason, I was careful not to state the conclusion in these terms, God
cannot make any nature whatever the supposit, but in these, I do not assent to the
statement which says that God can make any nature whatever the supposit. It
cannot be inferred from this conclusion‚Äîin virtue of its language‚Äîthat God
cannot do this, nor can it be said that these claims are asserted by me in the
conclusion.
  3 In fact, neither in the Ô¨Årst refusing part nor in the second‚Äîfrom which it
follows merely that only the point made distinctly and in particular about a
rational nature is treated as conceding‚Äîdo I refuse, speaking for myself, particular
and explicit assent to the assertion that God can make any nature whatever the
supposit. And I can refuse this reasonably, both because it is clear that I am not
obliged to believe the opposite explicitly, even if it were true, since this could still
be unknown to me as explicit and in particular, and especially because it is not
easy to disprove or prove the assertion that God cannot and so on, thus detracting
from God‚Äôs omnipotence.
  4 The best proof of this comes on the authority of Henry of Ghent, the Stately
Doctor, who, after holding the opposite view‚Äîthat any nature could be taken
on‚Äîthen turned away from this toward the view that only a reasoning nature can
be taken on. This is what Henry writes:
    5 Regarding the possibility of taking a nature on, since there is no doubt
  about God‚Äôs power to take on any nature at all that can be taken on, then the
  whole difÔ¨Åculty in this question turns on the possibility for a nature to be taken
  on, and there are two established views about this. Now the more widespread
  and better established view, which I used to support somewhat, states that any
  nature subsisting in creatures can be taken on. . . . And such people hold that
  some nature may be raised up whose corresponding potency may not be raised
  up‚Äîa position that seems to me impossible to take in a reasonable way.
    6 To understand this, note that God has being in creatures in two ways: in
  one way naturally and in a manner suiting the nature; in another way by willing
  and in a manner surpassing the nature‚Äôs limits. In the Ô¨Årst way God has being
  by inÔ¨Çowing in every nature and creature. In the second way he has being only
   in a nature taken on by co-envelopment.

  7 And although God is close within every creature by inÔ¨Çowing‚Äîcloser than

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù°ÔùÆÔù¢Ôù•Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•ÔùÆÔùØÔùÆ?  141

  cannot be any Ô¨Ånal cause‚Äîfor whose sake the taking on would be done by

  purpose in taking a nature on except somehow to advance and complete its
  being raised up, which can happen only for an intellectual nature.
This is Henry‚Äôs statement, almost to the word.
  24  It was not correctly decided, then, that my conclusion, in virtue of its
language, suggests heresy: my thesis only refuses positive assent, just as Henry
speaks in opposition, and I have never read a condemnation of his statement, nor
do I believe that these people have ever read one. I know this because neither
Scotus nor Godfrey nor John of Ware nor Durand nor Thomas the Englishman
nor Robert of Collotorto nor Bernard de Gannat,  all of whom I have seen
repeating this very view of Henry‚Äôs, has ever judged it heretical or suggestive of
heresy. But they too might seem to have done this without good reason, so I want
to give a brief account of how we see Henry defending his position.
  25 Against Henry, then, there are two main arguments. The Ô¨Årst is based on the
fact that every pattern for producing a substance is in God because he is the
fullness of all being, so evidently he can make any nature the substance and the
supposit. The second argument is that during the three days God made the body
that was in the tomb a supposit, and then, just as he made this body a supposit
during the three days even though it was non-reasoning, he could have made it a
supposit for whatever time he wanted. These are the two main grounds for
opposing Henry‚Äôs view, and‚Äîas seen when he states his view‚ÄîHenry cites them
against himself.
  26 As we see, he replies to the Ô¨Årst argument that the fullness of the whole of
existence truly is in God, where every pattern for making a substance and a
supposit may be found. But this is not the total cause of any nature‚Äôs ability to
be taken on, and yet it is a co-cause. For unless this were so, the one who takes on
could not take on. But in the taking on, something beyond this is needed for what
can be taken on, and this is the pattern of an image and, as a Ô¨Ånal goal, a
supernatural Ô¨Åtness for being raised up to beatiÔ¨Åc activity. And since this is
found only in an intellectual nature, clearly  it is Ô¨Åtting that only this can be
taken on, since clearly it is reasonable that a nature that cannot be raised up to
a subsequent act cannot be raised up to the initial act.
  27 This answer makes it plain that Henry wants to guard against anything that
might detract from divine omnipotence, since, for the whole of what those people
claim to be in God, they conclude that he can make just as much of it a supposit.
Henry also attributes the whole of this to God, nor does he say‚Äîto account for a
requirement on the part of the one taking on‚Äîthat God cannot take on some
nature which then would not be in God‚Äôs perfect fullness. But this is the case, he
says: that what is incapable of being taken on is the unreasoning nature itself, not
because of a failure of God‚Äôs omnipotence but rather because of the divine nature‚Äôs
excellence and the ugly neediness of an unreasoning nature.

nature can be taken on by God, the understanding is that nothing except this‚Äîif it
is taken on primarily and able to be taken on as a whole equal to the task‚Äîcan be
taken on in this way. But in the triduum, clearly, the body was not taken on in this
way‚Äîonly as part of the reasoning nature that was taken on as a whole and
primarily.
  29 In addition, someone might also say that during the three days this body
could not really be described as being made a supposit because this is not a nature
that really can be made a supposit‚Äîusing ‚Äúsupposit‚Äù in the strict sense. Hence one
could also say, not unacceptably, that this follows: to delimit the dependence of
this body‚Äôs nature is not to make it a supposit, or else a whole that delimits the
dependence or reliance of a part would make the part a supposit, which no one
says. And thus, although the Word during the triduum delimited that dependence
of the body which the whole humanity had delimited‚Äîwhereby the body during
the triduum shared its own property, lying in the tomb, with Christ‚Äîthe Word
was still not really making the body a supposit as it had made the human nature a
supposit. The most obvious sign of this is that this body‚Äôs nature did not share
with Christ the property, body during the triduum, in the way that the human
nature shared, and still shares, with him the property human in a concrete way.
  30 And thus there  is a clear response to the aforesaid objections against
Henry‚Äôs view: what he seems to say about other unreasoning natures, basically,
is that the Word cannot take them on by co-envelopment or make them the
supposit‚Äîespecially by a supposit-making that has a Ô¨Ånal cause. He says this, in
fact: that the purpose cannot be to take on any but an intellectual nature because
there  is no taking-on except to improve the nature taken on, and only an
intellectual nature can have such an improvement. As Augustine comments on
John‚Äôs Gospel: the Word chose to be born from a human so that you would be born
from God and saved. This makes it plain that it would have been useless for God to
be born from any nature other than one born through grace from God.
  31 From these points, one may clearly see that I can have made a statement,
reasonably enough, that does not explicitly contradict this particular view.
  32 Now since in this matter these teachers have also strongly insisted that, in
any case, such conclusions should not be debated because of scandal, I wish to say
about this conclusion that, even if its opposite were more correct and more in line
with the shared approach, still, if that opposed claim were debated in public, it
would be more scandalous for the uneducated and more offensive to pious ears.
  33 Now what simple and ignorant person would not be offended to hear
someone call it possible that these propositions are true: God is a donkey and

God is a stick? And what about propositions granted by many who follow the stated
view? God can be damned to Hell. God can be tortured in Hell by the Devil. The Devil
can be God. Although there is no scandal involved for many learned people when

let them say whether this inference is good:


   I do not assent nor do I grant assent to this conclusion: a Pope is sitting;

   therefore, I believe this one: a Pope is not sitting.


Would the antecedent not be true of someone sleeping, and would anyone also
absolutely exclude the case in which the consequent might be false?

theologians, they ought to know what difference there is between explicitly not

4
        Where Is God‚Äôs Body?

                    4.1 Debating the Eucharist

Pico has been famous for Ô¨Åve centuries, but only one likeness survives that shows
him as he probably was: a bronze medal from the 1480s. Although there are many
pictures of the prince, some are completely Ô¨Åctitious, and most have no docu-
mented connection with him‚Äîincluding the fresco that Cosimo Rosselli Ô¨Ånished
in 1486 for the Church of Sant‚Äô Ambrogio in Florence. Rosselli placed the Ô¨Ågure
often identiÔ¨Åed as Pico in a crowd gathered in the Church‚Äôs piazza as a procession
was forming or breaking up‚Äîprobably organized by one of the city‚Äôs confratern-
ities. These civic societies provided health and social services to their members,
reinforcing bonds of religion and liturgy. The name of the Sant‚Äô Ambrogio society
was Corpus Domini‚Äîthe Lord‚Äôs Body‚Äîwhose holy season ran from late May
through late June: in between came the movable feast of Corpus Christi and
special devotion to the blessed sacrament of the eucharist.¬π
  Corpus Christi was revered in Pico‚Äôs time, but it was not an ancient festival.
Juliana of Cornillon had the visions that inspired her to promote the new liturgy
around 1208. In Belgium she and other devout women‚Äîbeguines‚Äîeventually
attracted support from the newly founded Order of Preachers. But papal interest
in the feast wavered until 1298, when Guillaume Durand‚Äîthe canonist Bishop of
Mende‚Äîadvertised  it in his liturgical manual, an authoritative Guide to Holy
Services. Twenty years later, Pope John XXII gave Corpus Christi a permanent
place among the Church‚Äôs holy days. Unlike a different Guillaume Durand‚Äîthe
theologian who became Bishop of Meaux‚Äîthe Pope was an ardent Thomist in
ecclesiastical politics: he made Aquinas a saint in 1323. Meanwhile, Durand‚Äôs
Guide had already spread the Corpus Christi rite to parishes all over Europe.¬≤
  Today, people still know a hymn from this liturgy: Bach, Palestrina, Schubert,
and many others have written music for the poetry of Pange lingua, whose Ô¨Årst
lines exalt the ‚Äúmystery of a glorious body‚Äù in words written by Thomas Aquinas.
The Ô¨Åfth stanza is even better known:

The ‚Äúnew rite‚Äù was the eucharist, whose splendor overwhelmed feeble human

the central moment of the Mass, no such body was visible on the altar. The
congregation saw only bread shaped and colored as it had been just moments
before. Thomas prayed in his hymn for faith to overcome this failure of the
senses.¬≥
   Artisans caught the spirit of Thomas‚Äôs poetry in gems, glass and gold: they
made monstrances or ostensoria to show God to the faithful as sanctiÔ¨Åed bread.
Carried in processions, displayed inside churches and outside, these imposing
receptacles of divinity gave humans a look at God. Just to see the deity in this way,
even without receiving God‚Äôs body in communion, was a blessed experience.
A missal made in the early Ô¨Åfteenth century showed two angels kneeling beside
such a monstrance; the bread (‚Äòhost‚Äô) inside was a divine sun whose rays were
beams of grace. Later vessels of this dazzlement were Ô¨Çamboyant baroque con-
fections that enthroned God as a Sun King.‚Å¥

wafer of communion bread, was hostia or ‚Äòvictim‚Äô in Latin; the Mass was a
sacriÔ¨Åce; and the implications were troubling. To complete the second part of

  On the day before he suffered, he took bread into his holy and worshipful hands
