The bread that Jesus blessed for his disciples on one day became his body‚Äîbeaten,
bruised, and bloodied on the next day. The suffering was familiar to worshippers
who sang the Pange lingua and accepted that their senses deceived them when
they saw only a shining wafer exhibited in gold. They also knew the gory Gospel
story by heart: the body of Jesus whipped, his head crowned with thorns, hands
and feet nailed to a cross, side pierced by a spear. The religious power of this
brutality bled through in pictorial legends like Saint Gregory‚Äôs Mass (Figure 4.1).
  People told this tale about Pope Gregory I in the early Middle Ages. The saint
was saying Mass when someone showed doubt about the eucharistic miracle until
a host became a bloody Ô¨Ånger. The story gathered detail in many retellings.
A woodcut made in Pico‚Äôs day showed Jesus rising from a tomb at the rear of
Gregory‚Äôs altar with a cross farther back. Displayed in the surrounding space were
the ‚Äòarms of Christ,‚Äô the heraldry of a tortured God: these arma Christi were a
hammer, a ladder, a sword, a spear, a whip, the hand that slapped the holy face‚Äî
two dozen objects in all. The scene was grim, and for embodied humans the
message was searing: the body of Jesus was torn and tormented for you.‚Å∂
  For most believers, it was enough to see the horriÔ¨Åc furniture of Gregory‚Äôs Mass
or gaze at the solar rays of a monstrance or hear prayerful verses by Aquinas, using
the failed senses that Thomas warned about. Through his whole life, however, the
poet of Pange lingua worked to mitigate a different failure‚Äînot of the senses but
of understanding. He believed that since God gave minds to humans, the purpose
of thinking was sacred: the Creator‚Äôs gift obliged reasoning creatures, within their
limitations, to apply their powers of reason to the holiest truths‚Äîmysteries like
the Trinity, incarnation, and eucharist.‚Å∑
  The eucharistic miracle was a theological imperative for Thomas. Among
experts of his era, he was the most forceful advocate of the most favored account
of the sacrament: transubstantiation. To understand this theory and how Pico
contested it, one place to start is the very idea of a sacrament.
  The sacraments of the medieval Church were rituals. The rite of baptism, for
example, began a Christian‚Äôs pilgrimage with cleansing by water‚Äîa symbol of
rescue from sin and spiritual renewal by God‚Äôs grace. Jesus did not need to invent
this  rite: he was baptized by his cousin John. But in a different case‚Äîthe

  Seven such rites may have been traditional even before the twelfth century.

but also to sanctify‚Äù‚Äîeach one ‚Äúa sign of God‚Äôs . . . invisible grace such that it bears

were signs using other signs that were both words and things. The baptismal rite, a
visible sign of invisible grace, made use of additional signs: oil, salt, a candle, a
white garment, and especially water. Water that cleansed the body signiÔ¨Åed
puriÔ¨Åcation, but sacramental purifying, the res sacramenti, was spiritual, not
bodily, and the signifying water‚Äîjust a symbol‚Äîcould not really be what  it
signiÔ¨Åed.‚Åπ
  What about bread and wine? When Jesus established the eucharist, he told the
disciples to perform a ritual ‚Äúin memory of me.‚Äù If their celebrations were to be
commemorative and nothing more, separating wine from bread during the
ceremony would merely symbolize how the body of Jesus was divided from its
lifeblood on the cross. If so, when Jesus said ‚Äúthis is my body‚Äù and ‚Äúthis is the cup
of my blood,‚Äù his words were Ô¨Ågurative, not to be taken literally. Accordingly, the
body and blood would not really be on the altar, and no one would need to explain
why they looked and tasted like bread and wine.¬π‚Å∞

changing while the things themselves remained: when fresh bread turned moldy,
the bread‚Äôs substance remained even though its appearances or accidents changed.
This substance was a subject of inherence for changing accidents. And if someone
said ‚Äòthe bread is moldy,‚Äô the word ‚Äòbread‚Äô was a subject of predication for the
predicate ‚Äòmoldy.‚Äô The predicating was logical and semantic, but the inhering was
metaphysical when the accident called ‚Äòmoldy‚Äô combined with the substance called

  Ôù£the bread‚Äôs substance changes into Christ‚Äôs substance but the bread‚Äôs accidents

counter-explanations. Lawyers were alert to these disputes but less conÔ¨Åned by

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  155

recovered resource‚ÄîAristotle‚Äôs pagan philosophy‚Äîto Christian purposes. One
part of this ancient heritage was a physics or ‚Äònatural philosophy‚Äô that Aquinas
managed to supernaturalize while respecting‚Äîwithin limits‚Äîits naturalism.
   Physics, the science of matter in motion, provided new tools for theology,
including a concept of motion (kin√™sis) that overlapped with change‚Äîmutatio
as well as motus. A general framework for all change and motion was the act/
potency polarity. Consider: there you were, in one room, and then you moved to
another: you were potentially in the second room while you were actually in the
Ô¨Årst. All such motions and changes went from potency to act. Mutatio (‚Äòchange‚Äô)
was a synonym of conversio, and there were four types of mutatio-motus, in the
broadest sense, for quality, quantity, place and substance. The eucharist was a
mystery of change, and Thomas used Aristotle‚Äôs philosophy to demystify it.¬≤¬≤
  Both the Scriptum and the Summa treated Ôù°- and Ôù≤-theories not as alternatives
but as threats to the Ôù£-theory that Thomas promoted. Before rejecting the
Ôù°-theory of annihilation, he had already shown that Christ‚Äôs gloriÔ¨Åed body did
not move in any ordinary way‚Äîlike a wagon or a horse‚Äîfrom place to place.
Then he explained why the eucharistic bread was not annihilated:


   because every motion (motus) gets its name (denominare) from an end-point
   (terminus ad quem), like the motion that goes toward whiteness (albedo) and is
   called ‚Äòwhitening‚Äô (dealbatio); hence a transformation (transmutatio) can be
   called ‚Äòannihilation‚Äô (annihilatio) only  if nothing (nihil) is its end-point. But
   this cannot be true of this change (conversio), where the end-point of the change
  must be Christ‚Äôs body. The reason is that something can come to be here, where it
  was not before, only through a motion or change (motus aut mutatio) of its own
   or another‚Äôs that somehow ends (terminatus) there. Hence,  if the aforesaid
  change (conversio) into Christ‚Äôs body had no end-point, the body would need
   to be here on the altar, after consecration happened, where it was not before, by a
  motion of its own: but this has already been rejected.

How did Christ‚Äôs heavenly body arrive on an altar where bread was already
present but not the body? Maybe the bread was annihilated, and then the divine
body came to take its place. But arrival-and-replacement, like every such motus-
mutatio, needed both an end-point and a starting-point. And if the bread was
annihilated, its end-point would be nothing at all. Yet no item can go to an end-
point except by (Ôù•) its own or (Ôù¶) another‚Äôs motus-mutatio. And if nothing was the
bread‚Äôs end-point, an Ôù•-motus ending with a divine body could never happen to
the bread, leaving an Ôù¶-motus as the only possibility for the body to arrive and
replace the bread. But if the body arrived on an altar down here on earth and came

and only one supposit on the altar where the bread remained with Christ‚Äôs body.¬≥‚Å∞

impanation, his formulas‚Äîassumpta ad suppositum Verbi or assumpta a suppo-
sito Verbi‚Äîwould have worked just as well. But the nature taken on to incarnate
the Word, not to impanate  it, would have been human, a natura hominis or
humanitas, and not bready, a natura panis or paneitas.¬≥¬≤
  For impanation, however, human and divine as well as bready natures had to be
on the altar when Christ was really present. As Quidort understood the eucharist,
the Word sustained two non-divine natures, humanness and breadness, just as
this same supposit was the site of the two natures‚Äîhuman and its own divine
nature‚Äîunited by incarnation. In this hypostatic union, human and divine
natures both belonged to the second person of the Trinity, the incarnate Christ,
and‚Äîsince they were natures of a single supposit‚Äîthere was nothing to prevent
properties from being shared: Quidort concluded that the same was true for the
natures united by supposition in the blessed sacrament. Distinguishing the bread-
ness (paneitas) of bread from the bread itself (panis), he explained that breadness
was what Christ‚Äôs supposit‚Äîdivine and uncreated‚Äîtook on in the eucharist. This
sanctiÔ¨Åed bready nature no longer belonged to unconsecrated, created bread‚Äî
panis creatus. Impanation divinized the consecrated bread‚Äôs breadness so that
what had been a creature somehow became uncreated‚Äîthe nature of a panis
increatus, so to speak.¬≥¬≥
   Strictly speaking, this bready nature was not bread, just as a bodiness (corpor-
eitas) was not anyone‚Äôs body (corpus). After consecration, since the only body on
the altar was Christ‚Äôs, the bread that remained had to be bodiless. And yet its
persistent appearances‚Äîsize, shape and so on‚Äîseemed like features of a body.
Quidort‚Äôs solution was two bodinesses. One of them belonged to Christ‚Äôs body on
the altar‚Äîa divinized body that  still looked like bread. The other bodiness
belonged to Christ‚Äôs human nature supposited by the Word, where sharing of



   ¬≥¬π John 1:14; Quidort, Determinatio, in Martin (1975), pp. 205, 211, 213, 220.220, 221.248‚Äì62;

divinized in this way,  it had been a creature, but after consecration  it was

  The bread‚Äôs substance remains under its own accidents‚Äînot in its own supposit

   view, there are two bodinesses (corporeitates)‚Äîfor breadness (panietas) and for
  humanness (humanitas)‚Äîbecause my view puts a humanness and a breadness
   (paneitas) there. Only one thing has a bodiness there, however, because on the

   because of the supposit‚Äôs sameness (identitas). Hence, although there is a double
   bodiness for this sacrament, the body is still the same because of the unity of
   Christ‚Äôs supposit.

On the altar after consecration, there was just one body, Christ‚Äôs, along with the
disembodied bread‚Äôs substance and nature. This sanctiÔ¨Åed substance was not a
body, according to Quidort, and it needed no supposit of its own because its being
had been taken into the Word‚Äôs supposit. On the altar, then, the only substance
with a bodiness was Christ‚Äôs, which also had its own humanness. In heaven with
the Trinity, meanwhile, the Word took the breadness on, and they shared prop-
erties through the bodiness of Christ‚Äôs human nature, which was also there. This
exalted bodiness mediated between the Word and the divinized breadness‚Äîin the
way that the surface of a substance mediated the substance‚Äôs color.¬≥‚Å¥
  In Quidort‚Äôs world, saying ‚Äòan apple is red‚Äô simpliÔ¨Åed the metaphysics. The red
color, a quality, inheres in the apple‚Äôs substance by way of a quantity, the apple‚Äôs
dimensional surface. Seen as a body (corpus), the apple is both a whole-body
(corpus totum or genus) and a part-body (corpus pars). The whole-body has many
features‚Äîsweet, shiny, crisp, and so on. But the part-body is just a batch of matter.
The batch is a quantity, however, with features like size, shape, and weight, and
Christ in his human nature has a body with some such features. The bodiness of
Christ‚Äôs quantitative part-body mediates‚Äîin Quidort‚Äôs theory‚Äîbetween the
Word‚Äôs supposit and the consecrated bread in its nature of breadness.

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  161

  Because the Word takes bread on by the mediation of the body or Ô¨Çesh as part,
   there is sharing of properties between bread and body or Ô¨Çesh as part, and they
   are predicated of one another to say the body is bread or my Ô¨Çesh is bread or the

Both the bodiness that mediates and the mediated breadness behave like accidents
in relation to the divine supposit in the role of subject. But the unchanging and
impassible God cannot be a subject of accidents. To Ô¨Ånd a solution, Quidort
looked to one of the complementary pairs at the root of Aristotle‚Äôs metaphysics:
act/potency, cause/effect, form/matter, subject/accident, and so on. His solution
needs a little context.
   Aristotle died in the fourth century Ôù¢Ôù£Ôù•, and he met Dante in Hell long
afterward: like all humans, the Philosopher had an immortal soul‚Äîso Christians
believed‚Äîthat lived on after his body was gone. This human soul/body pair
matched a metaphysical form/matter pattern in an obvious way, as also for
many non-human creatures. But what about the bodiless Creator?
   Surely God‚Äôs immaterial thoughts about creation were not formless. Averroes,
explicating Aristotle, suggested that divine thoughts, despite their immateriality,
could be treated as content (like matter in relation to form) with a certain
structure (like form in relation to matter). Every divine thought was absolutely
immaterial, and yet each one was shaped by such a form/matter pairing. But a
non-bodily form/matter pair like God‚Äôs was a bad match for a human pair that
was embodied in  its essence: the analogy was weak, then. Nonetheless, for
creatures and for the Creator, embodied and bodiless, Quidort and his colleagues
saw explanatory power in a number of form/matter relations‚Äîlikewise for act/
potency, cause/effect, subject/accident, and so on.¬≥‚Å∂
  These theologians taught that bread‚Äôs substance, in ordinary circumstances, was
also a subject with non-substantial features: size, shape, color, texture, taste, weight
and so on. These features were the bread‚Äôs accidents or appearances. By seeing,
feeling and tasting, people sensed these appearances of ordinary bread but not its
substance nor  its nature‚Äîan imperceptible breadness that was distinct from
bread‚Äôs imperceptible substance.
   After consecration, Quidort maintained, the Word‚Äôs supposit took on the
breadness of the newly transformed bread in a subject/accident relation specially



   ¬≥‚Åµ Quidort, Determinatio, in Martin (1975), 217.101‚Äì7; Plotnik (1970), pp. 27‚Äì8;  cf. Aquinas,
De ente 3.
   ¬≥‚Å∂ Taylor (1998), pp. 514‚Äì17, explains the form/matter analogy in metaphysics as presented by

                           4.3 Pico‚Äôs Q6

When Pico introduced the ‚Äútaking-on of bread by the incarnate Word‚Äù in Q6, he
had already written his Oration, whose topic was not the dignity of man: other-
wise, by not highlighting the doctrine of incarnation in his speech, the prince
would have missed an opportunity. Every Christian worshipped the ‚Äúincarnate
Word‚Äù dissected so thoroughly in Q6. At Mass the faithful prayed to a God who
was ‚Äúincarnated (incarnatus) from the Holy Spirit out of the Virgin Mary and was
made human.‚Äù This basic article of faith delivered good news from John‚Äôs Gospel:
by announcing that ‚Äúthe Word was made Ô¨Çesh (caro) and dwelled among us,‚Äù
scripture  declared  that embodied humans had a value unheralded  in the
Oration.‚Å¥‚Å∂
  But the orator may have been wise‚Äîas a theologian‚Äînot to make human
dignity a theme of his speech and not to ground it in the incarnation. The word
dignitas never occurs in the Latin New Testament. Although Pico had no reason to
notice the scriptural silence, the result was that proof-texts for a theology of
dignity were in short supply. Human embodiment was also a problem. Paul
assured Christians that they were ‚Äúall Christ‚Äôs body, part for part.‚Äù But what
could that possibly mean? Communal hope about a mystical body was less
plausible than lonely despair about ancestors who had deÔ¨Åled their Ô¨Çesh in
Eden. After the Fall, bodies descended from Adam and Eve were vessels of

  Although Pico made little of the incarnation in the Oration, circumstances

himself.‚Å¥‚Å∏
  Pico never addressed the seeming contradiction: there is no sign that he was
even aware of it. The Q6 and Q4 theses were only two of his 900 Conclusions:
making each and every one of them compatible with all the others was not his
plan. Each proposition was its own puzzle to be solved by disputation, though few
of the 900 theses were actually tested by debate. The Apology recorded only
thirteen such discussions in accounts that Pico shaped for his own purposes.
With so much at stake in these few statements, the author of the Apology should
have been  alert  for embarrassments  that  slipped  past  the author  of  the
Conclusions.
  But Pico noticed no conÔ¨Çict between the Q6 and Q4 theses when he defended
them in the Apology. In  all likelihood, neither of the doctrines involved‚Äî
incarnation and eucharist‚Äîmeant any more to him than to any other educated
believer, including his judges. They and he revered such dogmas without giving
them much thought‚Äîexcept in special situations, like hearing a sermon or
attending a disputation or deliberating at Pico‚Äôs  trial in Rome. When Pico
confronted his judges, some of his provocative ideas were distinctly his own‚Äî
projects for philosophical concord and a Kabbalist Christianity. But the incarna-
tion and the eucharist, both for him and for his judges, were settled dogmas to be
accepted tacitly‚Äîuntil Pico‚Äôs theological adventuring provoked the authorities.‚Å¥‚Åπ
  Otherwise, the topic of Q6 was nothing special for the prince. He said nothing
new about  it, and there was little original about how he said it. In fact, Q6 is
indistinguishable‚Äîall its content and almost all its presentation‚Äîfrom many
similar accounts of eucharistic metaphysics by scholastic theologians and canon
lawyers. Most of them, building on Peter Lombard‚Äôs Sentences from the twelfth

between the condemnations of 1277 and the Angelic Doctor‚Äôs canonization in
1323. In Cabrol‚Äôs survey of the sacraments, one question asked ‚Äúwhether the
substance of bread remains there after consecrating the eucharist.‚Äù Cabrol‚Äôs
answer summarized Thomas‚Äôs position in three ‚Äúconclusions‚Äù:


  The substance of bread and wine does not remain in this sacrament after
   consecration.

  The substance of bread and wine, although it does not remain in the eucharist
   after consecration, is not annihilated‚Äîstrictly speaking.

  The substance of bread and wine is changed into Christ‚Äôs body and blood by an
   absolutely unique change (conversio singularissima).

The Ô¨Årst two conclusions rejected Ôù≤- and Ôù°-theories of eucharistic change, and the
third conÔ¨Årmed Thomas‚Äôs Ôù£-theory. Cabrol documented all three extensively with
passages from the Scriptum on the Sentences, the great Summa, the Summa
Against the Heathens, and a Quodlibet. Then he documented objections to
Thomas‚Äôs views  just  as thoroughly with testimony from  four objectors‚Äî
Durand, Pierre Auriol, Quidort, and Scotus. Finally he resolved these objections,
sometimes calling on Pierre de la Palud, another zealous Thomist, for help.‚Åµ¬π
   Cabrol‚Äôs procedure was the same in all four volumes of his commentary. On the
eucharist, however, he did not quote Quidort directly, describing him vaguely as
‚Äúa certain John of Paris.‚Äù He probably never saw Quidort‚Äôs Determination
and relied instead on accounts by two opponents, Durandus and Paludanus.
Herv√© de N√©dellec, Jacques de Th√©rines, and Thomas of Strasbourg had also
criticized Quidort, but Cabrol did not mention them. Pico‚Äôs position in Q6 was
essentially Quidort‚Äôs, and his knowledge of Quidort came from Cabrol, who did
not  cite Quidort directly: this may explain why Pico also never mentioned
Quidort, his chief inspiration, in Q6.‚Åµ¬≤
   Nevertheless, one of the prince‚Äôs judges, Bishop Garcia, recognized that ‚Äúhe
chases after John of Paris . . . on this issue‚Äù‚Äîmeaning the Q6 material. Garcia

   ‚Ä¢ I‚Äôm talking about a possible sacrament, its possible reality (possibilis veritas)

Pico needed no help from Quidort to distinguish conjecture from dogma. His
entire case in the Apology‚Äînot just Q6‚Äîwas a plea about possibility: his theo-

  We  can  test  Pico‚Äôs  theory  of  his  case  with  the  thirteen condemned

  The Q6 thesis as Pico stated it in the Apology differs slightly but not importantly
from the wording in the Conclusions:



   ‚Åµ‚Å∑ Pico, Apo. pp. 60‚Äì1, 73, 75 (Afr., pp. 194‚Äì6, 232, 242); Edelheit (2008), pp. 331‚Äì4.
   ‚Åµ‚Å∏ Pico, Apo. pp. 11, 33, 37, 41, 47, 75‚Äì6, 99, 103, 105‚Äì6, 108‚Äì9 and the Q6 thesis on p. 60 (Afr.,

On the assertoric/problematic and categorical/modal terminology, see Arist. PA 33b25‚Äì6, opening a
discussion that led eventually to the former distinction; Boethius (MPL 64:657) translated Aristotle‚Äôs
elusive Greek into Latin as literally as he could: ‚ÄúOf propositions, since one of them means being-in
(inesse) or not being-in (non inesse), while another means that it depends (contingere), accordingly there
will be a syllogism constructed in these terms‚Äù; also Aquinas, Opusc. de prop. modal.: ‚ÄúThere are three
ways to use mode. One delimits (determinat) a subject of a proposition, like a pale human runs. One
delimits a predicate, like Socrates is a pale human. And one delimits a predicate‚Äôs actual application
(compositio) to a subject, like saying that Socrates runs  is impossible, and only in this way  is a
proposition called modal (modalis). But other propositions that are not modal are called about
being-in (de inesse). And the modes that delimit application are six: namely, true, false, necessary,
impossible, possible, contingent. But the true and false add nothing beyond the meanings of proposi-
tions about being-in. . . . Possible and contingent have the same meaning, so that propositions about the
possible (de possibili) are equivalent to those directed at the contingent.‚Äù

The ‚Äúusual approach‚Äù granted that any creature‚Äîpossibly‚Äîcould be taken on by
a divine supposit: this taking-on was possible because God could do anything.
Pico had rejected this approach in the Q4 thesis, but the Q6 thesis at least

eucharistic consecration is no less possible than a Ôù£- or an Ôù°-theory. Aristotle had
surveyed the boundaries of possibility by distinguishing assertoric from problem-

  What Pico said about equivalent possibilities in eucharistic doctrine seems

not problematic, unlike ‚Äúcan happen (potest Ô¨Åeri)‚Äù and ‚Äúwould make a statement

  He had said in Q1 that his shorter, simpler and purely assertoric thesis on the

explain why so little of Q6 actually defends its thesis‚Äîas distinct from locating it
on Pico‚Äôs map of theological controversy. If avoiding clarity was his strategy for

   After stating his thesis, which supports an Ôù≤-theory of the eucharist, Pico denied

hypothetical Ôù≤-theory, not an amendment of sacred doctrine.‚Å∂‚Å¥
   Since the Q6 thesis asserted nothing, according to its author, it asserted no
doctrine at all‚Äîjust listing a number of possibilities‚Äîand only a heretic would

172  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

This is Pico‚Äôs analysis: the Ô¨Årst statement about an incarnate Father is heretical
and de sic esse, unlike the next statement de possibili, which is orthodox. Call the
Ô¨Årst statement g¬π and the second g¬≤:


   g¬π The Father is incarnate. Pater est incarnatus.

   g¬≤ The Father can be incarnated. Pater potest incarnari.

Dogma says that g¬π is false but (because of God‚Äôs omnipotence) that g¬≤ is true.
There is no incarnate Father‚Äîas an article of faith and a matter of fact‚Äîbut there
could be. Pico means to contrast g¬π as a factual and assertoric statement with g¬≤ as
possible and problematic, and this is enough to make his point. But what an
‚Äúopposite‚Äù of either statement might be needs more attention than he gave it. He
could have, but did  not, explain the difference by comparing these other
propositions:


   p¬π That the Father is incarnate / is / true. Quod Pater est incarnatus / est / verum.

   p¬≤ That the Father is incarnate / is / possible. Quod Pater est incarnatus / est /
   possibile.

At Ô¨Årst glance, the form of both seems to be Ôù≥/c/Ôù∞, where the copula (c) is est and
the predicate term (Ôù∞) is verum in p¬π but possibile in p¬≤. The general form of all
propositions is Ôù≥/c/Ôù∞, and the simplest are ‚Äòcategorical‚Äô statements that link a
categorical or predicate term (Ôù∞) with a subject term (Ôù≥) by way of a copula (c).
Subjects of simple categoricals are non-complex terms, often representing unitary
items‚Äîthings or animals, like apples or frogs, or else persons, like a human
(homo) or the Father (Pater). But some logicians treated the subject or dictum
(Ôù§) of a ‚Äòmodal‚Äô proposition‚ÄîÔù§/c/Ôù∞‚Äîas a complex state of affairs with its own
internal structure, like ‚Äòthat an apple is red‚Äô or ‚Äòthat the Father is incarnate.‚Äô
  Q6 describes the opposite of g¬≤ as ‚Äúheretical.‚Äù But what is the opposite of ‚Äòthe
Father can be incarnated‚Äô? Maybe g¬≥‚Äî‚Äòthe Father cannot be incarnated‚Äô‚Äînot
found in Pico‚Äôs account. But his reasoning seems to contrast the problematic g¬≤
with the assertoric g¬π. And yet a claim about incarnational possibility, like g¬≤, is not
the opposite of a claim of incarnational fact, like g¬π‚Äì even though g¬π is different
from g¬≤. Likewise, p¬π is different from p¬≤, but being true is not the opposite of being
possible: otherwise, ‚Äòan apple is red‚Äô could not be both possible and true. Pico
certainly understood all this about propositions like g¬π and g¬≤. To show his judges
up, however, he would have done better to make his point more carefully. He
could have explained that p¬π is false but p¬≤ is true, and he knew enough modal logic
to do the job‚Äîwhich needed only the basics.
  Every student of logic learned that propositions have six modal states‚Äî
possible/impossible, necessary/contingent and true/false. But textbooks in Pico‚Äôs

## Page 186

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  173

day focused on the Ô¨Årst four because true/false was a choice for all propositions,
including modals. Modals like p¬≤ that express possibility or impossibility can be
true or false, also those like p¬π that express truth or falsity. The dictum is the same
in p¬π and p¬≤: ‚Äòthat the Father is incarnate.‚Äô By itself this noun-clause makes no
complete statement and is neither true nor false, unlike p¬π and p¬≤. Truth is the
modality expressed by p¬π, a complete false statement; likewise p¬≤, a complete true
statement, expresses possibility. But suppose Pico had changed p¬π and p¬≤ into s¬π
and s¬≤ by substituting Filius for Pater to make a different dictum: ‚Äòthat the Son is
incarnate.‚Äô


   s¬π That the Son is incarnate / is / true. (Quod Filius est incarnatus / est / verum.)

   s¬≤ That the Son is incarnate / is / possible. (Quod Filius est incarnatus / est /
   possibile.)


Then both s¬π and s¬≤ would be true, whereas p¬π, a modal statement of truth, is false
while p¬≤, a modal statement of possibility, is true.‚Å∂‚Å∂
  The prince let all this ride in Q6 on an undeveloped distinction between de
possibili and de sic esse statements. By counting on a few gestures in a few
paragraphs, he weakened his defense. Haste might have served him better had
he been short on time, as he probably was when writing Q9 and Q10, two other‚Äî
very brief‚Äîeucharistic questions. But Q6 goes on and on. Q1 on Christ in Hell is
also long, but it gains energy by presenting the author‚Äôs case in his own terms
against heavy odds. As things stood, Pico‚Äôs chances in Q6 were no better than in
Q1, but he made them worse by not speaking for himself‚Äîafter a few paragraphs.
   After the brisk beginning, the rest of Q6, in great detail, explores accounts of
eucharistic change‚ÄîÔù≤-, Ôù°- and Ôù£-theories‚Äîthat were not Pico‚Äôs own, not even the
Ôù≤-theory of remaining that came to him from Quidort by way of Cabrol. Without
mentioning Quidort, he defended this unattributed theory at such length in Q6
not because he had any great stake in it but because his enemies chose to attack it.
In fact, the Q6 thesis in its original setting, the Conclusions, seems difÔ¨Ådent‚Äî
unlike the other twelve condemned propositions. How much Pico really cared
about incarnational metaphysics is impossible to say, but his command of this
intricate subject is plain to see in Q4. As eucharistic theology, however, there‚Äôs
nothing original or memorable about Q6‚Äîbeyond the author‚Äôs display of himself
as a stupor mundi of the mind.‚Å∂‚Å∑
  So why should this prodigy have worked so hard to say so much that was so
routine? Because in his place and time it was smarter to bank on authority and
tradition than on creativity or novelty‚Äînot least during a heresy trial. True: the
prince had nothing new to say in Q6. But he took old news‚Äîindirectly, for the

174  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

most part‚Äîfrom authorities with standing. Even Quidort, despite his troubles
with the Church, was a recognized expert in his day, and Cabrol became a clarion

witnesses called by the prince were eminent theologians.‚Å∂‚Å∏
   If his account of several centuries of controversy was correct in  its broad
outlines‚Äîhis story about which theorist backed which eucharistic theory for
which reasons‚Äîno one who read Q6 in 1487, when  it was published, could
have reasonably concluded that the theological state of play was anything but an
unsettled contest between the Ôù£-theory, long the most favored, and its Ôù°- and
Ôù≤-competitors. As a practical matter, Pico made a mistake by challenging the
Thomist Ôù£-theory of change. But he stood accused of doctrinal error, not bad
judgment, and his judges were wrong and rigid, not at all reasonable, in their
indictment: it was simply not possible, within Pico‚Äôs range of options, to make a
doctrinal mistake serious enough to deserve a heresy charge about a doctrine as
Ô¨Çuid as eucharistic consecration.
   After restating his thesis, explicating  it and defending  it in a perfunctory,
foolhardy way, Pico turned to the ‚Äúacademic exercise‚Äù in four parts that Ô¨Ålled
the rest of Q6. First, he stated and explained passages from patristic and other
authorities‚Äîthe Damascene, the Areopagite, Augustine, Ambrose, Gregory the
Great, Peter Lombard, and the trouble-making Berengar‚Äîthat Quidort and many
others had cited. Next he lined up seven arguments against the Ôù≤-theory of
remaining. Then he rebutted them. Finally he returned to the promised ‚Äúexercise‚Äù
and chased down problem after problem that his theological masters could not
solve with their elaborate apparatus of body and bread, bodiness and breadness;
remaining, annihilating and changing; subsisting and existing; substance, accident
and modes; suppositing, sustaining, and taking-on.‚Å∂‚Åπ
  The vocabulary of Pico‚Äôs account had been in use for centuries: assumptio,
communicatio, conversio, corpus, genus, homo, humanitas, idiomata, natura,
panis, paneitas, pars, sacramentum, sanguis, species, subiectum, subsistentia, sub-
stantia, substentare, unire, Verbum, vinum, et cetera, et cetera. He may have
introduced this terminology to a few Quattrocento readers, but he did not invent
it. One word that might have puzzled even the omnisapient Poliziano‚Äîthe
prince‚Äôs friend who taught Aristotle‚Äôs logic in Florence‚Äîwas suppositum and its
cousins, used eighty-two times in Q6.‚Å∑‚Å∞
  Almost all the suppo- words there are metaphysical. Quidort‚Äôs‚Äîhence Pico‚Äôs‚Äî
special insight was that eucharistic suppositing, the suppositatio of the Q6 thesis,
was just like incarnational taking-on or assumptio. The rock-solid dogma of
taking-on by incarnation authenticated Quidort‚Äôs Ôù≤-theory of bread remaining

in the eucharist, understood as a kind of suppositing, metaphysical suppositing,

treatment of the same terminology as metaphysical. Here, as in other cases, the
prince carried on a medieval tradition, using the vocabulary, concepts and pro-
cedures of scholastic theology to resist attacks that damaged his reputation and
could have killed him.‚Å∑¬≥


                   4.4 A Logic for Consecration


The hazardous topic of eucharistic theology was the subject of three of the thirteen
conclusions that the prince defended in 1487, though none of the three led to a
heresy conviction. His judges decided that the Q9 thesis, like the proposition
defended by Q6, was erroneous, but that the Q10 thesis was just ‚Äúscandalous and
contrary to the usual view of saintly teachers.‚Äù Since Q6 covered so much ground,

If the Q9 thesis had been a composite modal like

  mK that an accident exists without existing-in / is (est) / impossible,

the copula (c), ‚Äúis,‚Äù would have linked the predicate (Ôù∞), ‚Äúimpossible,‚Äù with the
dictum (Ôù§), to the left of ‚Äúis,‚Äù in order to assert the impossibility of Ôù§‚Äîof accidents

called his thesis a ‚Äúdivided modal,‚Äù like

  mD an accident / cannot (non potest) / exist without existing-in,

whose structure might have looked more categorical (Ôù≥cÔù∞) than modal (Ôù§cÔù∞)

sentences, mK and mD, state the same proposition and express the same mode,
impossibility, about accidents existing in subjects. But mK makes the proposition‚Äôs
modal character more obvious.

While schooling the pope‚Äôs agents about divided modals, he also recommended a
refresher on ampliation, a division of supposition theory. If the bishops, lawyers
and theologians dusted off their logic books, they would see that ‚Äúampliation is
widening . . . from a smaller supposition to a larger one, as when someone says a
human can be the Antichrist, the term human supposits not only for those that
are but also for those that will be. Hence, it is ampliated to those in the future.‚Äù
Ampliation  is a kind of supposition‚Äîsemantic as distinct from metaphysical
suppositing‚Äîand supposition is not the same as signiÔ¨Åcation. The signiÔ¨Åcations
of subject and predicate terms in a categorical proposition like


  h¬π some human / is / the Antichrist,

               quidam homo / est / Antichristus,


stay the same when the proposition is modalized, like


  h¬≤ some human / can / be the Antichrist,

               quidam homo / potest / esse Antichristus,


a divided modal whose composite form is


  h¬≥ that some human is the Antichrist / is / possible,

              quendam hominem esse Antichristum / est / possibile.

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  181

When h¬π is modalized as h¬≤‚Äîa divided modal‚Äîwhat changes is not what its terms
mean or signify but what they stand for or supposit, and suppositing can be
broadened or ampliated by a proposition‚Äôs tense and mode. A modal copula,
like potest in h¬≤, ampliates a proposition‚Äôs subject term, homo in h¬≤, to supposit for
items outside the scope of an unmodalized copula, like est in h¬π. Accordingly, the
term homo in h¬≤ supposits (indirectly) for  all the humans who can be the
Antichrist, unlike the homo in h¬π that supposits only for humans who actually
are the Antichrist.‚Å∏‚Å¥
  In 1487, when Pico advised his judges to check their logic books, the world had
not yet ended, the Antichrist had not arrived and h¬π was false because no human at
all was the Antichrist: the homo in h¬π supposited for no one then on the planet. But
at the same moment, because God can do anything, he could have made any
human the Antichrist, and h¬≤ was true since God could have picked anyone‚Äî
dead, living or unborn‚Äîto be the Antichrist: the homo in h¬≤ supposited for many
possible candidates. Modal ampliation broadened the supposition of homo in h¬≤
far beyond the same term‚Äôs unmodalized supposition in h¬π.
  This was the logic that Pico had in mind when he claimed that the phrase
eucharistiae sacramentum in his Q9 thesis was ‚Äúampliated (ampliative) to what is
or can be‚Äù because the proposition that contained it was a divided modal. Without
ampliation, the phrase would have supposited only for the actual sacrament as
ofÔ¨Åcially deÔ¨Åned, but ampliation broadened the supposition of the phrase to
include possible and unofÔ¨Åcial conceptions of the sacrament‚Äîlike Pico‚Äôs. His
judges, apparently oblivious about ampliation, astounded him:

   I don‚Äôt know what logic led these teachers to see that a divided thesis about
   possibility (de possibili) was to be condemned as false because it was made from a
   thesis about being-in (de inesse) using the same terms. And I‚Äôm astonished that
   they don‚Äôt know that this is a fallacy of the consequent.‚Å∏‚Åµ

In the simpler case (leaving eucharistiae sacramentum unampliated) where Pico‚Äôs
‚Äúdivided thesis about possibility‚Äù was something like

  mD without existing-in, an accident / cannot / exist

                      accidens nisi inexistat / non potest / existere,


he could have supplied a composite equivalent,

  mK that an accident exists without existing-in / is / impossible,

                  quod accidens existens non inexistit / est / impossibile,

with the same terms, mD, was a divided modal and only implicitly problematic.
Could this have been Pico‚Äôs choice for an assertoric ‚Äúthesis about being-in‚Äù? After
all, mD makes the strong claim that ‚Äúwithout existing-in, an accident cannot exist.‚Äù
However, an even stronger  statement, without any  quiet modality, would
claim that

   rE without existing in, an accident does not exist,

                            accidens non existit nisi inexistat.

In a rhetorical way, mK seems at least as assertive as mD, but only rE allows no
modality of any kind, even implicit: impossibile in mK is a modal predicate, and
potest in mD is a modal verb. But all three statements have the same terms. Hence,
the result of transporting the unmodalized content of rE into the dictum of mK
might be that rE, ‚Äúa thesis about being-in,‚Äù was transmuted‚Äîas Pico charged‚Äî
into an equivalent of mK, which could be mD, a ‚Äúdivided thesis about possibility.‚Äù
If so, then rE would have been the assertoric (de inesse) statement that the judges
correctly ‚Äúcondemned as  false‚Äù when they incorrectly‚Äîaccording to Pico‚Äî
condemned mD,  a  problematic  (de  possibili)  thesis  because  of  its modal
character.‚Å∏‚Å∂
  ‚ÄúI‚Äôm astonished,‚Äù the prince exclaimed, ‚Äúthat they‚Äù‚Äîhis judges‚Äî‚Äúdon‚Äôt know
that this is a fallacy.‚Äù How could anyone fail to distinguish a culpable proposition
like rE from a blameless statement like mD? Like other victims of logic, the judges
were tricked by their own sophistry‚Äîby a ‚Äúfallacy of the consequent.‚Äù This and
twelve other fallacies, like begging the question and equivocation, were topics of
elementary logic, like modality and supposition‚Äîmore opportunities to reschool
the court with the prince as schoolmaster. The judges went wrong by arguing


  from a statement less broad (minus amplum) to a broader one in order to
   eliminate (destructio) the broader one, which is like saying no one is at home,
   so no one can be at home. For they argue in the same way to condemn this
   proposition: in no sacrament of the Eucharist that actually exists or was actually
   established by Christ are there accidents in a subject; therefore, in no sacrament
   that exists or can exist or be established by Christ can there be accidents in a
   subject.‚Å∏‚Å∑


   ‚Å∏‚Å∂ Pico, Q9.5 (Apo. p. 104; Afr., 336‚Äì8). Pico‚Äôs compressed and elliptical Q9 makes it hard to know
exactly which divided modal he had in mind. Milo Crimi suggests ‚Äúsome sacrament of the eucharist is
possibly a non-C-type‚Äô as the divided proposition, in contrast to this composite, ‚Äúthat some sacrament
of the eucharist is a non-C-type is possible.‚Äù
   ‚Å∏‚Å∑ Pico, Q9.5 (Apo. p. 104; Afr., 336‚Äì8); Peter of Spain (2014), pp. 268‚Äì70, 356 (7.23‚Äì4, 101), with
‚ÄúIntroduction,‚Äù pp. 46‚Äì50.

## Page 196

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  183

The fallacy detected by Pico, often called ‚ÄòafÔ¨Årming the consequent,‚Äô might
be expressed today as a bad inference‚Äîfrom certainty that p entails q to
assurance that q entails p: bad reasoning because conÔ¨Ådence that p entails q,
even if this is true, does not justify conviction that q entails p. Or put it another
way. Although this (the argument-form  still called modus ponens) is good
reasoning:


    if p, then q;
   p;
   therefore q;


this is a bad reasoning:


    if p, then q;
   q;
   therefore p.

The antecedent of  ‚Äúif  p, then q‚Äù comes before the comma; the consequent
follows the comma; and the second premiss of the second argument, q, afÔ¨Årms
the consequent, giving the  fallacy  its  label. Peter of Spain‚Äôs Summaries  of
Logic, a medieval textbook, illustrated the fallacy in a different way with a
pair of hypotheticals. Peter deÔ¨Åned a hypothetical as two or more categorical
propositions like


  h this is a human, hic est homo,


and


  a this is an animal, hic est animal,

joined or disjoined by connectives like ‚Äòand,‚Äô ‚Äòor‚Äô and ‚Äòif,‚Äô as in

    if this is a human, this is an animal (h ! a)
   therefore, if this is an animal, this is a human (a ! h).

If this were so, and if Socrates was human, hence an animal, then the philosopher‚Äôs
horse, certainly an animal, would have been human: obviously not so. The error
could be analyzed, according to the Summaries, in genus/species terms‚Äîa com-
mon medieval approach. Since every schoolboy knew that the genus Ôù°ÔùÆÔù©Ôù≠Ôù°Ôù¨
contains not only the Ôù®ÔùµÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆspecies but also other species like Ôù®ÔùØÔù≤Ôù≥Ôù•and
Ôù¶Ôù≤ÔùØÔùß, any beginner could see the point of Peter‚Äôs slightly funny illustration.
Human horses, like myriad Antichrists, made teenagers smile when they studied

## Page 197

184  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

Peter‚Äôs textbook in universities of Pico‚Äôs era: they turned the jokes against their
teachers, of course, and Pico knew how to use the same raillery against his
judges.‚Å∏‚Å∏
  But the bad inference that the prince wanted to expose was more cerebral‚Äîand
no laughing matter since a sacred ritual was involved. By assenting to eucharistic
dogma, including the treatment of accidents in the Thomist Ôù£-theory of change, he
accepted dogmatic arguments‚Äîin particular, ofÔ¨Åcial reasoning about eucharistic
accidents:


    if this ofÔ¨Åcially is a sacramental accident, it has no subject;
   this ofÔ¨Åcially is a sacramental accident;
   therefore, this has no subject.

But a related argument, Ô¨Çawed by afÔ¨Årming the consequent, would have been
unacceptable:


    if this ofÔ¨Åcially is a sacramental accident, it has no subject;
   this ofÔ¨Åcially has no subject;
   therefore, this ofÔ¨Åcially is a sacramental accident.


The argument is fallacious, and its conclusion might be false. But a false conclusion
was not the mistake that Pico railed at. Conclusions are propositions that go wrong
by being false. But being fallacious, like being invalid, is a failure of arguments‚Äî
not propositions. So when Pico accused his judges of inferring falsely ‚Äúfrom a
statement less broad to a broader one,‚Äù he attacked an argument which, to qualify
as an argument, had  to have  at  least two  propositions.  If  the narrower
statement was

   rE without existing-in, an accident does not exist,


might the broader statement have been the divided modal,

  mD without existing-in, an accident cannot exist?

Another option would have been to modalize rE as this composite, hence visible,
statement of possibility:

  rK that an accident does not exist without existing-in is possible.

This composite modal  is an afÔ¨Årmative proposition even though  its dictum
(before the ‚Äúis‚Äù) is negative, and the whole statement eliminates nothing, not
even the Thomist claim that

  tN no eucharistic accident exists by existing-in,

since tN and the dictum of rK might both be possible even if either or both were
false. But tN eliminates Pico‚Äôs view, as in mD. He probably thought that a propos-

which excludes nothing: then he could impute the narrower tN to his judges. But he
did not regard tN as a false statement: on the contrary, since he assented to it as a
matter of doctrine, he could not have denied it. Nonetheless, he treated tN as part of
a fallacious argument invalidated by a fallacy of afÔ¨Årming the consequent.

   say that (Œ¥) a eucharistic accident can have a subject: (Œµ) the non-modal Œ≥ is

The Œ¥-clause is what Pico claimed that his Q9 thesis proposed. The Œ≥-clause

their bad reasoning was a fallacy of afÔ¨Årming the consequent, as Pico charged,
instead of this good argument (modus ponens),


    if p, then q;
   p;
   therefore q,


they would have used this bad one:


    if p, then q;
   q;
   therefore p.

186  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

   Œµ is correct;

That ‚ÄúŒ∂ is correct,‚Äù however, was not granted by Pico. And if the judges‚Äîas he
charged‚Äîhad been snared by a  fallacy, their inference to  Œ∂‚Äôs correctness
was invalid. Hence, assuming that he was right about the type of mistake that
his judges made, he had shown that their logic was faulty: their dogmatic
certainty foreclosed an important theoretical possibility because they reasoned
badly.
  On the other hand, even if there was a fallacy lurking in the court‚Äôs charges,
Pico‚Äôs exposure of it was badly underargued. He unpacked his thesis in order to
defend  it: a reasonable procedure. But the exposition in Q9 involved three
interlocked technical issues‚Äîa divided modal, an ampliated proposition and a
fallacy of afÔ¨Årming the consequent‚Äîthat needed more discussion. The peevish
prince failed to inform his judges‚Äîor worse: rushing to dazzle them with logic, he
confused and insulted them instead.



                    4.5 Liturgy, Law, and Logic

The tirade did not continue in Q10, Pico‚Äôs shortest Question on the eucharist.
Maybe he thought that a less theatrical defense would work because canon law was
so clearly on his side. This is the Q10 thesis in the Apology‚Äîslightly different from
the original in the Conclusions, as in other cases:


  The words this is my body that are said in the consecration are taken materially
   (materialiter) and not as signifying (signiÔ¨Åcative).‚Åπ‚Å∞





   ‚Åπ‚Å∞ Pico, Q10.1 (Apo. p. 105; Afr., 344); cf. Con. p. 42 (Bnd. p. 90) with these variants: Illa verba (hoc

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  187

To defend this proposition, Pico cited just one non-biblical authority, ‚Äúthe ofÔ¨Åcial

Consecration‚Äîin Ô¨Åve sections or ‚Äúdistinctions‚Äù‚Äîis the third part of Gratian‚Äôs

guidance on liturgy and sacraments in hundreds of ‚Äòchapters‚Äô or ‚Äòcanons.‚Äô The
lawyers who wrote, debated, and adjudicated these regulations were less interested
in theology than in human conduct‚Äîwhether priests and laity treated sacred
matters like sacraments with due reverence. Before receiving the eucharist, for
example, married males were told to abstain from sex for a few days‚Äînot just
food for a few hours.‚Åπ¬π
  Canon 25 of De consecratione, titled Timorem (‚ÄúFear‚Äù), extracted a larger
principle from New Testament passages where Paul admonished Christians
about their celebrations of the eucharist. ‚ÄúHe that eateth and drinketh unworth-

reÔ¨Çected Paul‚Äôs minatory language also cited the Lord‚Äôs sacramental words, ‚Äúthis
is my body,‚Äù as a hopeful message of faith:


  They get damnation, not salvation, who share unworthily in Christ‚Äôs sacraments.
  The Apostle teaches us fear, in fact, when he says one who eats and drinks
   unworthily eats and drinks judgment for himself. But the Lord‚Äôs word instructs
   us in faith, saying this is what for many is given, my body.

Hoc est, quod pro multis datur, corpus meum: Canon 25, echoing Paul‚Äôs Epistle,
broke the puzzling eucharistic formula up into smaller pieces‚Äîprobably because
Christ‚Äôs words, as recorded in the New Testament, were not exactly the same as a
priest‚Äôs words of consecration. This close examination of sacramental language
invited other lawyers to focus on a form of words that had been Ô¨Åxed immemori-
ally in the canon (unchanging part) of the Mass.‚Åπ¬≤

margins of the Decretum, producing layers of commentary that commented on
comments and on and on. By the middle of the thirteenth century, strata of
commentary were organized as a Standard Gloss (Glossa ordinaria): like similar
guides to the Bible and civil law‚Äîand like Peter Lombard‚Äôs Sentences‚Äîthese were
mainstays of instruction at Bologna and other universities that had law faculties.

  This theory distinguished material from personal supposition, sometimes called
signifying. Ordinarily, a word like ‚Äòfrog‚Äô supposits personally for ordinary things‚Äî

  Some say that in this utterance (locutio), this is my body, the statement (dictio)
   implies that the indicating is conceptual (demonstratio concepta), not put to use
   (exercita), because that whole utterance is taken materially (materialiter) when

   Christ‚Äôs statement that this is my body. This view cannot stand, however, because
  then the words would not apply to a present material body, and so the sacrament
   could not be carried out . . . . Besides, this view does not totally eliminate the
  problem because the same principles  still operate as they did for the Ô¨Årst
   utterance of these words when Christ spoke them, when obviously they were
  used not materially but to signify (non materialiter, sed signiÔ¨Åcative).

If priests repeated the formula just to narrate Christ‚Äôs utterance of the same words,
the sacramental mystery would fade into a mere memorial. Thomas equated such
narrating with the priest‚Äôs ‚Äúwhole utterance . . . taken materially‚Äù because he feared
that an incorrect suppositional analysis of the formula would have the same result,
which he called ‚Äúheretical.‚Äù Hence he insisted that the priest‚Äôs words, like Christ‚Äôs
original statement, had to be ‚Äútaken as signifying and not merely materially.‚Äù¬π‚Å∞¬π
  Thomas was emphatic but not entirely persuasive. When Scotus addressed the
same problem, he focused on the sequence of sacramental words, concluding that
hoc est corpus meum ‚Äúis true only at the Ô¨Ånal instant of its utterance.‚Äù Unless a
priest spoke the right words in the right order, ‚Äúplainly change would not happen
by the power of those sacramental words.‚Äù Also, ‚Äúwhen we are talking to someone
else (alius) and put some words Ô¨Årst, we signify that the words of another (alter)
have not been said by ourselves but only narrated (recitata) and were said
originally by someone else (alius).‚Äù The Ô¨Årst ‚Äúsomeone else (alius)‚Äù addressed by
the priest would be another person in attendance, and Christ would be the alter
whose words were not ‚Äúsaid by ourselves but only narrated.‚Äù Scotus thought it
acceptable, evidently, to describe the priest‚Äôs words of consecration as narrative.¬π‚Å∞¬≤
  Durand de Saint-Pour√ßain, a Dominican like Thomas, went farther than the
subtle Franciscan. Remembering the glossator‚Äôs warning that a priest ‚Äúwould be
lying‚Äù who pronounced the words of consecration in order to signify, Durandus

Durandus supported the narrative approach as vigorously as Thomas opposed

when the speaking began‚Äîstarting with the pronoun hoc‚Äîhe cleared priests of

glossator‚Äôs account would make the sacrament ineffective.¬π‚Å∞‚Å¥
  Roughly forty years later, when Jean Cabrol recorded the state of play on this

sacramental words, Cabrol maintained that


   the hoc indicates (demonstrat) in a delimited way (determinate) neither any
   terminus from-which nor any terminus to-which of transubstantiation but instead
  something common to both in the mode of an indeterminate particular (indivi-
  duum vagum) . . . . The hoc also does not indicate anything by disjunction . . . .
   Instead, its supposition there is neither simple nor determinate but merely
  confused‚Äîif not by reason of the language then still by what Christ established
  when  it pleased him to adapt this practical and productive expression to
   suppositing and signifying of this type.

Cabrol‚Äôs labels for the relevant types of supposition‚Äîsimple, determinate and
merely confused‚Äîwere elements of the theory as fully developed by Ockham

   Christ spoke to the apostles. But the consecrating priest does not speak to the
   apostles and says take and so on to them only as narration. For only the priest

‚Äútake and eat.‚Äù These imperative verbs made sense to Pico only as narration, when


   a narrator‚Äôs statement supposits (supponit) for a statement made by another
   person. Accordingly, this narrative (recitatoria) statement about another‚Äôs state-

   narrating but as signifying, the shedding of Christ‚Äôs blood for others‚Äînot for the
  one consecrating‚Äîwould be indicated by saying which for you and for many is

narrator‚Äôs statement substitutes (supponit) for a statement made by another

and says ‚Äúthis is my body‚Äù as a substitute for the Lord‚Äôs words. But the Q10 thesis

               4.6 Pico‚Äôs Ninth and Tenth Questions

        Ninth Question: On Accidents in the Sacrament


   1. Someone who says that an accident cannot exist without existing-in will be
      able to hold to the sacrament of the eucharist even while holding that the
      substance of bread does not remain.
   2. These teachers have not actually said that this conclusion is heretical or
      suggests heresy but only that it is wrong. Had they given any reasons for this
      view,  I could either believe them or respond to them. But neither in
     condemning this conclusion or the others have they brought up any reasons
      that I have seen. To make this conclusion clear, however, I state that by
     proposing it I do not mean it to say categorically and unconditionally that
     an accident cannot exist without a subject or that in the sacrament it is




   ¬π‚Å∞‚Å∑ Pico, Q10.1 (Apo. p. 105; Afr., 344).
   ¬π‚Å∞‚Å∏ Pico, Apo. p. 74 (Afr., p. 238); section 4.2 of this chapter.
   ¬π‚Å∞‚Åπ Pico, Q10.3, 6 (Apo. p. 106; Afr., 344‚Äì6).

      actually in a subject. Quite the contrary: I agree Ô¨Årmly with the Church that
      the sacramental accidents actually exist without a subject.
   3. But I maintain that someone who says there can be no accidents without a
      subject and, like Thomas, holds that there is a real distinction between the
      bread‚Äôs existence and  its essence, could claim that there  is nothing to
      prevent divine omnipotence from keeping the bread‚Äôs existence without
        its essence, just as God preserves the substantial and accidental act without
        its potency, and as a result the accidents would have a subject in the very
      being of the bread while the bread‚Äôs substance would not remain. Christ‚Äôs
     body could be really present to these accidents, in fact, just as it is present
    now to accidents that have a subject nowhere.
   4. And whatever may be true of the sacrament of the eucharist that Christ
      actually established and that the Church believes in, where the consensus is
      that no bread remains‚Äîso much so that that reproducing it would be a
      creation‚Äîstill, by holding Thomas‚Äôs aforesaid view about a real distinction
     between essence and  existence, and understanding ‚Äúsacrament of the
      eucharist‚Äù as ampliated to what is or can be, as this is understood in my
      conclusion, which is a divided modal, then, not only is the present conclu-
      sion acceptable and not in error, the actual mistake is to say that there could
     not be any sacrament of the eucharist where the accidents were in a subject.
   5.  I don‚Äôt know what logic led these teachers to see that a divided thesis about
       possibility was to be condemned as false because it was made from a thesis
     about being-in using the same terms. And I‚Äôm astonished that they don‚Äôt
    know that this is a fallacy of the consequent‚Äîarguing from a statement less
     broad to a broader one in order to eliminate the broader one, which is like
      saying no one is at home, so no one can be at home. For they argue in the
     same way to condemn this proposition: in no sacrament of the Eucharist
      that actually exists or was actually established by Christ are there accidents
      in a subject; therefore, in no sacrament that exists or can exist or be
      established by Christ can there be accidents in a subject.
   6. These statements clearly show how wrong  it was for these fathers to
    condemn as mistaken the present conclusion about a possibility. They
     should have remembered that some Catholic experts have also held, in
       fact, that accidents in the sacrament of the altar have a subject in the
      existence of the substance that remains. In fact, one approach taken by
     theologians‚Äîas noted by Pierre de la Palud on book 4, distinction 12,
      question 3‚Äîstates that just as quality remains here naturally because its
       direct subject, which is quantity, remains, even though its distal subject,
     which is substance, is removed, and thus the quality itself does not subsist
     but inheres, so also quantity is certainly missing from its distal subject,
     which  is the essence of the substance, but  it has  its own special and
     proximate subject, which is the being of the substance, by means of which

## Page 210

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  197


      it is-in the substance because it is possible for that being to remain when
    the essence does not remain. Indeed, they say that the remaining  is
    necessary in consideration of how this being ceases.
 7. And they say that this is clearly possible, in fact: even though essence is
    prior to being itself‚Äîfrom which it would seem, since the prior can be
    separated from the posterior, that essence without being, rather than the
    reverse, is more able to be separated and produced‚Äîyet on the other hand,
    considering that essence relates to being as potency to act and as matter to
    form, then, since act depends less on potency than the reverse, so being is
    plainly more producible without essence than the reverse. But in the
   Word‚Äôs incarnation God produced the essence of humanness without its
    being, as stated by many experts. Therefore, it is much more possible in
     this case to produce the being of the bread‚Äôs substance without the essence,
    wherefore, just as in that other case the essence is taken on in and remains,
    but not the being that does not remain, so in this case the essence can be
    changed and not remain, while the being will remain and stay unchanged.
 8. Second, they show that this is not only possible but necessary because
    nothing created can be changed into the uncreated. But one created thing
    can be changed into another when they share in matter and in genus:
    otherwise not‚Äîjust as an accident is not changed into a substance nor,
    following the usual opinion, into an accident of another category. But the
    essence of bread agrees in matter and genus with the essence of Christ‚Äôs
    body. Hence one could be changed into the other even in the course of
    nature if the body were mortal. But the bread‚Äôs being shares in neither
    matter nor genus with the substantial being of Christ‚Äôs body, since this
    divine body is uncreated because it belongs to the divine supposit, since in
    Christ there is only a single being of actual substantial existence. Therefore
      it is impossible for the bread‚Äôs being to be changed and necessary for it to
    remain.
 9. My own view is that it is not necessary for the being to remain. For one
    thing, if it cannot be changed, it can still be annihilated, or else it can be
    destroyed by slipping into the potency of matter, such that, just as human-
    ness can be assumed when the being has not been assumed while remain-
    ing in the potency of matter, so essence can be changed when the being has
    not been changed but forced back into the potency of essence‚Äîalthough,
    to tell the truth, these ‚Äòforcings back‚Äô are easier to say than to understand.
10. Also, while the bread‚Äôs being cannot be changed into uncreated being, it
    can still be changed into a created essence because these share in matter
   and can be transformed into one another, granted that they do not share in
    proximate matter but only in distal matter, just as an element can be
    changed into something alive and the reverse. But the bread‚Äôs being and
    the being of Christ‚Äôs body agree in prime matter, which  is the distal

      potency of both, although they do not agree proximately because the
       matter is pure potency while the essence is potency with act. Indeed, the
       contrary reasoning, if it were good in any way, would also conclude that
      from all the food that Christ ate before he suffered, only the essence was

          it to remain. But just as, in my view, it is plainly wrong to say that it is
       necessary for it to remain, it is likewise no less wrong to say that it cannot
       remain.


        Tenth Question: On the Words of Consecration


   1. The words this  is my body that are said in the consecration are taken
      materially and not as signifying.
   2. These teachers have said that this conclusion is scandalous and contrary to
      the general view of saintly teachers and that the view of the glossator on this
      point does not hold. But I say that their Ô¨Ånding is scandalous and contrary
      to the view of many experts, and that the view of the Gloss on this point
      rather than theirs should be held, which will be clear from the evidence once
      the conclusion has been understood.
   3. The Ô¨Årst thing to note is that I do not understand these words to have been
     taken materially when uttered by Christ but only when uttered by a priest of
      the Gospel when consecrating, and therefore in my conclusion I have said
      the words . . . said in the consecration‚Äîmeaning those uttered now by those
    who consecrate. But it is certain that a priest when consecrating narrates
      Christ‚Äôs aforesaid words so that the phrase uttered by that priest supposits
      for the phrase uttered by Christ and taken as signifying, and this view not
     only is not erroneous or scandalous but rather the opposite view plainly
      contradicts the aforesaid sense of the form of consecration in which the
      priest says those words as a narration.
   4. For the priest when consecrating narrates the take and eat and so on that
      Christ spoke to the apostles. But the consecrating priest does not speak to
      the apostles and says take and so on to them only as a narration. For only
      the priest receives the sacrament, and he is not also talking to the servers

## Page 212

Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù©Ôù≥ÔùßÔùØÔù§‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù¢ÔùØÔù§Ôùπ?  199


  when he says take and eat and so on. Likewise, when he says this is my body,
  what is narrated by the priest is the phrase this is my body taken materially
   for a like phrase made of the like words uttered by Christ as signifying,
  which is also clear through the words said before or after consecrating the
   blood, namely which for you and for many is shed and so on. For they would
   be false if taken as signifying because Christ‚Äôs blood is certainly not shed just
   then, so those words are taken only as a narration.
5. But what is narrated except a narrator‚Äôs statement that supposits for a
   statement made by another person? Accordingly, this narrative statement
   about another‚Äôs statement should be taken materially. Also, if these words
   were taken not as narrating but as signifying, the shedding of Christ‚Äôs blood
   for others‚Äînot for the one consecrating‚Äîwould be indicated by saying
   which for you and for many is shed, although which for us and so on ought to
   be said.
6. Accordingly, my reason for making these statements is not that I believe
   that the opposite view cannot also be defended but in order to make it clear
   that my view is not only Catholic but also more acceptable than its opposite.
  From all this it is clear that the present conclusion is not scandalous but
   acceptable and that the view of the ofÔ¨Åcial glossator On Consecration in
   Distinction 2, in the canon Fear, should not be rejected on this point but
   approved‚Äîjust as until now it never has been rejected by the Church.

## Page 213

5
         Is Heresy Willful? Pico‚Äôs Q8



                      5.1 Acts and Attitudes


This is the thesis that Pico defended in the eighth Question (Q8) of the Apology:


   Just as no one‚Äôs opinion is exactly thus and so (praecise sic opinatur) because he
  wants to have this opinion (vult sic opinari), so no one believes (credit) it to be
   true thus and so exactly (praecise) because he wants to believe it so (vult sic
   credere).


As in the Conclusions, he followed with a corollary:


   It is not in a person‚Äôs free power (libera potestate) to believe (credere) an article of
   faith (articulum Ô¨Ådei) to be true when he decides (sibi placet) and to believe
   (credere) it not to be true when he decides.


But in the Apology he omitted a passage that preceded the slightly different thesis
in the Conclusions:


   I state this as acceptable (probabiliter), and I would assert the opposite Ô¨Årmly
   (Ô¨Årmiter) except that theologians generally say it that way; I assert nonetheless
   that this statement is acceptable in itself (in se esse probabile), and this is it: just as
  no one has an opinion that something is thus and so (aliquid ita esse) exactly
   because he wants to have this opinion, so no one believes that something is true
   exactly because he wants to believe it is true.

   Corollary: It is not in a person‚Äôs free power to believe an article of faith to be true
  when he pleases and to believe that it is false (falsum) when he decides.

The wording of the thesis in both versions is forced‚Äînote the placement of
‚Äúexactly (praecise),‚Äù to be discussed later‚Äîand the confusing preamble heightens
the strain: maybe Pico was wise to leave it out. As a matter of framing, however,
his point was consequential: labeling the thesis ‚Äúacceptable‚Äù rather than ‚Äòdemon-
strated‚Äô or ‚Äòcertain‚Äô made it less provocative. Other small differences in wording
between the Conclusions and the Apology could also have led somewhere: maybe
Pico‚Äôs judges would think that a ‚Äúfalse‚Äù belief, for example, was not the same as a

Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù≥ÔùπÔù∑Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù¶ÔùµÔù¨? Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØ‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù±Ôôã  201

‚Äònot true‚Äô belief. The thesis and corollary defended by the Apology, in any case,
were those printed there as Pico‚Äôs position on ‚Äúfreedom of believing‚Äù‚Äîde libertate
credendi. The opening topics were belief, faith, opinion, and constraints on willing
them freely.¬π
  Philosophers today treat believing as a mental state‚Äîa believer‚Äôs attitude,
explicit or implicit, about a proposition (p). Someone who believes that p has a
believing attitude about p and/or the state of affairs that p represents. To believe
that p  is a positive attitude; other attitudes, negative as well as positive, are
doubting, fearing, hoping, knowing, suspecting, trusting, wishing, worrying, and
so on. If the list were in Greek, both the noun pistis and the verb pisteu√¥ could add
the crucial (for Pico) idea of faith. An inexact noun/verb parallel in Latin is Ô¨Ådes/
Ô¨Ådere, but in English there is no verb ‚Äòto faith‚Äô: instead, we ‚Äòhave faith‚Äô that p.
Having faith that p is a kind of believing that p, especially in matters of religion.¬≤
   If I believe that p, am I right to do so? An epistemic evaluation will focus on
reasons and evidence. But is believing that p doing the right thing? A moral
evaluation may also be in order. Answers to such questions might contribute to
an ethics of belief, a doxastic ethics‚Äî‚Äòdoxastic‚Äô because the topic is right or wrong
belief: doxa in Greek, which is also common or popular opinion. Pico‚Äôs thesis (Q8T)
and its corollary (Q8C) are statements that bear on doxastic ethics, a modern label
unavailable to the prince for his medieval moral principles.¬≥
  The contemporary consensus is that belief and other propositional attitudes are
mental states that are often implicit, not made explicit by deliberation, nor
occurrent or under active consideration. You probably believe that‚Äîunlike
Doctor T in the Ô¨Ålm from the 1950s‚Äîyou have fewer than 5000 Ô¨Ångers, even if
this belief has never occurred to you. You now have and have had a belief that you
have not chosen to have: your belief about your Ô¨Ångers is tacit and involuntary.
With so  little at stake, are you morally responsible for it? Suppose you also
believe‚Äîin the same unconscious way‚Äîthat women should serve men because
males are naturally superior? Aristotle‚Äôs reÔ¨Çexive views about women and slaves
were just as disgraceful, and he made them explicit. Were he and other sages
responsible for believing that an enslaved human was a ‚Äúpiece of property‚Äù? I hope
so. But what about you and your Ô¨Ångers?‚Å¥
  A feature of beliefs, apart from moral standing, is epistemic standing. Belief,
faith, and opinion‚Äîpropositional attitudes that Ô¨Ågure in Pico‚Äôs Q8‚Äîare epistem-
ically and psychologically different: a belief is Ô¨Årmer than an opinion, for example.
Are propositional attitudes also comparable in other ways? Is an opinion a type of
belief, or the reverse or neither, and how does each compare with knowledge?
Pursuing such questions, modern philosophers have treated knowing as a type of

   Faith, along with other propositional attitudes, still gets the attention provoked
by William Clifford‚Äôs celebrated declaration in 1877: ‚Äúit is wrong always, every-

people are sometimes obliged, not just allowed, to believe without sufÔ¨Åcient

ethical approaches to questions of evidence.‚Å∂
   If your epistemic attitude to evidence were involuntary‚Äîlike your belief about
Ô¨Ångers‚Äîyou might not be responsible for it. On the other hand, if your attitude
were voluntary, you could be responsible. Suppose that scientists have a strange
theory (like quantum entanglement) backed by experimental evidence and a
consensus of experts. One such expert, with no better theory of her own, might
not believe the consensus and yet accept  it for practical purposes‚Äîto design
experiments, perhaps. An acceptance of this sort would be a voluntary propos-
itional attitude‚Äîlike choosing to accept that God  exists, as a profession of
religious doctrine, while not actually believing in God. A plausible but meager
faith could deem the doctrine true‚Äîaccepting it by policy or stipulation, without
conviction.‚Å∑
   If faith was the propositional attitude in question, feelings or affects might
prevail over thoughts or perceptions, and epistemic questions might recede. If
cognitive attitudes were salient, however, most philosophers today would treat
them as mental states, not acts‚Äîunlike Pico, whose Q8 examines an ‚Äúact of
believing (actus credendi)‚Äù in relation to an ‚Äúact of the intellect (actus intellectus)‚Äù
and an ‚Äúact of will (actus voluntatis).‚Äù Pico thought of faith‚Äîalong with belief,
knowledge, and opinion‚Äîas primarily a mental act, not a mental state. Moreover,
faith, knowledge and opinion were not isolated epistemic categories in his medi-
eval theory of mind. He and his scholastic teachers graded them from most to least
secure, where the latter‚Äîlike doubt, opinion and suspicion‚Äîwavered between
contradictory propositional options: ‚Äúfearing the opposite,‚Äù as Pico said, stuck
between p and not-p by a formido ad oppositum.‚Å∏

Understanding (Ôù≠Ôù©)‚Äîwhich Thomas called intelligere and intellectus‚Äîis direct

On Truth was to explicate a deÔ¨Ånitional statement by Augustine about belief: ‚Äúto
believe is to think while assenting (credere est cum assensione cogitare),‚Äù where
cogitare, unlike  intelligere or  intueri,  is  deliberative. The other element  of
Augustine‚Äôs deÔ¨Ånition was assensio, an act of will necessary for faith.
  For Thomas assent had to be more robust than acceptance in today‚Äôs doxastic
ethics. Belief in God as a mere policy, without conviction, would have been thin
gruel for his ancient faith of martyrs. And simple mental acts at level Ôù°, which
grasp non-propositional objects, were no good for assent because they could not
tell true statements about God from false statements. At the Ôù™-level, by contrast,
opinions (Ôù≠ÔùØ) were propositional yet insecure‚Äîhence also unreliable for assent.
Three other Ôù™-level acts‚Äîunderstanding (Ôù≠Ôù©), knowing (Ôù≠Ôù≥), and believing (Ôù≠Ôù£)‚Äî
were more secure than opinion. But Augustine said that believing (credere) needed
both assent and deliberation (cogitare), thereby excluding non-deliberative, Ôù°-level
acts of understanding (Ôù≠Ôù©) from belief, hence from faith as a kind of belief. Both
parts  of  Augustine‚Äôs  deÔ¨Ånition,  however‚Äîassent  along  with  deliberative
thinking‚Äîwere present at both Ôù≠Ôù£and Ôù≠Ôù≥levels as belief and knowledge.¬π¬π
  But the type of deliberative activity‚Äîrigorous demonstrative reasoning‚Äî
needed for knowledge (scientia) left less room for volition than faith required,
according to Thomas: faith, given by God, also had to be willed and could not be
proved like a theorem of geometry. Only at the Ôù≠Ôù£-level were non-demonstrable
grounds for faith available as belief, which was also acceptable (probabalis),
though not demonstrable, as an object of the will‚Äôs assent. This is Thomas‚Äôs
dense summary, starting with an act of the intellect and its religious object:


  As far as this comes from the intellect itself, the intellect is not satisÔ¨Åed by it, does
  not reach its end (terminus) just on its own part and gets to it only from outside.
  And this is why a believer‚Äôs intellect (intellectus credentis) is said to be made
   captive (captivatus)‚Äîbecause it is held to another‚Äôs ends, not its own, as in 2
   Corinthians 10:55: bringing the whole intellect into captivity and so on. This is
   also why in a believer (credens)‚Äîthough not in one who understands (intelligens)
   or knows (sciens)‚Äîmovements can arise contrary to what one holds very Ô¨Årmly.
  By assent (assensum), then, believing (credere) is separated from the activity
   (operatio) whereby the intellect looks at (inspicit) simple forms‚Äîwhatnesses
   (quidditates)‚Äîand is also separated from doubting (dubitatio) and from having
  an opinion (opinio); but deliberative thinking (cogitatio) separates belief from
  understanding  (intellectus); while having assent (assensus) and deliberation
   together and in equal measure separates belief from knowledge (scientia).¬π¬≤

suggests no epistemic wavering. But Hugh also offered a sharper deÔ¨Ånition of faith

  Hugh‚Äôs account of faith was the best available when Peter Lombard addressed
hard questions about Christ‚Äôs suffering and death. How could it be that ‚ÄúGod

  Paraphrasing Hebrews 11, the Lombard afÔ¨Årmed that ‚Äúfaith is correctly called

religious kind. In a looser sense, faith or trust is just a quality (qualitas) of
mind‚Äîa mental state. But the faith needed for salvation and given by divine
grace is an active virtue (virtus) animated by love of God, not a passive attitude.
Like knowledge or belief, trust can result from any soul‚Äôs mental power (also

theologica). Nonetheless, even evil demons have a perverse faith about‚Äînot in,
some would say‚Äîthe God who has put them in Hell to ‚Äúbelieve and tremble.‚Äù¬π‚Å∏
   Peter Lombard‚Äôs theology was a full-time job for Aquinas when he lectured on

career, in the second part of the great Summa, he returned to the problem of faith
to study it again in painstaking detail and at great length. He punctuated his

208  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨


long analysis with summary statements about topics that Pico would address in
Q8‚Äîmental acts, habits, and objects of assent, belief, faith, intellect, knowledge,
opinion, and will that might or might not be virtuous and meritorious for
salvation:


   Faith (Ô¨Ådes) is half-way between knowledge (scientia) and opinion (opinio) . . . .
   Therefore, since knowledge and opinion are about statements (enuntiabilia),
   clearly faith is likewise about statements, and so the object of faith (objectum
   Ô¨Ådei) is something complex (complexum).

  The act of faith (actus Ô¨Ådei) is to believe (credere), . . . which is an act of the
   intellect delimited (actus intellectus determinati) to one item from a command of
   the will (ex imperio voluntatis). Hence an act of faith is directed (habet ordinem)
  both to the will‚Äôs object (objectum), which is a good and an end (bonum et Ô¨Ånis),
  and to the intellect‚Äôs object, which is something true (verum).

   Faith is the habit of mind (habitus mentis) whereby eternal life begins in us,
  making the intellect assent (assentire) to things that are not apparent (non
   apparentibus).

   Believing (credere) is itself an act of the intellect that assents (actus intellectus
   assentientis) to divine truth from a command of the will moved (ex imperio
   voluntatis motae) by God‚Äôs grace, so that the act is subject to free will (subjacet
   libero arbitrio) as directed (in ordine) toward God.

  A person who has an eager (prompta) will to believe cherishes the truth that is
   believed, deliberates (cogitat) about it and embraces any reasons that can be
  found for it.

   Believing is an act of the intellect in keeping with its being moved (movetur) to
   assent by the will, for an act of this sort proceeds (procedit) both from the will
  and from the intellect, each of which is naturally completed by a habit (natum
   est per habitum perÔ¨Åci). This is why, for an act of faith to be complete, there
  must be a habit both in the will and in the intellect . . . . But believing is an act of
   the intellect directly (immediate) because the object of that act is something
   true, which is the intellect‚Äôs special province. And this is why faith, which is the
   special origin (principium) of that act, must be in the intellect as in a subject (in
   subjecto).

Assent, belief, and faith, as Thomas described them, are ‚Äúabout statements.‚Äù From
our perspective, they are positive propositional attitudes, just as doubt, fear and
worry are negative attitudes: ‚ÄúI doubt that tomorrow will come‚Äù differs in this way
from ‚ÄúI believe that tomorrow will come.‚Äù But Thomas focused on such positive
attitudes as acts, not states, of intellect and will. These two powers of mind
cooperate in acts of belief, some of which‚Äîwhen ‚Äúdivine truth‚Äù is the object‚Äî
are acts of faith.

Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù≥ÔùπÔù∑Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù¶ÔùµÔù¨? Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØ‚ÄôÔù≥Ôù±Ôôã  209

  In general, the intellect‚Äôs object is ‚Äúsomething true,‚Äù hence propositional. But
the will‚Äôs special object is ‚Äúa good and an end,‚Äù not as such propositional. The
intellect ‚Äúassents to divine truth,‚Äù however, only if the will moves and commands
