the mind, and the soul. When faith in this truth results, its location is the intellect,
where faith exists ‚Äúas in a subject,‚Äù completing a transaction initiated by the will‚Äôs
command, so that the act of belief that gains assent as faith is ‚Äúsubject to free will.‚Äù
The will‚Äôs command has ‚Äúdelimited‚Äù the intellect‚Äôs act to one of many possible
objects‚Äîa holy truth.¬π‚Åπ
  According to Thomas, will and intellect are sites of acts, not states, of mind:
accordingly their operations are not primarily attitudinal. But he also describes
faith as a ‚Äúhabit of mind‚Äù located ‚Äúboth in the will and in the intellect.‚Äù Elsewhere,
not in his discourse on faith, habits are effective (efÔ¨Åcient) causes of acts, and acts
are Ô¨Ånal causes or purposes of habits: a habit is ‚Äúa kind of disposition directed to a
thing‚Äôs nature and to its activity or purpose.‚Äù Through experience and education,
then, people acquire dispositions to act or not act in various ways. But this natural
and cultural habituation does not incline sinners effectively toward faith, a habit
given only by God‚Äôs grace‚Äîa theological virtue not gained by human effort alone.
This habit is the ‚Äúformed‚Äù faith that love inspires, unlike the ‚Äúformless‚Äù faith
possessed even by hateful demons, who can have the right habit intellectually but
cannot will the love that makes it effective.¬≤‚Å∞
  The human intellect, without help from the will, does not love, desire, or
choose: its special object is the true, not the good. Prompted by the senses, it
sees (videt) possible objects mentally as propositions in contradictory pairs, p or
not p, of which only one is true. Such sights are steps in a perceptual process with
sensory as well as intellectual phases, according to Thomas. But in religion no
seeing, whether ocular or mental, is grounds for trust (Ô¨Ådes) or even opinion
(opinio), he insisted: scripture says that faith (Ô¨Ådes) is ‚Äúevidence (argumentum) of
things not seen (non apparentium).‚Äù Assent to this sacred unseen evidence hap-
pens not perceptually or intellectually but


   voluntarily by a choice (electio) that turns to one side rather than the other. And
    if this happens when there is doubt and wavering from fear (dubitatio et formido)
   of the other side, it will be opinion (opinio), but if it happens with certainty
   (certitudo) and without such wavering, it will be trust (Ô¨Ådes).

  Like understanding (Ôù≠Ôù©) and knowing (Ôù≠Ôù≥), believing (Ôù≠Ôù£) that p or that not p is

Like knowledge, faith is certain, whereas weak and wavering opinions are uncer-
tain. But faith, arising from an act of will, differs from knowledge by not assenting
under epistemic compulsion: faith is willed believing, not forced knowing, and this
blessed believing originates in a ‚Äúcompleted will‚Äù that opinion lacks‚Äîas it also
lacks merit. Nonetheless, Thomas did not exclude all volition from opinion: an
‚Äúuncompleted‚Äù will, disposed by habit to want an educable opinion, might put the
intellect on a path to faith through opinion and then belief. Faith, belief and
opinion are all positive, in any case, and Thomas found them all helpfully, if not
fully, attuned or at least attunable to virtue and salvation.¬≤¬≥

Thomas saw them as coordinated acts of intellect and will‚Äîacts, more or less
Ô¨Årm, of the same kind: mental acts. Faith was such an act‚Äîplainly volitional‚Äîand
since faith was a kind of belief, at least one way of believing was volitional also.

perhaps somewhat volitional, as Thomas suggested. But the suggestion clashed

                   5.2 Faith, Belief, and Opinion


Q8, like other Questions in the Apology, opens with a thesis that Pico had to
defend, Q8T, but also with the corollary, Q8C, that went along with Q8T in the

nothing exceptional‚Äîat Ô¨Årst glance‚Äîabout stating Q8C along with Q8T. The main
contents of Q8T are believing (credere), opining (opinari), and willing (velle). Belief
and faith (Ô¨Ådes) are explicit in Q8C, where willing is ‚Äúdeciding‚Äù (sibi placere) by the

  Q8T Just as no one‚Äôs opinion is exactly thus and so because he wants to have

Like hundreds of other theses in the Conclusions, Q8T is telegraphic: it‚Äôs hard to
know, for example, exactly what Pico meant by using ‚Äúexactly‚Äù (praecise) twice in
its nineteen words. Praecise is an adverb, which ‚ÄòmodiÔ¨Åes‚Äô a verb, an adjective or
another adverb. Where praecise comes up for the second time in Q8T, the nearest
eligible word is vult, a verb: hence, ‚Äúexactly because he wants (vult).‚Äù But praecise
in its Ô¨Årst occurrence‚Äîpraecise sic opinatur‚Äîis closer to another adverb,  sic:
hence, ‚Äúexactly thus and so.‚Äù However, neither English phrase conveys the core
sense of praecise, from the verb praecidere‚Äîwhose parts (prae- ‚Äòin front,‚Äô -cido ‚Äòcut
off ‚Äô) suggest not just ‚Äúexactly‚Äù but ‚Äúexactly and only.‚Äù¬≤‚Å∑
  Pico would have seen precisely this use of praecise, in precisely the same
context, in Cardinal d‚ÄôAilly‚Äôs Sentences commentary, which he cited in Q8. After
concluding that the will ‚Äúcan turn the intellect‚Äù toward one object or another, the
Cardinal denied ‚Äúthat the will can do this exactly (praecise) by its command‚Äù‚Äî
more like the second praecise in Q8. Pico‚Äôs predicament was that he had not
written his 900 theses to withstand such Ô¨Åne scrutiny in a judicial setting. He had
hoped for a vigorous but collegial academic exchange‚Äînot the prosecution that he
faced in 1487. In this legal combat, adverbs were weaponized.¬≤‚Å∏
  As usual in the Apology, Pico taunted the court about Q8T by describing his
thesis as ‚Äúnot only Catholic but entirely correct and far more correct than its
opposite.‚Äù Making the same claim for Q8C, he reminded his judges that this
corollary was an inference from the main thesis and therefore no less impeccable.
In one sense, he was simply saying what a corollary is‚Äîa derived proposition (q)
that follows uncontroversially from a previous proposition (p) already established.
In this case, if p as Q8T were true by stipulation, q as Q8C would also be true if the
inference was good. In effect, Pico claimed that the truth-values of the two
propositions‚ÄîQ8T and Q8C‚Äîwere tied inferentially: the truth of the main thesis
secured the truth of the corollary.¬≤‚Åπ

214  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

  The main thesis itself wasn‚Äôt a risky proposition: the stakes were just opinions
and beliefs, attitudes that need not have threatened religion. But when Pico moved
on in the corollary to ‚Äúa person‚Äôs free power to believe an article of faith,‚Äù
Christianity itself was on the line. This was Bishop Garcia‚Äôs analysis: ‚Äúalthough
the  conclusion‚Äù‚ÄîQ8T‚Äî‚Äúfrom  which  the  aforesaid  proposition‚Äù‚ÄîQ8C‚Äî‚Äúis
derived might be acceptable in both parts, the proposition itself‚Äù‚ÄîQ8C‚Äî‚Äúas it
falls out of the conclusion‚Äù‚ÄîQ8T‚Äî‚Äúseems erroneous to me and suggestive of
heresy.‚Äù Garcia eventually changed his mind about condoning Q8T, and he never
doubted that Q8C was wicked because of what it impugned: the faith of the one,
holy, universal, apostolic Church.¬≥‚Å∞
  The defendant treasured his faith, but he wanted to distance it more from acts
of will than Thomists like Garcia would allow. The Q8T and Q8C propositions
were tied inferentially, according to Pico. If so, and if the restrictions on belief and
opinion in Q8T were correct, then the restriction on faith in Q8C would also be
correct, and faith could not be had just by making a decision. Since Pico did not
make the structure of his inference explicit, the logic of the claim is hard to
evaluate: evidently he thought that exclusions deÔ¨Åned by praecise in Q8T carried
over to Q8C‚Äîand that they were exculpatory.
   After restating Q8T, he called on Aristotle to show that this Catholic thesis was
not only orthodox but also ‚Äúentirely correct‚Äù as a philosophical matter. Since the
main thesis itself said nothing about faith, a pagan Philosopher could testify
authoritatively about  it, and so could  Averroes,  his Commentator.  Citing
Aristotle On the Soul by way of the Muslim‚Äôs Long Commentary, Pico had backing
from respected non-Christian experts for his statement that opinions are unwilled.
No one had ever disproved this claim, he added, ‚Äúand all our theologians have
approved it unanimously.‚Äù Unanimity is not a philosophical habit. But Aquinas,
in his commentary on De anima, had indeed summarized the crucial passage

  Robert Holcot‚Äôs perspective on the same passage was different:


  On the second book of De anima, comment 153, the Commentator aims to prove
   that imagination is not opinion . . . . We can imagine as we wish when we have the

   opinion as we wish because there is no opinion except of what appears true to
   us, and so opinion is not imagining . . . . Obviously it‚Äôs clear from this reasoning
   that, according to Aristotle and the Commentator, it‚Äôs not in a person‚Äôs free
  power (libera potestate) to have an opinion when he wants (vult).

                  5.3 The Will in Five Arguments

Having Ô¨Årst denied that will can command belief by the G-level mental activity of
direct intuition, Pico then extended his claim to faith: ‚Äújust to grasp terms, along
with a simple command from the will, is not enough to cause assent to faith.‚Äù This
was the view endorsed by ‚Äúexperts whom I follow,‚Äù he explained, calling their
position ‚Äúacceptable and reasonable‚Äù‚Äîthough not proved or certain. An eminent
witness for this doxastic anti-voluntarism was Augustine, a revered Doctor of the
Church often cited by Holcot and others. But Augustine was a saint who Ô¨Ålled a
book with retractions of his own teachings, which Pico had catalogued in Q1.
Although he expressed no doubts about the great Bishop in Q8, he planned to
proceed ‚Äúnot only on Augustine‚Äôs authority . . . but also by very strong reasons
(rationes).‚Äù He laid out Ô¨Åve such reasons‚ÄîÔ¨Åve arguments‚Äîto show why faith
needs more than just grasping by the intellect plus a command from the will.‚Å¥‚Å∞
  The Ô¨Årst argument was textbook logic again‚Äîthis time the logic of places or
topics, which was still a university subject in Pico‚Äôs day. To teach the abstract
principle that Pico cited, which was a topical rule or maxim, Peter of Spain had
used a concrete example: ‚Äúa king can‚Äôt take the fort; therefore, neither can a
soldier.‚Äù The label for this place or topic was ‚Äúfrom the greater (a maiori)‚Äù because
it started from something ‚Äúmore (magis),‚Äù the king, and went to something ‚Äúless
(minus),‚Äù the soldier. Peter‚Äôs maxim‚Äîone of many inherited from Aristotle by
Boethius‚Äîwas close to Pico‚Äôs: ‚Äúif what plainly belongs there more (magis videtur
inesse) is not there, neither is what belongs less (minus).‚Äù‚Å¥¬π
   Fort-taking, as in Peter‚Äôs example, belongs more to a king than to a soldier;
hence, where a king lacks this capability, a soldier also lacks it. Pico recited the
topical maxim and applied it to his problem about opinion and belief, describing it
as follows (with my numbering):

   (1Œ±) It‚Äôs not in a person‚Äôs free power (in libera potestate) to have one opinion or
  another when he wants; (1Œ≤) therefore, it‚Äôs not in a person‚Äôs free power to
  have one belief or another when he wants. (2) The inference is clear because
    if something plainly belongs more (magis videtur inesse) and  is not there,

mentioned ‚Äúevidence (argumentum)‚Äù of these holy non-appearances, but Thomas

walked on the water in Galilee, the apostles could not see the earthly Jerusalem

within reach epistemically: as they moved closer, the shining city would also
become visually apparent. By contrast, the content of s itself‚Äîas apart from
apparent evidence in favor of s‚Äîwas nothing apparent at all. The truth of s was
non-apparent, and believing that s could assent in faith to a non-apparent truth.‚Å¥‚Å¥
   Since the good, not the true, was the will‚Äôs proper object, the truth of s was
beyond reach of the will alone. Nor could the will by itself, without help from other
faculties of the soul, acquire apparent evidence for believing that  s. Having
suggested this crucial point, without explicitly making it‚Äîmuch less proving
it‚ÄîPico dropped the debate about opinion from his next four arguments.

only the mind‚Äôs eye, not the body‚Äôs, could judge such an appearance true or false.

character of appearances because sharing this feature with beliefs would make
appearances no more primitive than beliefs‚Äîpsychologically or epistemically.
And yet by deriving a feature of beliefs from appearances, he located appearances
upstream from  beliefs  in  the  doxastic Ô¨Çow. Even  farther upstream were
representations.
  For s or not s to appear to the intellect, according to Pico, was to be represented

  On Pico‚Äôs theory, appearances get to the intellect via sub-doxastic representa-

  Breathing this thin dialectical air, Pico next scaled a cliff of abstractions in his
third argument: he moved from representations to mental presentations as expli-

as relevant just because the prince was philosophizing about mind. Like many

‚Äòmaterial‚Äô or ‚Äòproductive‚Äô and so on‚Äîincluding a ‚Äòpossible‚Äô or ‚Äòpotential‚Äô intellect.

  An absolutely pure potency  is indifferent to any act, including the act of
intellect that is a belief‚Äîbelieving that s or that not s, for example. In a thoroughly
neutral state of potency, the (possible or potential) intellect acts in favor of neither
option because it does not act at all. In order to proceed to action, this passive
intellect needs something that  it cannot supply on  its own. The need  is for
something not volitional but intellectual‚Äîsomething to tip the balance toward s
or not s‚Äîand the will can‚Äôt supply it.
  Although the intellect might eventually need the will in order to act doxasti-

can recognize it as typed, as an item of species Ôù∏rather than species Ôùπ, Ôù∫and so on.
Before the will can move the intellect toward believing that s or that not s, such
options must already have been delimited‚Äîthough not by willing‚Äîfrom among

article of faith‚Äîcan‚Äôt happen exactly and only (praecise) because of what a person

   that Muslims or Jews act unreasonably (irrationabiliter) in that they do not

   exactly (praecise) because that person would not believe, this would be exactly
   because (praecise quia) I wanted (vellem) what the person did not want (vellet).
  Both acts of will would be tyrannical (actus tirannicus voluntatis), and neither

   either case, merely power (potestas) and will‚Äôs tyrannical command (imperium).


With help from Albert the Great, Pico turned this Christian prejudice into a

   ‚Ä¢ The believing of someone who, without any reason, wills to believe that s or
     that not s is tyrannical willing.
   ‚Ä¢ Muslims and Jews will to believe that not s without any reason.
   ‚Ä¢ Therefore, this unreasonable believing by Muslims and Jews is tyrannical
      willing.

This, in Pico‚Äôs words, was the upshot:


  Given two persons, one of whom wants to believe that a is true and the other does
  not want to believe it, this says that one of them surely wants it more reasonably
  and the other less reasonably. Therefore, they are not delimited to wanting one
   thing or another exactly because they want it that way (quia praecise sic volunt).

It‚Äôs reasonable, not tyrannical, to choose Jesus as the Savior and for proposition s
(a in Pico‚Äôs text) to say so not because of wanting s but because it‚Äôs true. Thus the
prince argued for a Ô¨Åfth time against believing by wanting or willing‚Äîif the will
was in charge exactly and only.‚Åµ¬π


                        5.4 Holcot‚Äôs Belief

Summing up his case against willed believing, Pico mentioned ‚Äúmany other
arguments‚Äù that he had not introduced, wishing ‚Äúnot to drag this material out‚Äù
because he had done ‚Äúenough to defend my conclusion.‚Äù He made an exception,
however, for Robert Holcot‚Äîa Dominican theologian with ‚Äúgreat Ô¨Ånesse‚Äù who
‚Äúheld unqualiÔ¨Åedly (simpliciter) that the act of believing is not free.‚Äù Holcot‚Äôs
sweeping position, as Pico described it, contrasted with his own narrow rejection
of willed believing, which he qualiÔ¨Åed again and again with the praecise restriction
that he derived from Q8T. Praecise and its relatives are thematic in Q8, where they
appear fourteen times in just a few pages.‚Åµ¬≤
   Holcot‚Äôs opposition to willed believing was adamant. By comparison, Pico‚Äôs
position was negotiable: the Q8T thesis plus its corollary, Q8C, were both restricted
by praecise. And unlike Pico‚Äôs presentation, Holcot‚Äôs account of faith, belief, will,
and freedom was long, complex and subtle. This is a summary in Holcot‚Äôs words:


  My general question is whether the will (voluntas) by its own command (imper-
   ium), because of the promise of eternal life, could cause assent (assensus) in the
   intellect regarding some proposition that was as doubtful in itself as before the
  command. In order to understand this, I take the negative side on this issue: in

   particular, that believing (credere) in articles of faith, or any proposition, is not in

As elsewhere in the Apology when other theologians were his sources, Pico relied
on Holcot tactically in Q8: ‚ÄúI must say,‚Äù speaking of Holcot, ‚ÄúI‚Äôve never read that
he was condemned on this issue‚Äù‚Äîmeaning doxastic involuntarism. Holcot‚Äôs
contemporaries resisted his notorious and uncompromising views on willed belief,
and yet he was not condemned. Then what grounds could there be for calling
Pico‚Äôs compromise heretical?‚Åµ¬≥
  One issue that Holcot addressed was the merit earned for salvation by belief.
A plausible way to assign merit was to grade belief as more or less free or unfree.
More freedom to believe that s would bring more merit for doing the right thing
despite pressure and believing that s‚Äîand for not believing that not s. But if there
were no freedom at all to choose between s and not s‚Äîhence no room for a will to
believe‚Äîa believing act would neither have merit nor lack it. Holcot‚Äôs solution,
according to Pico, was to award merit for obedience, not to attach it to freedom:
‚Äúan act is meritorious not because it‚Äôs free but because it conforms to the law that
promises a reward, . . . eternal life.‚Äù‚Åµ‚Å¥
  But the Creator could change his law in ways that creatures might Ô¨Ånd capri-
cious. God could rule, for example, that someone who ‚Äútrips over the edge of a stone
tomorrow would be saved, . . . and then that false step would merit eternal life . . . not
as free but as accepted by God.‚Äù If a human stumbles exactly as a horse falters, only
an unreasoning body would fall, but ‚ÄúGod can accept physical acts just as if they
were voluntary.‚Äù To illustrate, Pico repeated Holcot‚Äôs account of the Gospel story
about Herod slaughtering babies in order to eliminate the infant Jesus: this ‚Äúdeath of
innocents who had no use of free choice‚Äù was meritorious yet not voluntary.‚Åµ‚Åµ
  From Augustine‚Äôs teachings Holcot extracted three senses of ‚Äòbelieving‚Äô: concur-
ring, testimonial, and miraculous‚Äîto use my terms. He linked the Ô¨Årst concurring
sense, which was ‚Äúto assent that something is the case as indicated by a propos-
ition,‚Äù with Augustine‚Äôs celebrated deÔ¨Ånition, ‚Äúto believe is to think while assenting
(credere est cum assensione cogitare)‚Äù‚Äîa deÔ¨Ånition that said nothing about free-
dom, as Holcot pointed out. He found a second, testimonial sense in Augustine‚Äôs
equally famous rhetorical question: ‚Äúwhat is faith but believing what you don‚Äôt see?‚Äù
This believing ‚Äúbased only on testimony‚Äù accepted propositions that took cogni-
zance of what the believer could not see or prove without help from others.
  The most potent believing came third in Holcot‚Äôs list. Belief of this miraculous
kind, by cooperating with the supernatural virtue of faith given by God‚Äôs grace,

                    5.5 Pico‚Äôs Eighth Question

          Eighth Question: On the Freedom of Believing

From the conclusion I infer this as a corollary:


   It is not in a person‚Äôs free power to believe an article of faith to be true when he
   decides and to believe it not to be true when he decides.

  2 These teachers judged that the proposition, along with its corollary, was in
error and suggestive of heresy. Yet I consider the main conclusion to be not only
Catholic but absolutely correct and far more correct than its opposite. And I say
the same of the corollary, rightly understood, whose meaning‚Äîtaken according to
my way of thinking‚Äîmust be taken in no other way than as following from the
main conclusion for which the corollary is proposed.
  3 Clearly, however, the conclusion is not only Catholic but also entirely correct
because in support of its Ô¨Årst part‚Äîwhich is that no one has an opinion that
something is thus and so exactly because he wants the opinion to be thus and so‚Äî
there is Aristotle in his second book On the Soul, in the text of comment 153,
where Aristotle states the difference between imagination and opinion. Not only
has this view of Aristotle‚Äôs not been rejected; on the contrary, all our theologians
have approved it unanimously.
  4 But the second part of the conclusion‚Äîthat no one believes something to be
true exactly because he wants to believe it is true‚Äîcomes from Augustine in many
places. He says quite frequently that someone cannot believe from free will‚Äî
purely from the will‚Äôs command, in other words, lacking persuasion by a reason or
motivation. And Pierre d‚ÄôAilly, Henry of Oyta and Henry of Hesse hold the entire
conclusion that summarizes this view of mine: once an unsettled proposition has
been stated, for someone to believe or disbelieve, it‚Äôs not enough simply for terms
to be grasped and for the will just to issue an order. Furthermore, when I was in
Paris, almost the whole University of Paris held this view. While many Catholic
experts also consider this proposition so correct that they treat its opposite as
actually impossible, I have yet to read an expert who contradicts this view.
  5 But the corollary proposition should not be taken and understood without
the restriction or delimitation stated in the antecedent: namely, that it‚Äôs not within
the will‚Äôs free power to believe an article of faith to be true whenever one decides and
to believe it to be false when one decides. Accordingly, this very person believes that
it‚Äôs not exactly from what the will decides that the intellect believes.
  6 And to understand one such proposition through another proposition linked
with it is not unusual. On the contrary, sacred scripture often assumes that a
phrase is understood through what precedes or follows. And doing so in our case
is extremely important because I do not propose to say that it is not within our
power and so on as stated by itself as a separate conclusion; instead, I propose it as
a corollary from the preceding conclusion.
  7 But if these people dislike taking the understanding of the present corollary

that saying something to be in freedom or in the will‚Äôs free power means two

  8 In one way because‚Äîwith grasping provided in the intellect and no other act
of the intellect required‚Äîit would be in the will‚Äôs commanding power to provide

power in this way. Nor is  it Ô¨Åtting for any directive act, meritorious or not
meritorious, to be in a person‚Äôs power in this manner. In fact, grasping the

is needed in addition, according to the citation from Augustine.
  9 In another way, something is said to be in the will‚Äôs free power because‚Äîwith
everything else provided that is needed for there to be such an act‚Äîthe will can
put it or not put it into existence, and the will is not obligated to this act, no matter

will‚Äôs free power. This is the meaning of the corollary proposition. And from these

  11 And the Ô¨Årst argument is this. It‚Äôs not in a person‚Äôs free power to have one

something belongs more and is not there, therefore and so on. But hanging on
to something by fearing the opposite is less than doing so without fear. So then,
the antecedent was expressed by Aristotle in the second book On the Soul, in the
text of comment 153. This is also clear from experience because whoever experi-
ences in himself that a proposition offered to him is doubtful, then, if it is simply
doubtful to him, he is inclined to assent to an opinion on neither side until
something newly apparent reaches him in addition through syllogistic reasoning,
intuitive recognition, the testimony of many, the authority of the speaker or
something similar.
  12 The second argument is that it is not in a person‚Äôs free power to make
something appear to be true to his own intellect and make it appear not to be true.
Therefore, it is not in a person‚Äôs power to believe one thing or another to be true or
not true. The inference holds because it‚Äôs a contradiction for someone to believe
something and assent to it and for it not to appear to him to be true.
  13 The hypothesis is accepted because, for this and that to appear one way or
another to the intellect is to be represented to the intellect as this or that, in one
way or the other, which cannot depend on the will‚Äôs command because this

## Page 243

230  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

precedes the will‚Äôs act. What conÔ¨Årms that there is this appearing in some such
way to the intellect is that the object represented is either like that, and clearly so,
or else something different: what makes the object believable or not believable is
that it is like that and understood as like that. But making the item to be such and
such or to be understood that way is not in the will‚Äôs power; therefore, for a thing
to appear as such to the intellect is not in the will‚Äôs command.
  14 This is the third argument. Whenever some potency is purely neutral and
indifferent to two subsequent and opposite acts, the potency cannot proceed to
either of them unless it was previously delimited by something that is a Ô¨Årst act or
takes the place of a Ô¨Årst act with respect to either of the subsequent acts. But it is
the case that the intellect  is a purely neutral potency and indifferent to the
specifying of any act subsequent to it. Therefore, the intellect can be delimited
to no such specifying unless something else occurs that is either a Ô¨Årst act or takes
the place of a Ô¨Årst act with respect to such a delimitation.
  15 Under this reasoning, I then assume this consequent: a pure act of will
cannot happen as the Ô¨Årst act with respect to specifying of the intellect‚Äôs acts.
Therefore, the intellect could not be delimited to that specifying at the will‚Äôs
command alone. The consequent  is known from the reasoning previously
assumed, and the antecedent is clear from the shared consensual declaration of
all the experts: that even though an act of the intellect depends on the will for its
operation, for specifying there is no such dependency.
  16 Granted these points, I make my case as follows. The act of assent and the
act of dissent are acts whose reasons are presented as opposites. Therefore, the
intellect could not be delimited to assent or not to assent at the will‚Äôs command.
Therefore, if someone, through the intellect, believes or does not believe an article
of faith to be true, it does not happen through this exactly‚Äîbecause he wants or
does not want to believe in this‚Äîbut from something else, neutral by  itself,
delimiting the intellect to the specifying of such an act.
  17 This is the fourth argument. A person is not delimited exactly by an act of
will to believe a particular article. When asked why he should believe it, he would
not pick out an exact act of will, so that when someone is asked why he believes in
the Christian faith rather than the faith of Muslims, he would not pick out an act
of will exactly. Hence, he was not inclined to this belief by an exact act of will.
Moreover, it is clear from experience that people do not pick out a pure act of
will‚Äîalso from statements by our theologians who commonly cite eight reasons
why we should believe in the Gospel: prophetic foretelling, scriptural harmony,
the authority of writers, the reasonable content, the unreasonableness of every
error, the Church‚Äôs stability and the clarity of miracles.
  18 This  is the Ô¨Åfth argument: whenever any two people are delimited to
believing in opposite ways, and each is thus delimited exactly because he wants
to be delimited in this way, with no judgment or persuasion by a reason making
any difference at all, one cannot say that this person is moved more reasonably to

believe pro or con. But given two persons, one of whom wants to believe that a is
true and the other does not want to believe it, this says that one of them surely
wants it more reasonably and the other less reasonably. Therefore, they are not
delimited to wanting one thing or another exactly because they want it that way.
  19 The major premiss of this argument is known because, as Albert says in his
third book On the Soul, since the will acts this way exactly because it wants to act

his acting is not according to a reason but is acting at pleasure. The minor is
obvious because we say every day that Muslims or Jews act unreasonably in that
they do not believe and that because we believe we do act reasonably. But if I were

concludes by saying that the act of believing is meritorious not as free but as
conforming to the divine law that promises the believer such a reward‚Äî

as if they were voluntary acts. And in this way God evidently accepted for eternal

did nothing unreasonable because his will is reason enough for everything that he

Conclusions

                 Pico Free and Unfree



                   C.1 Holcot‚Äôs Classicism

Pico‚Äôs response in the Apology to Robert Holcot reÔ¨Çected some habits, though not
others, of the author‚Äôs time and place: he made use of the theologian‚Äôs skillful
dialectics, but he said nothing about the classicism and literary talent applauded
centuries later by Holcot‚Äôs modern readers. For our ears the silence is loud because
the locus of the prince‚Äôs fame in modern times has been what we now call a
‚Äòrenaissance,‚Äô often understood as a rebirth of ancient Greco-Roman arts, letters
and learning. Since Pico has long been an icon of this classicism (or ‚Äòhumanism,‚Äô
as many call it), why did Holcot not interest the prince as a precursor of the
transformation of culture that changed so many things during his own brief
lifetime?
  In Pico‚Äôs day the most famous book by Holcot was his commentary on the Book
of Wisdom: students of literature still remember it as an inspiration for Chaucer.
The Liber Sapientiae or Wisdom of Solomon came into the Latin Vulgate from a
Greek text‚Äînot from a Hebrew scroll‚Äîthat echoed other wisdom literature in the
Septuagint. Although Holcot was aware of the shaky provenance, he wanted to
keep Wisdom in the Bible. But he had learned from Jerome that


   a certain Philo, a Jew very skilled at Greek, compiled this book titled Wisdom
  from many statements made by Solomon. Therefore Jerome says that this book is
   Philo‚Äôs, not Solomon‚Äôs, that it was not found with the Hebrew books nor should
    it be counted among sacred scriptures. Yet many clear points of evidence
  (argumenta) contradict this view. . . . It‚Äôs possible, nonetheless, that this Jew
   Philo, who was quite expert in the Greek tongue, brought out a book of
  Solomon‚Äôs in Greek‚Äîor of statements by him somehow included in a book‚Äî
  and that this was translated from Greek into Latin, and to this extent he might be
   called an author.¬π





   ¬π Holcot (1518b), fol. iiiv; Smalley (1960), pp. 148‚Äì65; Pratt (1977); Reese (1993); Watts (1994);
Slotemaker and Witt (2016), pp. 1‚Äì4.

A few pages later, still introducing Wisdom before explicating the book in detail,
Holcot meditated on the fortunes of texts:


  A stream gets its water from a source, and a scribe (scriptor) gets what he writes
   (scribit) from a master-copy (exemplar). So if a master-copy is corrupted and
  wrong (corruptum et falsum), the scribe has a good enough excuse even if the
  book that he writes is wrong. . . . Bookmakers (stationarii) usually take care that
   master-copies are corrected when they provide copies for scribes. This is how the
   author of this book began, wishing at every point to spread moral virtues through
   the whole body of the Church by telling judges, princes and prelates to ‚Äòlove
   justice.‚Äô


The author of Wisdom, as Holcot presented him, was like a vigilant maker of
books, keeping watch at the fountainhead of justice, who took care that his pages
would irrigate the Church with steady streams of virtue. The commentator‚Äôs
instincts, like Pico‚Äôs, were bookish. And he thought of bookmaking‚Äîeven for
books of scripture‚Äîas a risky task that might result in ‚Äúunjust editions,‚Äù as in
Auden‚Äôs lines on Housman:


             In savage foot-notes on unjust editions
            he timidly attacked the life he led.

Those ‚Äúsavage foot-notes‚Äù started to  pile up in Pico‚Äôs century‚Äînot yet in
Holcot‚Äôs‚Äîwhen Poggio Bracciolini, Agnolo Poliziano, Lorenzo Valla, and other
inhumane ‚Äòhumanists‚Äô traded eloquent invectives. But students of Holcot in our
time have seen him as a ‚Äúproto-humanist‚Äù who sometimes expressed himself in
the antiquarian and philological way of Pico‚Äôs Italian contemporaries. Holcot‚Äôs
hesitations about the authorship of Wisdom, for example, and his thoughts about
the book‚Äôs journey from Greek into Latin, were crude and Ô¨Çeeting anticipations of
Valla‚Äôs inquiries into the text of the New Testament.¬≤
  Holcot taught the Bible and the Sentences at Oxford until 1338, and then he
found a patron in Bishop Richard de Bury of Durham, a powerful and remarkably
learned prelate. The Bishop bought books and gathered a library for his clients:
Thomas Bradwardine, Walter  Burley, Walter Chatton, Richard  Kilvington,
Holcot, and a few other learned clerics. De Bury had met the young Petrarch,
another bibliophile, on a diplomatic mission to Avignon around 1330. Traces of
the Bishop‚Äôs books are visible throughout Holcot‚Äôs writings.¬≥



   ¬≤ Wisd. 1:1; Holcot (1518b),  fol.  ivv; Slotemaker and Witt (2016), pp. 133‚Äì5; Linde (2012);
W. H. Auden, ‚ÄúA. E. Housman.‚Äù

Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔù£Ôù¨ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆÔù≥  235

  Before he joined De Bury‚Äôs distinguished familia, Holcot had worked out his
theory of belief and faith while still teaching at Oxford. In that role, when he
presented ‚Äúmiracles and deeds of the ancients‚Äù as irresistible evidence for belief, he
was thinking mainly of Bible stories‚Äîlike Moses parting the waters or Jesus
walking across a lake. Other ‚Äúdeeds of the ancients‚Äù also impressed him, however,
and the heroes were not always Jews or Christians: the story of Lot‚Äôs wife turned to
salt reminded him of a tale about Ulysses‚Äîhow Circe changed the hero‚Äôs crew
into pigs. Holcot aimed to make such fragments of a deep past intelligible to
Christians and useful for salvation, relying not just on anthologies collected from
Cicero and Vergil but also on less renowned sages like Aulus Gellius. Other friars
were his main audience: he showed them how to preach better sermons by
reinforcing Christian morals with pagan wisdom. Likewise, more than a century
later, buttressing biblical faith with pagan learning would be Marsilio Ficino‚Äôs
procedure in his book On Christian Religion, a monument of Renaissance culture.‚Å¥
   If Holcot was a forerunner of Ficino‚Äôs pious syncretism, how effective was he?
Pico was in a good position to answer‚Äîhad he ever noticed Holcot‚Äôs classicizing
habits. He could have seen them at their peak in an early printing of the Wisdom
commentary, which he owned. And in 1487, during his ordeal in Rome, he had
access to Holcot‚Äôs theological writings, which he cited in the Apology.
  The other, exegetical book by Holcot from Pico‚Äôs library had words of wisdom
and hope to comfort him‚Äîespecially a promise that ‚ÄúGod‚Äôs mercy will come most
readily at a time when worldly tribulation hangs over us.‚Äù Holcot found such
reassurance not only in the Psalter and tales of Saint Martin, a Christian knight
and bishop, but also in records of Roman valor collected by Julius Frontinus, a
pagan general. Citing Frontinus, Holcot urged his brothers to stay cheerful and
emulate noble Romans, Scipio and Caesar, commanders who raised morale for
their troops by making light of their own mishaps.‚Åµ
   Affecting stories about the ancients‚Äîdimly, haltingly, awkwardly, inaccurately
told‚Äîhistoricized the lived experience of clergy whose lives in religious commu-
nities were like nothing at all on pages written by Frontinus. Petrarch, Holcot‚Äôs
contemporary, told stories about the same heathen heroes, also for moral pur-
poses, but with genius and in lively, readable prose. There can be no comparison.

But there was a convergence of material and intent‚Äînotably the intent to link
human lives vicariously with a lost alien past of great moral value for the present.
  Holcot was Ô¨Årst and foremost a theologian, however‚Äîhence a philosopher who
practiced a craft of abstraction which, willy-nilly, distanced him from other
people, living and dead. The patron of his calling was Socrates, a pagan saint,
who frequently appeared as Sortes in medieval books about philosophy. The
martyred Athenian was so often the subject of example sentences, like ‚ÄúSocrates
is mortal,‚Äù that Socrates, the name of the human person, withered away, and the
husk left behind was Sortes: as empty a name as can be. In his technical career,
nonetheless, Sortes went on and on: Peter of Spain‚Äôs logic book mentioned him
more than two hundred times. Meanwhile few noticed that Socrates had vanished:
the medieval practice of philosophy was relentlessly non-historical. To say ‚Äúdehis-
toricized,‚Äù as if there had been a purge, would be wrong, because the European
past had never yet been historicized. This was an achievement to which Holcot
contributed in a small way.‚Å∂
  One student of a student of Sortes kept his full Latin name, Aristoteles, although
books sometimes did not name him at all: often he was just the Philosopher, and
Averroes, the greatest expert on his philosophy, was just the Commentator.
Personal, historical identity had nothing to do with these august labels. Medieval
philosophers seldom distinguished the Philosophus or the Commentator from their
theories about ethics, logic, metaphysics, natural philosophy, and psychology:
the wise were assimilated by their wisdom. But Aristotle himself had written
about earlier philosophers, and he had a student‚ÄîAlexander of Macedon‚Äîwhose
epochal deeds Ô¨Ålled volumes of historical narrative. Legends in Latin about
Alexander the Great also described his teacher and furnished anecdotes about the
Philosopher. One tale was preserved in a Treatise on the Apple (de pomo) and Death
of the Eminent Aristotle, Prince of Philosophers.‚Å∑
   After Holcot took lessons of hope from ancient Roman history, he went on to
praise Aristotle as a ‚Äújust person hopeful in his own dying‚Äù‚Äîwords from the Book
of Proverbs: like the Book of Wisdom, Proverbs gave prudent counsel in wise
sayings. Although Holcot had worried about the authorship of Wisdom, he asked
no questions about the authenticity of De pomo, an Arabic text that came into
Latin via Hebrew in the middle of the thirteenth century.‚Å∏
  De pomo moved Holcot to reÔ¨Çect on his own Christian death, by way of a pagan
philosopher‚Äôs death, in order to edify his brethren. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not for a philosopher to
fear death,‚Äù he assured them, ‚Äúbut to rejoice more and be of good hope when death
is imminent. When Aristotle was dying he explained this to his students with two

arguments, . . . his face shining with joy as he was about to die.‚Äù The dying
Aristotle, like Socrates in the Phaedo, reasoned with his grieving followers. But
he also brought his reasoning to life with a parable about mortality, immortality
and the apple in his hand.


  Do you suppose in your hearts that I would be happier by hoping to evade my
  own endlessness (inÔ¨Ånitas)? Well I know that I am not about to die‚Äîwere I not
   holding this apple in my hand, with its smell to comfort me and prolong this life
   for a bit, now that I‚Äôve already breathed my last. . . . As the apple Ô¨Ånally slipped
  from his hand, he died, and the philosophers prayed for him: may He who takes
  up the souls of philosophers take your soul up. Isaiah 40: whoever hopes in the
  Lord will exchange courage for death‚Äîby crossing over to life, that is to say.

The philosophers who prayed in De pomo were Aristotle‚Äôs pagan students,
transported to Holcot‚Äôs Christian world along with Isaiah‚Äôs good news from
Zion, where ‚Äúthe grass withereth, the Ô¨Çower fadeth, but the word of our God
shall stand forever,‚Äù as the prophet said‚Äîand Aristotle endorsed this message
with an apple.‚Åπ
   Critical philology was not Holcot‚Äôs strength, obviously, despite his qualms
about Solomon‚Äôs Wisdom. But empathy with the ancient dead caused him to
anticipate‚Äîthough with small effect‚Äîcompassionate responses to antiquity by
the more  skilled  Latinists and eloquent  Hellenists who admired Pico and
befriended him in the next century. The prince‚Äôs friendships with Poliziano and
other learned Italians were rooted in shared experience‚Äîincluding the experience,
still rare in their day, of critical philology framed by historical perspective. If Pico
knew the story of  Aristotle‚Äôs apple or  if he read Holcot‚Äôs  retelling of the
Philosopher‚Äôs death, what did he think? Did he see De pomo as a cheap exploit-
ation, in debased Latin, of the glorious Greek cadences at the end of the Phaedo?
Or did he, an ambitious philosopher himself, feel empathy for Holcot‚Äôs artless
empathizing with long-dead heathen sages? And does either response call for the
label ‚Äòhumanist‚Äô‚Äîthat inevitably honoriÔ¨Åc word?¬π‚Å∞


                    C.2 Pico No Humanist

238  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

corollary, Q8C. As far as these few paragraphs are concerned, we know for sure
that the prince thought of Holcot as he thought of Durand, Henry of Ghent and
other distinguished theologians‚Äîas not guilty of heresy and, in that life-or-death
way, just like himself. To secure this Ô¨Ånding and dodge a guilty verdict, he
empathized with these revered masters of the schools and internalized their way
of thinking, speaking and writing‚Äîthe practice of lawyers, philosophers and
theologians in the tradition of scholasticism (Figure C.1). This tradition, not
anyone‚Äôs ‚Äòhumanism,‚Äô was Pico‚Äôs universe in the Apology.
  In the record of history, the Robert Holcot who died of the plague in 1349 was
two persons. Despite unconventional views about faith and will, one of these
Holcots worked within a well-established tradition, scholastic theology, whose
great days were over when the pandemic took him. The other Holcot took halting
steps toward innovation. His commentaries on the Bible faintly foreshadowed the
Christianized classicism that became a Renaissance only in Pico‚Äôs century. Pico
studied writings like Holcot‚Äôs carefully, but the only Holcot known to him‚Äîas far
as the evidence shows‚Äîwas the traditional scholastic, whom he treated no
differently than Aquinas or Ockham or Scotus. Had Pico‚Äôs perspective been
humanist in his published writings, might he have noticed differences between
Holcot and these masters of the schools‚Äîdistinctive achievements of the innovat-
ing, classicizing Holcot?¬π¬π

## Page 252

Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔù£Ôù¨ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆÔù≥  239

  We cannot know. And in any case, humanism was not Pico‚Äôs point of view or
persona in the Conclusions and Apology, which were products of late medieval
culture, and his stance in these books was in no way progressive. Nonetheless, the
recent record of history has recognized only one Pico, purportedly a progressive
hero. This one and only Pico, a humanist prince, has been antiquity‚Äôs courier to
modernity: he brought news not only of a deep past, the Greco-Roman culture of
Plato and Cicero, but also of a deeper past, exotic myths about Zalmosis and
Zoroaster, and he aimed to build a sanctiÔ¨Åed Christendom on the recovered ruins.
  However, this forward-looking Pico, a prophet of Enlightenment, did not write
the Apology. The grim scene in his book‚Äîa prince‚Äôs battleground for life and
honor‚Äîwas the proximate, medieval past of scholastic theologians who despised
human history as the annals of sin. Good Christians in Pico‚Äôs time did not expect
their grandchildren to live happier lives in a better world. They hoped to escape
the world, the Ô¨Çesh, and the Devil, praying that Christ would rescue them and
their descendants from sin when he parted sheep from goats on the last day.
  These were also Pico‚Äôs otherworldly hopes. In his environment of dismal
expectations for the here and now, personal mistakes recorded by the Apology
were the book‚Äôs least noteworthy content. It‚Äôs no surprise that a rich young man
from a powerful family behaved as foolishly as such people had often done and
still do. The Apology shows that its post-adolescent author was smart but not wise.
The book, full of fury at Pico‚Äôs judges, reÔ¨Çects face-to-face clashes with them,
which by the defendant‚Äôs account must have been even nastier than the written
documents. Again and again, he belittled and insulted the Pope‚Äôs agents, some-
times neglecting to explain his own thinking because he was too busy demeaning
theirs.
   Pico‚Äôs outbursts were excessive and injudicious but not unjustiÔ¨Åed. Whether his
trial was fair or unfair seems not to have interested his prosecutors. The prince
came to Rome to kiss the Pope‚Äôs feet, so he said, and a spectacular show of
submission might have placated the authorities. Maybe they expected appease-
ment and were caught short by Pico‚Äôs intransigence. He and they both knew many
ways to climb down gracefully from points of doctrine as Ô¨Åne as those contested
by the Conclusions. And, by prevailing standards, the prosecution‚Äôs case was far
from open and shut. Although the Conclusions questioned basic articles of faith,
the Apology proved that the doctrines in play‚Äîin Pico‚Äôs formulations and in
normal theological give-and-take‚Äîwere too Ô¨Çuid to support heresy charges.
Pico‚Äôs anger was justiÔ¨Åed, then, but he risked everything by acting on his rage.
  Making the Apology public was foolhardy deÔ¨Åance‚Äîrash behavior that time
might have tempered. But the prince was in a hurry: he scurried to complete his
book in twenty days. Before he Ô¨Ånished writing, he had decided to go public
quickly. But why set so short a deadline? After Q13, he brieÔ¨Çy reviewed the
proceedings and said that speed was more important than accuracy. What was
the urgency? What payoff did he expect for careening into danger?

## Page 253

240  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

  Maybe rumors about his Ô¨Åghts with the commission had been leaking from
their meetings. But counter-rumors could have been arranged, and the whole
disaster might have faded away. The prince already had one catastrophe to live
down‚Äîa botched elopement with a married woman that ended in violence: his
male friends were quick to forgive, and he published no memoir about his
escapade. Once the Apology was printed, however, the ancient maxim operated:
‚Äúbooks have fates all their own,‚Äù and the fate of Pico‚Äôs book was to incite yet more
papal wrath. If we admire the intellect that produced such a book within three
weeks, we should also pity the will that let it be produced in such haste.¬π¬≤
  As for the book itself, some features of its content, form and text may not be
obvious at Ô¨Årst glance.
  The Apology printed in 1487 is the only version with authority, and this version
is textually unreliable. Comparing this text with its scholastic sources conÔ¨Årms
what Pico‚Äôs nephew said: his uncle sometimes went so fast that he was ‚Äúbarely in
control of what he put on paper.‚Äù Gianfrancesco was describing unprinted
material, often illegible, that he edited after Giovanni died in 1494. But his uncle‚Äôs
habitual hastiness, aggravated by his trial in Rome, had also damaged the Apology,
already in print before Gianfrancesco‚Äôs editorial work.¬π¬≥
  The Apology relied from start to Ô¨Ånish on Jean Cabrol, Durand de Saint-
Pour√ßain, Henry  of Ghent,  Pierre de  la Palud, Jean Quidort, William  of
Ockham, Robert Holcot, and other scholastic theologians, sometimes naming
them, sometimes not. Where Pico quoted these authorities directly, the passages
in the Apology can be compared with the originals. If the original is coherent but
Pico‚Äôs version is incoherent, two conclusions are possible: either Pico‚Äîas he
prepared the book for printing‚Äîtook no notice because he didn‚Äôt care that his
words were nonsense; or else the nonsense was a mistake that he could have
noticed and would have Ô¨Åxed, either his personal error or someone else‚Äôs‚Äîa
copyist‚Äôs or, later, a printer‚Äôs. Not caring is implausible: the price of nonsense
was too high because Pico was writing philosophy to be scrutinized in a capital
case by judges who demanded linguistic precision. Undesired mistakes were surely
made, then, and the prince was authorially responsible for them.
  Accordingly, a thorough edition of the Apology would emend the 1487 text by
comparing what Pico wrote with its sources in passages that quote a source
directly. For the Questions studied by this book, such comparisons have shown
the 1487 text to be defective, sometimes unintelligible. In my opinion, the whole of
the 1487 Apology should be treated as no more reliable than these borrowed
passages‚Äîless reliable, in other words, than some other incunabula of the same
type, books by philosophers who were inclined to be careful with words. Because
Pico deliberately wrote ‚Äúbarbarian‚Äù Latin in the Apology, distinguishing one type

Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔù£Ôù¨ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆÔù≥  241

of Ô¨Çaw (authorial errors of diction, grammar, orthography and so on) from
another (textual corruption) is difÔ¨Åcult. A principle of charity, in light of the
prince‚Äôs enormous ambitions and remarkable  achievements, has been my

  The text as it stands‚Äîthe words on the pages that Pico published in 1487‚Äîwas
his responsibility. But the topics of this Apology had been set by the Pope‚Äôs
condemnation of propositions already published in the Conclusions. Pico publi-
cized all these troublesome theses at the end of 1486, and this decision was also his
responsibility. But he did not assemble an Apology from Q1, Q2, Q3 and ten other
Questions because he had any special interest in the doctrines challenged by them.
The eucharist, the incarnation and other articles of faith were no less important to
him than to other educated Christians‚Äîbut also no more important, as far as
we know.
   Pico‚Äôs detachment from the doctrinal content of his own theses is evident: at
Ô¨Årst glance, the Q4 and Q6 theses look incompatible; likewise the Q6 and Q9
theses. When he Ô¨Årst made these propositions public in the Conclusions, each was
its own puzzle, and he expected each solution to display his ingenuity. There were
900 such theses: he could not have guaranteed that all 900 were compatible, and he
had no reason to try. If his project had been a Summa, the genre would have
obliged him to Ô¨Åt everything into a systematic architecture. But the genre of the
Conclusions was quodlibetal, with no requirement for order or system between
one proposition and another.
  Thirteen disconnected theses from the Conclusions were the Apology‚Äôs starting
points, and the outcome was disorderly. When Pico ended one Question to begin
another, only the court‚Äôs indictment linked successive parts of his defense; he gave
each Question its own internal structure, but he left the sequence as a whole
unpatterned. He did not follow the order of condemned theses in the Conclusions
because there was no order to follow‚Äîat least none that he had made explicit. In
Q13 he mentioned a ‚Äúhidden linkage‚Äù whose secret may have been more numero-
logical than doctrinal. Yet he did not bother to number his theses in the 1486
Conclusions, which again is the only text with authority. Because time ran out, he
made the last six Questions in the Apology shorter than the Ô¨Årst seven. Otherwise,
the only structure in the book was decided by his judges: his thirteen Questions
deÔ¨Åed the court roughly in the order of its charges against him.¬π‚Åµ
  The form of the Apology was formless, the author did not choose its topics, the
theological doctrines defended by it were not his inventions and he had no special
stake in most of them. In Q4 on the incarnation, he gave more space to Henry of
Ghent than to himself. He took the theory of the eucharist defended in Q6 from
Jean Cabrol‚Äôs digest of Jean Quidort‚Äôs Determination. The novel ideas about faith

## Page 255

242  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

and will in Q8 were Robert Holcot‚Äôs, and Pico watered them down to accommo-
date tradition. As a Christian theologian, he had little new to say in the Apology,
and he often covered what he said with tactical maneuvering.
  The prince‚Äôs attitude was often combative, his procedure was disputational, and
the procedure itself was a scholastic creation. Within each Question the debate
was a contest between dogmatic necessity and hermeneutic possibility, which was
true of the scholastic tradition itself. Pico‚Äôs method in this combat was non-
partisan. He went for help to all the sects, labeled in the usual way: nominalists,
Scotists, Thomists and so on. And this non-sectarian borrowing complied with his
own ideology: concordia, a utopian project for harmonizing all the discordant
philosophies. Pico‚Äôs irenic ambition was truly original in its day, like nothing else
in the Apology, and never to be realized.
  The quarrelsome example of the Apology stopped no one from disputing. How
could it? Pico did not stop disputing. Five years later he was still battling with
Antonio Cittadini, a scholastic professor who picked a Ô¨Åght with him about his
unÔ¨Ånished  treatise On Being and the One. Gianfrancesco Pico, the prince‚Äôs
nephew and biographer, saw the gaps between his uncle‚Äôs ideals and his actions.
The nephew‚Äôs solution was to paper them over with hagiography in a pious Life of
his uncle, where he strained to present the slightly older Pico as a saintly
Thomist‚Äîin line with their shared admiration for Savonarola, the most conspicu-
ous Dominican in Italy. But the author of the Apology was no Thomist; leading
witnesses for his defense were theologians whose views were notoriously anti-
Thomist: Durand, Henry of Ghent, Holcot, Ockham, Quidort, and others.
  The Pico of the Apology, not a Thomist, was no longer the Pico of the
Conclusions. A few propositions in the earlier book challenged Aquinas directly,
but there was no comprehensive attack on the sainted Dominican. Quite the
contrary: in the group of six ‚ÄúLatin philosophers and theologians‚Äù who open
the Conclusions, Aquinas got far more theses‚Äîforty-Ô¨Åve out of 115‚Äîthan any
of the others; Scotus came next with twenty-two. And some of the theses linked
with Thomas used Thomas‚Äôs words to state familiar Thomist positions, just as
some theses given to Scotus were recognizably Scotist. Nonetheless, Pico‚Äôs purpose
in these propositions was not to endorse but to debate‚Äîmaybe to agree, maybe to
disagree, maybe to make an entirely different point.¬π‚Å∂
  Had Pico proposed to discuss only a few dozen propositions, maybe no one
would have noticed. The number 900 was provocative in itself; thirty years later,
ninety-Ô¨Åve theses would be enough for Luther. ‚ÄúAiming to debate so many
questions,‚Äù the prince complained, was ‚Äúexcessive, ambitious and beyond my
ability‚Äù‚Äîso his enemies charged. He surely lit even more fuses with an ostenta-
tious campaign of self-promotion, promising to post his theses ‚Äúin  all the




   ¬π‚Å∂ Pico, Con. p. 1 (Bnd. p. 6).

## Page 256

Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔù£Ôù¨ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆÔù≥  243

universities of Italy‚Äù and pledging ‚Äúto cover costs of travel . . . for any philosopher
or theologian . . . who wishes to come to Rome and debate.‚Äù Some observers of this
grandstanding were Thomists‚Äîwho were thick on the ground in Italy, especially
in Rome. And Pico‚Äôs Thomist opponents in Rome targeted theses that were anti-
Thomist, thus forcing their victim to make yet more anti-Thomist statements in
the Apology.¬π‚Å∑
  True to his ideology of concord, however, the prince was neither Thomist nor
anti-Thomist. Otherwise, he should have aligned himself frontally with anti-
Thomists in the Conclusions‚Äîthe ‚Äúterminists,‚Äù ‚Äúmoderns,‚Äù and ‚ÄúOckhamists‚Äù
opposed by Cabrol. In fact, Pico listed none of these innovators among the six
Latin theologians who opened his Conclusions. But this was not because their
dissenting views did not interest him. The Apology‚Äîassembled in twenty days‚Äî
proves that he knew their works quite well and used them expertly. And he needed
anti-Thomist weaponry to defend himself against Thomist attacks‚Äîwith Bishop
Garcia leading the charge.¬π‚Å∏
   Pico‚Äôs alliances with Holcot, Ockham, Quidort and others were expedient, not
principled. Indeed, at the end of Q8, he disclaimed Holcot‚Äôs views on faith and
will‚Äîeven after modeling Q8 on arguments from Holcot‚Äôs Sentences commentary.
In Q1 he distanced himself from Durand in the same way after quoting him
extensively and praising his theology. These equivocations, in Pico‚Äôs disputational
calculus, were not duplicitous. The possibility that eminent theologians were not
wrong to take unconventional positions underwrote the possibility that his own
views on the same points were procedurally correct and hence blameless. One
possibility was no more or less certain than another, and he saw no need to
endorse conjectures just because he exploited them.
  The record of Pico‚Äôs versatility‚Äîthe  list of his opportune alliances in the
Apology‚Äîestablishes his place in history. The extensive evidence of his book,
the longest that he made public in print during his lifetime, shows the prince not
behaving or writing like a humanist. There was no display of classical philology in
his Questions to emulate Barbaro or Poliziano or Valla. There was no sanitizing of
ancient pagan morality in the way of Bruni or Ficino or Manetti. The doxastic
ethics in the Apology‚ÄîPico‚Äôs claim that believing was not a free act of will‚Äîwas
his pragmatic response to trouble in a courtroom. If there was a will to believe,
Garcia and the other judges could have held him responsible for believing the
condemned theses, so he evaded responsibility by denying that he was free to
believe, or not believe, what his own propositions proposed.

244  Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù£ÔùØÔù§Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≤Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ÔùØÔù¨Ôù°ÔùØÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨

  The hymn to human freedom and dignity commonly attributed to Pico‚Äîa
misreading of his Oration‚Äîwas a song that he never sang in public; in fact, his
abandoned and unpublished speech was not about human dignity. Moreover, in
the Apology he argued with energy and skill that there was no human will to
believe, denying that people have this freedom that Aquinas had conÔ¨Årmed. His
motives for taking a less progressive stand than the saint‚Äôs were expedient‚Äîlike
other motives of his Apology‚Äîand his pragmatic views entered the public record.
  But the Apology‚Äîa difÔ¨Åcult, unappealing book‚Äîprobably had few readers after
Pico‚Äôs disaster in 1487. Gianfrancesco praised it in his Life of his uncle: the thirteen
conclusions had been misunderstood and wrongly condemned, he insisted, and
the proof was in the Apology. Thomas More read Gianfrancesco‚Äôs excuses when he
translated the Life into English. But what would More and his friend Erasmus have
thought of Q8 and its exclusion of freedom from the ethics of belief? The question
may seem easier than it is. Like Pico, neither More nor Erasmus was a secularist
progressive: like him, they were not ‚Äòhumanists‚Äô in this sense‚Äîa word commonly
misused in modern times about them and others.
  The Apology‚Äîto be read or not read‚Äîwas printed because Pico decided to go
public with his anger. His decision has preserved evidence that belies his reputa-
tion as a spokesman for progressive humanism. The same book records the
prince‚Äôs transactions of convenience with theologians whom Erasmus, More,
and their followers despised even more than other scholastics. The metaphysics
promoted by these theologians was nominalist, opposed to Thomist realism, and
their logic was formidably technical, fed by a post-Thomist theology of divine
omnipotence: contortions of logical possibility magniÔ¨Åed the reach of God‚Äôs
power. Ockham, according to Cabrol, was the father of  this philosophical
movement.
  In the Ô¨Ånale of Q1 on Hell‚Äîa very long Question‚ÄîPico called on Ockham to
claim a certain kind of freedom: the philosophical freedom to speak philosophy‚Äôs
special dialect. The work by Ockham that Pico cited was an anti-papal polemic
that had denounced Ockham‚Äôs prosecutor, the Pope in Avignon, as a heretic
(Figure C.2). Pico‚Äôs message to the reigning Pope in Rome could not have been
plainer: your agents have attacked me, but  I‚Äôll defend myself with Ockham‚Äôs
weapons‚Äîwhich at the time were still fearful.
  In the early 1470s, about ten years before the prince went to Paris, the Most
Christian King of France and his advisers feared Ockham‚Äôs nominalism so much
that they banned it from their ancient university. They ordered faculty in Paris not
to teach this doctrine, and they directed booksellers and librarians to keep writings
about it chained up and unread. The professors objected by explaining who these
dreaded nominales really were, Ô¨Årst by saying what they taught, then by naming
their leaders from the past.
  They described the Ô¨Årst distinctive feature of nominalism as a metaphysical
doctrine‚Äîopposed  to ‚Äúmultiplying things as terms are  multiplied.‚Äù In the

of freedom was far from robust and not modern at all. But his will to defend it‚Äî
especially in his own interest‚Äîwas Ô¨Åerce, bold and sometimes too bold: reckless,
in fact. The panicky Apology that he wrote in such haste records a harsh test of his
mind and character.
  He died in 1494 at the age of thirty-one. And only a few years passed before
someone carved words in a wall of San Marco to proclaim that his fame had
girdled the globe. Today this church still keeps his bones in Florence, but ‚Äúthe rest
of him the Tagus knows, the Ganges too.‚Äù The boast was hollow at the time,
however: no one near the Ganges had yet heard of the prince, and not many in
Portugal. Only later, after decades and centuries passed, would Pico‚Äôs many ghosts
earn the epitaph that a modern poet could have supplied, had she wished, in lines
about a sacred Ô¨Åre:


         My candle burns at both ends;
                  It will not last the night;
            But ah, my foes, and oh, my friends‚Äî
                  It gives a lovely light!¬≤¬π
